White, Audrey Patocs, Marla Beauchamp, and Parminder Raina \nIndex ................................................................................................................. 213 \nix \nForeword \nJohn Gero \nUniversity of North Carolina at Charlotte \nDesigning is a foundational activity of humans. It is the way humans intention -\nally change the world around them with the goal of making it better for their \npurposes. Until the 20th century, designing was restricted to large-scale products such as machines, ships, civil structures, and buildings. However, the develop -\nment of consumer goods and then software applications has changed the land -\nscape of design and noticeably broadened the scope of design. Designing has moved beyond its historical roles to encompass the design of organizations, and social and governmental systems. The range of design has spawned the creative industries, a catch-all phrase that encompasses designing well beyond its tradi -\ntional areas. The creative industries contribute around $1 trillion a year to the global economy. Designing is one of the foundations of the creative industries. Designing has become a signifcant activity in both the national and global econ-omy. As a consequence, it plays an important role in economic and social wealth generation. What is surprising is that given the ubiquity of designs and its contin -\nuous history from the earliest days of civilization (the Epic of Gilgamesh , written \naround 4,000 years ago, mentions designing), how recently designing has become an area of research. Designing had been learned through the master-apprentice model for generations. Formal research into designing began in earnest only post World War II, in the 1950s, although there were earlier individual efforts. This research has as its goal to produce a body of knowledge that would lead to the understanding of designing as a set of activities that could be formalized, taught, and potentially improved. The teaching required that there be knowledge to teach. \nThe knowledge of designing could be obtained through induction from obser -\nvations of designers in action. Or this knowledge could be generated through executing experiments that elucidated different aspects of designing. These two streams of knowledge generation intertwine with each feeding off the other. Experiments produced models of designing based on either behaviors of design -\ners or concepts from cognitive science, i.e., how the mind behaves. Both of them have been labeled “design thinking” and this has caused confusion as to which is being referred to. So, design thinking can be what designers do or it can be what happens in the minds of designers, which results in what designers do. The reader of this volume will fnd examples of both streams. \nWhat both these streams agree upon is that designing is a set of processes, \nwhere the processes can be individually delineated. The confuence of these \n \n \n \n   \n x Foreword \nprocesses produces designing and it is this set of processes that distinguishes \ndesigning from other human activities. \nThis current design thinking model of designing, derived from design prac -\ntice, distinguishes itself from earlier models through the inclusion of the user in the process under the label of “empathy”. This notion of empathy, imagining the world from multiple perspectives, was missing as a signifcant factor from earlier models of design thinking, such as Asimov’s 1962  model of engineering \ndesign  (Asimov, 1962 ) and in Pahl and Beitz’s 1984 model of engineering design \n(Pahl & Beitz, 1984). The highly infuential book by Herb Simon, The Sciences \nof the Artifcial ( Simon, 1969 ) also does not address this notion of empathy in its \ndescription of designing. It was an article in the Harvard Business Review  by Tim \nBrown, CEO of IDEO ( Brown, 2008 ), that initiated the widespread interest in \ndesign thinking. The article was expanded into a highly infuential book ( Brown, \n2009 ). Brown distilled what he had learned from his own design practice to pro -\nduce a near-prescriptive model of design thinking and framed it within a business perspective that elevated designing from an activity associated with producing products to one generating value for an organization. \nThe other stream, exemplifed by Lawson ( Lawson, 2005 ) and Cross ( Cross \n2023), focuses on the cognition of designing within a process model. The two \nstreams complement each other. \nThis volume brings together 12 contributions on design thinking that span \nboth streams and contribute to the literature in unique ways. In the introductory chapter, Becker outlines the foundational activities that go to make up design thinking. In Chapter 2 , Cabrera focuses on the way design thinking contributes to \ninnovation within the social context. In Chapter 3 , Huck and Sukhov dive more \ndeeply into the role of ideas and their sharing in design development. In Chapter \n4, Mesa and Ruiz-Paster, in contrast to Chapter 3 , expand the scope of designing \nto cover the circular economy within a design thinking framework. In Chapter 5 , \nMorcos brings the concept of the empathy aspect of design thinking to the start -\nup culture. In Chapter 6 , Mortati, Mariani and Rizzo bring design thinking to \nbear when developing public services, exemplifed by projects in the European Union. In Chapter 7 , Kelly and Grace pursue design thinking from a more \ncognitive aspect when considering a framework for the role of human design -\ners in an AI-human hybrid intelligence. In Chapter 8 , Genert, Stenftenagel and \nBlandon take a more traditional systems-subsystems view of design thinking as they develop a Multidisciplinary Design and Optimization model of their design -\ning activity. This model generally excludes direct human aspects. In Chapter 9 , \nKannengiesser and Gero look at the empirical data that supports the human cog -\nnitive behavior of the dual process modes of thinking when designing. In Chapter \n10, Hahri, Lubis and Pei look at Co-Design from a design thinking perspective. \nIn Chapter 11 , Palmås pursues an example of one of the processes that makes up \ndesign thinking, namely, the creation of ideas using metaphors and its place in design education. In Chapter 12 , White, Patocs, Beauchamp and Raina, fttingly \nas the fnal chapter of this volume, present results from a large scale seven-year study of using design thinking. \n \n \n \n \n xi Foreword \nThis volume brings material not normally found together in a single place. It \nbrings design thinking from a cognitive perspective along with design thinking \nfrom the empathy perspective. This shows both the breadth and depth of the two streams of design thinking and how they complement each other. Recent research on the brains of designers as they design has shown that both streams provide frameworks for what happens in the brain ( Gero & Milovanovic, 2023 ). \nREFERENCES \nAsimov, M.  (1962). Introduction to Design . Prentice-Hall. \nBrown, T.  (2008). Design Thinking . Harvard Business Review. \nBrown, T.  (2009). Change by Design: How Design Thinking Transforms Organizations \nand Inspires Innovation . HarperBusiness. \nCross, N.  (2023). Design Thinking: Understanding How Designers Think and Work , 2nd \nEdn. Bloomsbury VA. \nGero, J. S. & Milovanovic, J.  (2023). Design thinking and design neurocognition, in K. \nStraker & C. Wrigley (eds), Research Handbook on Design Thinking . Edward Elgar, \npp. 7–24. https://doi.org/10.4337/9781802203134 \nLawson, B. (2005). How Designers Think , 4th Edn. Routledge. \nPahl, G. & Beitz, W. (1984). Engineering Design: A Systematic Approach . Springer. \nSimon, H. A. (1969). Sciences of the Artifcial . MIT Press. \n\nxiii \n Preface \nDesign Thinking: Theory and Practice is more than a guide. It is a call to action \nthat invites designers, engineers, policy-makers, educators, and innovators to rethink their approaches and embrace a mindset that prioritizes adaptability, empathy, and collaboration. To be successful in today’s highly technological and globally competitive world, one requires the use of a different set of skills. This book seeks to empower readers to leverage design thinking not only as a tool for innovation but also as a means to create a better and more sustainable world. Through case studies, theoretical frameworks, and empirical research, it chal -\nlenges readers to consider whether and how design thinking can sustain long-term relevance in evolving contexts. As the chapters unfold, readers are invited to jour -\nney through the diverse dimensions of design thinking. From understanding its foundational elements to witnessing its integration with modern disciplines such as using artifcial intelligence, product design, and circular economy principles, the narrative of this book highlights the versatility of this approach. The book offers insights into how design thinking can drive change across industries and domains. In essence, this book presents a comprehensive exploration of design thinking principles, methodologies, and applications, extending from the design of everyday products to strategies for addressing global challenges. \n\nxv \nAbout the Editors \nEujin Pei began his career as a product designer, with his work exhibited at the \nCooper Hewitt Smithsonian Design Museum in New York. He now works as a Full Professor and serves as Associate Dean for the College of Engineering, Design and Physical Sciences at Brunel University of London, UK. Since 2015, Eujin has been the Director of the BSc Product Design Engineering course, where he leads its development and ensures graduates are equipped with the skills to excel in a rapidly evolving industry. Before this, he served as Director for Postgraduate Research, where he played a key role in maintaining academic standards and promoting excellence among PhD students. Eujin is a Fellow of the Institution of Engineering Designers (FIED), and his professional qualifcations include being a Chartered Engineer (CEng), Chartered Environmentalist (CEnv), and Chartered Technological Product Designer (CTPD). His work refects a strong commitment to innovation, sustainability, and the advancement of technology in design and engineering. \nKurt Becker  spent his career as an engineering and technology educator and \nhas extensive international experience working on engineering education and \ntechnical training projects funded by the Asian Development Bank, World Bank, and U.S. Agency for International Development (USAID). Countries where he has worked include Armenia, Bangladesh, Bulgaria, China, Egypt, Indonesia, Macedonia, Poland, Romania, and Thailand. Kurt is Professor Emeritus in the College of Engineering at Utah State University (USU) where he spent 30 years working in the discipline of Engineering Education. He was Department Head and Founder of the Department of Engineering Education at USU. His research in engineering education spans more than three decades, and his work preparing PhD students to be the next generation of leaders and researchers demonstrates his commitment to the discipline. While in the College of Engineering at USU he stated the National Academy of Engineering endorsed, “Grand Challenges Scholars Program”. This program provides undergraduate engineering students with curricular and co-curricular activities while being mentored by engineering faculty. Kurt’s work in engineering education and design thinking is a tribute to his dedication to the feld of engineering and technology. \n\nxvii \nList of Contributors \nMarla Beauchamp \nMcMaster University, Canada \nKurt Becker \nUtah State University, USA \nNick Blanton \nLockheed Martin Missiles and Fire \nControl, USA \nSixto Cabrera Hardware Engineer, USA \nJaime Albero Mesa Cogollo \nUniversidad del Norte, Colombia \nGlenn Gebert \nLockheed Martin Missiles and Fire \nControl, USA \nJohn S. White \nSouth East Technological University, \nIreland \n\nxix \nAcknowledgments \nThis book is the brainchild of a chance meeting between two engineering educa -\ntors from different continents who care deeply about engineering and how design \nthinking is critical to the engineering discipline. This book would not have been possible without the invaluable contributions of authors and co-authors for sharing their knowledge so generously, and whose expertise and dedication have enriched its pages. A special thanks to Shatakshi Singh and Katya Seward from Taylor & Francis for their unwavering guidance and support in bringing this project to fruition. Thanks to our friends and family members also who have supported us along the way. This book is a testament to the collaborative spirit that underpins the very essence of design thinking. \n\n1 \n   \n \n   Introduction to1 Design Thinking \nKurt Becker \nToday, organizations pursue innovation to be successful. Innovation can increase \nthe bottom line, improve quality, or make processes more effcient and produc -\ntive ( Magistretti et al., 2021 ). Apple Inc. is a good example, as it has had a core \nvalue of innovation dating back to its formation, and the result is an array of products setting it apart from its competitors. Innovative goods and services are now synonymous with superior quality. The more an individual or organization demonstrates a fuller understanding of innovation, the greater is the propensity to attain innovation ( Kahn, 2018 ). \nThe one constant thing in today’s work environment is change, and the rate \nof change is increasing. The global economy drives change and has become the norm today. Companies and institutions that fail to adapt to change will lose their competitive edge ( Igbal, 2011 ; Çetinkaya et al., 2019 ). An example includes \nResearch in Motion Limited, the original company of BlackBerry Limited that was once a leader in wireless data technology, but it failed to innovate and keep up with the change in the industry. They are now struggling to regain market share. This is just one of many examples of companies that have failed due to lack of innovation. While innovation is often thought to start the design process, the innovative solution is discovered towards the end of the design process. This is because using the design thinking process requires the user to think deeply about the design solution and that the context is well understood. \n1.1 DESIGN THINKING \nTo complement innovation, the concept of design thinking has gained momentum in recent years. In many organizations, it has become a crucial element in the stra-tegic planning and decision-making processes. Design thinking is a methodology for innovation focused on understanding and solving complex customer problems. It emphasizes the importance of empathy for the user, a hands-on approach to problem-solving, and the iteration of solutions that address specifc user needs (Nakata, 2020 ). According to Brown (2008) , design thinking has emerged as a \nhuman-centred approach to innovation based on the ways that designers think and work. By incorporating design thinking into their business strategies, companies can gain a competitive edge by creating products and services that truly resonate \nDOI: 10.1201/9781003487524-1 \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n  \n 2 Design Thinking \nwith their target audience ( Nakata & Hwang, 2020 ; Jaskyte & Liedtka, 2022 ). \nDesign thinking is a practical, creative problem-solving method that leads to solu -\ntions with the intent of an improved future result ( Foster, 2021 ). \nDesign thinking is a method often associated with creativity and developing \nnew ideas. It is used in problem-solving and development of solutions for complex \nissues. In early use, design thinking was used in the development of specifca -\ntions for a product or service to defne how the product or service would be used by the customer or client. Over time, it has evolved and become a new discipline (Brown, 2009 ). \nDesign thinking is a process that involves using many different skill sets and \napproaches, and is not just about creating new products or services. It is about defning new ideas, creating new ways of doing things, and implementing inno -\nvative solutions to complex problems ( Auernhammer and Roth, 2021 ). Design \nthinking is a multidisciplinary approach that draws on the skills and knowledge of experts from a range of felds. Working together in a collaborative way, these experts identify underlying patterns of problems and create new and innovative solutions to those problems ( Verganti et al., 2021 ). \nTim Brown, CEO of IDEO, defnes design thinking as a methodology to \nunderstand user demands and requirements, challenge the assumptions that defne issues that are not easily understood, and create innovative solutions by consider -\ning conditions and resources. According to Dell’Era et al. (2020) , design thinking \nis accepted as a formal creative problem-solving method with the intent to foster innovation. This description has been based on how practicing designers claim to approach problems, rather than on how design has been defned in academic literature ( Brown 2008 ; Pande & Bharathi, 2020 ; Cross, 2023 ). \nAt its core, design thinking is a problem-solving approach that is analytical \nand creative and combines logic, intuition, and systemic reasoning to explore the possibilities of what could be and create desired outcomes that beneft the end user (i.e. the customer). According to Kim & Park (2021) , a key aspect of design \nthinking is that it is human-centred; it cares about the people who will utilize what is created and seeks to understand their wants, needs, and experiences. This way, it is possible to understand the extent of the problem and create a deep understanding of the people the solution aims to help. Design thinking also allows for greater collaboration between teams increasing the chance of creat -\ning a successful product or service. This is because, from the outset, it creates a culture that welcomes the sharing of ideas, and it decreases risk as the proposed solutions are well thought out. Figure 1.1  shows designers using the design think -\ning process. \n1.2 ORIGINS AND KEY CONTRIBUTORS \nDesign thinking has evolved since the 1960s, drawing from diverse infuences and approaches, and has paralleled this conceptual development as a framework of methodologies supporting complex problem thinking ( Henriksen, et al., 2017 ). \nAccording to Jamal et al. (2021) , “design thinking is a human-centric, structured, \n \n   3 Introduction to Design Thinking \nFIGURE 1.1 Designers using design thinking. (Google image – purchased) – web site \nhttps://stock.adobe.com/plans . \ncollaborative problem-solving approach that produces innovative products, pro -\ncesses, or experience solutions to address wicked problems” (p. 3). \nThe design thinking process practiced today was created through the com -\nbined efforts of many prominent engineers, economists, and designers over the \nspan of decades. Designers such as Herbert Simon, L.J.K. Smith, and John Chris Jones are identifed as early design thinkers, having theorized about the pro -\ncesses by which designers go about their work ( Thawani, 2023 ). According to \nThawani, they did this intending to describe these processes in such a way that non-designers could understand and potentially apply them to their professional work contexts. Discussions about design thinking processes and practices were fomented by early adopters like Richard Buchanan, Nigel Cross, and Tom Inns. More modern discussions and applications of design thinking have increased exponentially in recent years, and more high-profle stakeholders in contempo -\nrary design thinking include Tim Brown of IDEO, Roger Martin of the Rotman School, and David Kelley of the Stanford d.School ( Muratovski, 2021 ). \n1.3 DESIGN THINKING PROCESS \nDesign thinking utilizes elements like empathy and experimentation to arrive at innovative solutions. This process often involves much collaborative work in which interdisciplinary teams work together to solve problems and arrive at solutions. The design thinking process is best thought of as a system of overlap -\nping spaces rather than a sequence of orderly steps. More specifcally, the design thinking process is a method of generating useful and creative ideas and is also outlined as a cognitive, strategic, and practical process by which design concepts are engineered. There are several variations of the design thinking process in use today, and they have three to seven phases. However, all variations of the design thinking process are similar and consist of three main phases: Defne, Design, and Test ( Brown, 2008 ; Knight et al., 2020 ). \n  \n \n \n \n \n \n  4 Design Thinking \nFIGURE 1.2 Double diamond design thinking model. (Google stock photo) \nA commonly used version of design thinking consists of six chronological \nsteps: empathize , defne , ideate , prototype , test, and implement . Designers look -\ning for solutions to a problem, using this process, frst understand it better (empa -\nthize) and then use the understanding to get a clearer problem statement (defne). \nThe outcome is a never-ending process of problem-solving through creating and evaluating. \nToday, many different models and schools of thought exist for design think -\ning. Whether it is d.school at Stanford University, IDEO U, MIT Sloan, or any of the many other schools, the design thinking process consists of multiple phases. These range from the four-phase double diamond model (see Figure 1.2 ) to the \nfve-phase design thinking model (see Figure 1.3 ), to the six-phase design think -\ning model (see Figure 1.4 ). \nAlthough there is no single model of the design thinking process, for this chap -\nter, it can be explained by a fve-phase model. The following section discusses each phase of the design thinking process. \n1.3.1 PHASE 1. E MPATHIZE : UNDERSTANDING  USER NEEDS \nThe frst phase of the design thinking process is to empathize. During this frst step, the designer should empathize with the person who will use the product. This involves observation, engaging with, and learning from the person in his or her environment. This phase starts with observing and interviewing the end user about the problem or product needs. This phase constructs a situation map of the user’s experience. After understanding the real problem or project needs, get an agree -\nment with the user to make sure this is a real problem. This enables the designer to identify the needs to solve the user’s problem ( de Kervenoael et al., 2020 ). \n \n 5 Introduction to Design Thinking \nFIGURE 1.3 Five-phase process of design thinking. \nFIGURE 1.4 Six-phase process of design thinking. (Google stock photo) \n  \n  \n    6 Design Thinking \n1.3.2 PHASE 2. D EFINE: FRAMING THE  PROBLEM \nThe problem with much of design today is that it is poorly understood, undefned, \nor confusing. A clear statement on what the problem is provides more informa -\ntion than numerous vague statements. The “defne” stage is crucial to the rest of the process. This is where you, as a designer, can understand and articulate the problem. This may involve several sessions, with the design team present, to brainstorm on what has been learned in the empathi')

Let\'s build something amazing\ntogether .\nBaldurs L.\nT ABLE OF  CONTENTS\n❧\nChapter T itle\nIntro Introduction\n1 T ype Annotations\n2 Functions and Parameters\n3 Classes and OOP\nChapter T itle\n4 Control Flow and Loops\n5 W orking with Lists and Dictionaries\n6 Modules and Imports\n7 Asynchronous Code\n8 T ype Safety in Practice\n9 W orking with JSON and APIs\nChapter T itle\n10 T ooling and Build Systems\n1 1 T esting\n12 From Script to App\nApp Python vs T ypeScript – Syntax cheat sheet\nApp T ypeScript glossary for Pythonistas\nApp Resources for further learning\nChapter T itle\nApp Setup guide for T ypeScript + React (Bonus)\nINTRODUCTION\n❧\nW e l c o m e  t o  t h e  T y p e S c r i p t  J o u r n e y :  A  P y t h o n\nD e v e l o p e r \' s  G a t e w a y\nAs a Python developer , you\'ve experienced the elegance of writing clean,\nreadable code with dynamic typing that feels almost conversational. Y ou\'ve\nenjoyed the freedom of rapid prototyping, the expressiveness of list\ncomprehensions, and the simplicity of Python\'s syntax that often reads like\nnatural language. Now , you\'re standing at the threshold of T ypeScript—a\nlanguage that promises to bridge the gap between the dynamic flexibility\nyou love and the static type safety that modern web development demands.\nThis journey isn\'t about abandoning the principles that drew you to Python;\nit\'s about discovering how T ypeScript embraces many of the same\nphilosophies while adding layers of reliability and tooling that can\ntransform your development experience. T ypeScript represents Microsoft\'s\nambitious attempt to bring structure and predictability to JavaScript\'s wild\nwest, much like how Python brought clarity and simplicity to programming\nwhen it emer ged in the early 1990s.\nT h e  P h i l o s o p h i c a l  B r i d g e  B e t w e e n  P y t h o n  a n d\nT y p e S c r i p t\nWhen Guido van Rossum created Python, he emphasized readability and\nsimplicity with the famous principle that "there should be one obvious way\nto do it." T ypeScript, while operating in a dif ferent ecosystem, shares a\nsurprising number of philosophical similarities with Python that make it an\nnatural next step for Python developers.\nBoth languages prioritize developer experience and productivity . Python\nachieves this through its clean syntax and "batteries included" philosophy ,\nwhile T ypeScript accomplishes similar goals through intelligent type\ninference, comprehensive tooling, and gradual adoption strategies. When\nyou write Python, you often find yourself thinking about the shape and\nstructure of your data—what properties an object should have, what types\nof values a function should accept. T ypeScript makes these implicit\nthoughts explicit, providing a safety net that catches errors before they\nreach production.\nConsider how you might define a simple data structure in Python:\nclass User: \n    def __init__ (self, name: str, email: str, age: int): \n        self.name = name \n        self.email = email \n        self.age = age \n         \n    def get_display_name (self) -> str: \n        return f"{self.name}  ({self.email} )"\nEven in Python, you\'re likely using type hints—annotations that help both\nyou and your tools understand the intended structure of your code.\nT ypeScript takes this concept and makes it central to the language\'s design:\ninterface  User { \n    name : string; \n    email : string; \n    age: number; \n} \n \nclass UserProfile  implements  User { \n    constructor ( \n        public name: string, \n        public email: string, \n        public age: number \n    ) {}  \n     \n    getDisplayName() : string { \n        return `${this.name} (${this.email})`; \n    } \n}\nNotice how T ypeScript\'s approach feels familiar yet distinct. The interface\ndefinition provides a contract that ensures consistency , while the class\nimplementation leverages T ypeScript\'s parameter properties feature to\nreduce boilerplate—a concept that resonates with Python\'s emphasis on\nconciseness.\nU n d e r s t a n d i n g  T y p e S c r i p t \' s  P l a c e  i n  t h e  M o d e r n\nD e v e l o p m e n t  E c o s y s t e m\nT ypeScript emer ged from a practical need in the JavaScript ecosystem. As\nweb applications grew more complex and JavaScript codebases expanded\nbeyond simple scripts to full-featured applications, developers faced\nchallenges that Python developers rarely encounter in their typical\nworkflows. JavaScript\'s dynamic nature, while flexible, made it dif ficult to\nmaintain lar ge codebases, refactor with confidence, or catch errors before\nruntime.\nT ypeScript addresses these challenges by adding a static type system on top\nof JavaScript, similar to how Python\'s type hints provide optional static\nanalysis without changing the runtime behavior . However , T ypeScript goes\nfurther—it\'s a compile-time tool that transforms T ypeScript code into\nJavaScript, ensuring that the resulting code runs in any JavaScript\nenvironment while providing development-time benefits that dramatically\nimprove the coding experience.\nThe relationship between T ypeScript and JavaScript mirrors, in some ways,\nthe relationship between Python and C. Just as Python provides a higher -\nlevel, more expressive way to write programs that ultimately execute as\noptimized bytecode, T ypeScript provides a more structured, type-safe way\nto write programs that ultimately execute as JavaScript. This compilation\nstep isn\'t a burden—it\'s an opportunity to catch errors, optimize code, and\nensure compatibility across dif ferent JavaScript environments.\nFor Python developers, this compilation model might initially feel foreign.\nPython\'s interpreted nature means you can often run code immediately , see\nresults, and iterate quickly . T ypeScript preserves this rapid development\ncycle through sophisticated tooling and incremental compilation, but adds a\nlayer of verification that catches many classes of errors before they can\ncause runtime failures.\nT h e  T y p e  S y s t e m :  F r o m  D u c k  T y p i n g  t o\nS t r u c t u r a l  T y p i n g\nOne of the most significant conceptual shifts when moving from Python to\nT ypeScript involves understanding how each language approaches typing.\nPython embraces "duck typing"—if something walks like a duck and\nquacks like a duck, it\'s a duck. This philosophy allows for incredible\nflexibility and enables patterns like polymorphism without explicit\ninheritance.\nT ypeScript employs "structural typing," which shares DNA  with duck\ntyping but operates at compile time rather than runtime. In T ypeScript, two\ntypes are compatible if they have the same structure, regardless of their\nnames or explicit relationships. This means you can often use objects\ninterchangeably if they have the required properties, similar to how Python\nobjects can be used interchangeably if they implement the expected\nmethods.\ninterface  Drawable  { \n    draw() : void; \n} \n \nclass Circle { \n    draw() : void { \n        console.log("Drawing a circle" ); \n    } \n} \n \nclass Square { \n    draw() : void { \n        console.log("Drawing a square" ); \n    } \n} \n \nfunction  renderShape( shape: Drawable ): void { \n    shape.draw(); \n} \n \n// Both work due to structural typing  \nrenderShape (new Circle()); \nrenderShape (new Square());\nThis structural approach feels natural to Python developers because it\npreserves the flexibility you\'re accustomed to while adding compile-time\nverification. Y ou don\'t need to explicitly declare that Circle  implements\nDrawable —T ypeScript infers this relationship based on the structure.\nT o o l i n g  a n d  D e v e l o p m e n t  E x p e r i e n c e :  T h e\nT y p e S c r i p t  A d v a n t a g e\nPython developers often praise the language for its excellent tooling\necosystem—from IDEs like PyCharm to linters like pylint, from testing\nframeworks like pytest to package managers like pip. T ypeScript brings this\nsame emphasis on tooling to the JavaScript ecosystem, but with some\nunique advantages that stem from its static type system.\nThe T ypeScript compiler serves as more than just a translation tool; it\'s an\nintelligent code analyzer that provides real-time feedback about your code\'s\ncorrectness. Modern editors like V isual Studio Code (which is itself written\nin T ypeScript) of fer features that feel almost magical: intelligent\nautocomplete that suggests not just method names but also provides\nparameter information, instant error highlighting that catches typos and type\nmismatches, and refactoring tools that can safely rename variables across\nyour entire codebase.\nThis tooling integration creates a development experience where the editor\nbecomes a collaborative partner in writing code. When you start typing a\nmethod call, the editor knows exactly what parameters the method expects,\nwhat types those parameters should be, and what the method will return.\nThis level of assistance reduces the mental overhead of remembering API\ndetails and helps you write correct code faster .\nThe debugging experience in T ypeScript also benefits from the type system.\nSource maps ensure that when you debug T ypeScript code in the browser ,\nyou see your original T ypeScript source rather than the compiled\nJavaScript. Error messages are often more informative because T ypeScript\ncan provide context about what types were expected versus what was\nactually provided.\nM i g r a t i o n  S t r a t e g i e s :  G r a d u a l  A d o p t i o n  a n d\nP r a c t i c a l  A p p r o a c h e s\nOne of T ypeScript\'s greatest strengths for Python developers is its\npragmatic approach to adoption. Unlike some language transitions that\nrequire rewriting entire codebases, T ypeScript allows for gradual migration\nthat respects existing JavaScript code and development workflows.\nThis gradual approach resonates with Python developers who might be\nfamiliar with migrating from Python 2 to Python 3, or adding type hints to\nexisting Python codebases. T ypeScript provides several strategies for\nincremental adoption:\nThe most straightforward approach involves renaming .js  files to .ts\nfiles and gradually adding type annotations. T ypeScript\'s type inference\nmeans that even without explicit annotations, you immediately gain some\nbenefits from static analysis. As you become more comfortable with\nT ypeScript\'s type system, you can add more specific type annotations to\ncatch additional categories of errors.\nAnother approach involves starting new features or modules in T ypeScript\nwhile leaving existing code unchanged. T ypeScript\'s interoperability with\nJavaScript means you can import JavaScript modules into T ypeScript files\nand vice versa, allowing teams to adopt T ypeScript at their own pace\nwithout disrupting existing workflows.\nFor lar ger codebases, T ypeScript provides configuration options that allow\nyou to gradually increase type strictness. Y ou might start with loose type\nchecking that accepts most JavaScript patterns, then progressively enable\nstricter rules as your codebase becomes more type-safe. This approach\nmirrors how Python developers might gradually add type hints to existing\ncodebases using tools like mypy .\nT h e  L e a r n i n g  C u r v e :  L e v e r a g i n g  Y o u r  P y t h o n\nK n o w l e d g e\nAs a Python developer approaching T ypeScript, you possess several\nadvantages that will accelerate your learning journey . Y our experience with\nobject-oriented programming, understanding of type systems (especially if\nyou\'ve used Python\'s type hints), and familiarity with modern development\npractices provide a solid foundation for mastering T ypeScript.\nThe conceptual similarities between Python and T ypeScript extend beyond\nsurface-level syntax. Both languages emphasize code readability , both\nsupport multiple programming paradigms, and both prioritize developer\nproductivity . Y our experience with Python\'s data structures—lists,\ndictionaries, sets—translates well to T ypeScript\'s arrays, objects, and more\nsophisticated generic types.\nHowever , there are important dif ferences to acknowledge. T ypeScript\noperates in the JavaScript ecosystem, which means understanding concepts\nlike prototypal inheritance, event loops, and asynchronous programming\nbecomes important. The compilation step introduces new considerations\naround build tools, source maps, and deployment strategies that don\'t exist\nin Python\'s interpreted environment.\nThe module system in T ypeScript, while conceptually similar to Python\'s\nimport/export mechanisms, operates dif ferently due to the historical\nevolution of JavaScript module systems. Understanding these dif ferences\nwill help you navigate T ypeScript projects more ef fectively and make\narchitectural decisions that align with T ypeScript best practices.\nW h a t  L i e s  A h e a d :  Y o u r  T y p e S c r i p t  J o u r n e y\nThis book will guide you through T ypeScript from the perspective of a\nPython developer , highlighting similarities where they exist and explaining\ndif ferences in terms of concepts you already understand. W e\'ll explore\nT ypeScript\'s type system in detail, showing how it compares to Python\'s\napproach to typing. W e\'ll dive into object-oriented programming in\nT ypeScript, examining how classes, interfaces, and inheritance work\ndif ferently from their Python counterparts.\nY ou\'ll learn about T ypeScript\'s unique features—generics, decorators,\nadvanced types—and how they solve problems you might have encountered\nin Python development. W e\'ll explore the JavaScript ecosystem through a\nT ypeScript lens, showing how to work with popular libraries and\nframeworks while maintaining type safety .\nMost importantly , we\'ll focus on practical applications. Y ou\'ll see how to\nstructure T ypeScript projects, how to integrate T ypeScript into existing\ndevelopment workflows, and how to leverage T ypeScript\'s tooling to write\nbetter , more maintainable code. By the end of this journey , you\'ll not only\nunderstand T ypeScript\'s syntax and features but also appreciate how it can\nenhance your development practice and open new opportunities in web\ndevelopment.\nThe transition from Python to T ypeScript isn\'t about replacing one tool with\nanother—it\'s about expanding your toolkit and understanding how dif ferent\nlanguages solve similar problems in their respective ecosystems.\nT ypeScript\'s approach to static typing, its emphasis on developer\nexperience, and its pragmatic design philosophy make it a natural\ncomplement to the skills and mindset you\'ve developed as a Python\nprogrammer .\nAs we embark on this exploration together , remember that every expert was\nonce a beginner . The same curiosity and problem-solving skills that drew\nyou to Python will serve you well in mastering T ypeScript. The journey\nahead is not just about learning a new language—it\'s about discovering new\nways to think about code, new approaches to solving problems, and new\nopportunities to create robust, maintainable software.\nW elcome to T ypeScript. Y our Python experience has prepared you well for\nthis adventure, and the skills you\'ll develop will enhance your capabilities\nas a developer in ways that extend far beyond any single language or\ntechnology . Let\'s begin this journey together , building bridges between the\nworlds of Python and T ypeScript, and discovering how these two powerful\nlanguages can work together to make you a more ef fective and versatile\ndeveloper .\nCHAPTER 1: TYPE\nANNOT A TIONS\n❧\nB r i d g i n g  t h e  G a p  B e t w e e n  P y t h o n \' s  F l e x i b i l i t y\na n d  T y p e S c r i p t \' s  P r e c i s i o n\nAs a Python developer stepping into the world of T ypeScript, you\'re\nembarking on a fascinating journey that bridges two powerful paradigms of\nmodern programming. While Python has long embraced the philosophy of\n"duck typing"—where an object\'s suitability is determined by the presence\nof certain methods and properties rather than its actual type—T ypeScript\nintroduces a more structured approach that maintains JavaScript\'s flexibility\nwhile adding the safety net of static type checking.\nThe transition from Python\'s dynamic typing to T ypeScript\'s static typing\nsystem might initially feel like trading a comfortable pair of sneakers for\nformal dress shoes. However , as we\'ll discover throughout this chapter ,\nT ypeScript\'s type annotation system of fers a sophisticated balance that can\nactually enhance your development experience, providing the clarity and\nreliability that lar ge-scale applications demand while preserving the\nexpressive power you\'ve grown to love in Python.\nU n d e r s t a n d i n g  t h e  F o u n d a t i o n :  W h a t  A r e  T y p e\nA n n o t a t i o n s ?\nT ype annotations in T ypeScript serve as explicit declarations that tell both\nthe compiler and other developers what kind of data a variable, function\nparameter , or return value should contain. Unlike Python\'s type hints, which\nare primarily for documentation and tooling support, T ypeScript\'s type\nannotations are enforced at compile time, catching potential errors before\nyour code ever runs.\nConsider this fundamental dif ference: in Python, you might write:\ndef greet(name: str) -> str: \n    return f"Hello, {name}!"\nThe type hints here are suggestions—Python won\'t stop you from passing\nan integer or calling the function with the wrong type. T ypeScript, however ,\ntakes a more assertive stance:\nfunction  greet(name: string): string { \n    return `Hello, ${ name}!`; \n}\nIn T ypeScript, this declaration creates a contract. The compiler will verify\nthat name  is indeed a string and that the function returns a string. If you\nattempt to pass a number or return a boolean, T ypeScript will flag these as\nerrors during compilation, preventing runtime surprises.\nB a s i c  T y p e  A n n o t a t i o n s :  B u i l d i n g  Y o u r  T y p e S c r i p t\nV o c a b u l a r y\nT ypeScript\'s type system encompasses all of JavaScript\'s primitive types\nwhile extending them with additional precision and clarity . Let\'s explore\nhow these fundamental types compare to their Python counterparts and how\nthey form the building blocks of more complex type structures.\nP r i m i t i v e  T y p e s :  T h e  F o u n d a t i o n  S t o n e s\nThe primitive types in T ypeScript directly correspond to JavaScript\'s built-\nin types, but with explicit annotation capabilities that Python developers\nwill find both familiar and refreshingly precise.\n// Number type - encompasses both integers and floats  \nlet age: number = 25; \nlet price: number = 99.99; \nlet temperature : number = -15.5; \n \n// String type - for textual data  \nlet firstName : string = "Alice"; \nlet lastName : string = \'Johnson\' ; \nlet fullName : string = `${firstName } ${lastName }`; \n \n// Boolean type - true or false values  \nlet isActive : boolean = true; \nlet isComplete : boolean = false; \n \n// The special case of null and undefined  \nlet data: null = null; \nlet value: undefined  = undefined ;\nWhat makes T ypeScript particularly interesting for Python developers is\nhow it handles the concept of "nothing" or "empty" values. While Python\nhas None , T ypeScript distinguishes between null  (an intentional absence\nof value) and undefined  (a variable that hasn\'t been assigned a value).\nA r r a y s :  C o l l e c t i o n s  w i t h  T y p e  S a f e t y\nArrays in T ypeScript can be annotated in two distinct ways, each of fering\nthe same functionality but with dif ferent syntactic preferences:\n// Array syntax - similar to Python\'s List[type] annotation  \nlet numbers : number[] = [1, 2, 3, 4, 5]; \nlet names: string[] = ["Alice", "Bob", "Charlie" ]; \nlet flags: boolean[] = [true, false, true]; \n \n// Generic syntax - more explicit about the container type  \nlet scores: Array<number> = [95, 87, 92, 78]; \nlet cities: Array<string> = ["New York" , "London" , "Tokyo"];\nThe beauty of T ypeScript\'s array typing becomes apparent when you\nconsider operations on these collections. Unlike Python, where you might\naccidentally mix types in a list, T ypeScript ensures type consistency:\nlet temperatures : number[] = [20.5, 18.3, 22.1]; \n \n// This works perfectly  \ntemperatures .push(19.8); \n \n// This would cause a compile-time error  \n// temperatures.push("hot"); // Error: Argument of type  \n\'string\' is not assignable to parameter of type \'number\'  \nO b j e c t s :  S t r u c t u r e d  D a t a  w i t h  D e f i n e d  S h a p e s\nObject type annotations in T ypeScript provide a level of structure that\nPython developers might recognize from dataclasses or T ypedDict, but with\ncompile-time enforcement:\n// Inline object type annotation  \nlet person: { name: string; age: number; isEmployed : boolean \n} = { \n    name: "Sarah", \n    age: 28, \n    isEmployed: true \n}; \n \n// Multi-line format for better readability  \nlet product : { \n    id: number; \n    name : string; \n    price : number; \n    inStock : boolean; \n} = { \n    id: 1001, \n    name: "Wireless Headphones" , \n    price: 199.99, \n    inStock: true \n};\nThe structured nature of object types in T ypeScript means that you can\'t\naccidentally add properties that weren\'t declared, nor can you omit required\nproperties. This compile-time checking prevents many common runtime\nerrors that Python developers might encounter with dictionaries.\nF u n c t i o n  T y p e  A n n o t a t i o n s :  C o n t r a c t s  f o r\nB e h a v i o r\nFunctions in T ypeScript can be annotated with remarkable precision,\nspecifying not just the types of parameters and return values, but also\ncreating reusable function type definitions that can be applied across your\ncodebase.\nP a r a m e t e r  a n d  R e t u r n  T y p e  A n n o t a t i o n s\nThe syntax for function type annotations in T ypeScript will feel familiar to\nPython developers who have used type hints, but with some important\ndif ferences in behavior and enforcement:\n// Basic function with parameter and return type annotations  \nfunction  calculateArea( width: number, height: number): number \n{ \n    return width * height; \n} \n \n// Arrow function with type annotations  \nconst calculateVolume = (length: number, width: number, \nheight: number): number => { \n    return length * width * height; \n}; \n \n// Function with no return value (void type)  \nfunction  logMessage( message: string): void { \n    console.log(`Log: ${ message}`); \n} \n \n// Function with optional parameters  \nfunction  greetUser( name: string, title?: string): string { \n    if (title) { \n        return `Hello, ${ title} ${name}!`; \n    } \n    return `Hello, ${ name}!`; \n}\nThe optional parameter syntax using the question mark ( ? ) provides a\nclean way to handle functions with varying parameter counts, similar to\nPython\'s default parameter values but with explicit type safety .\nF u n c t i o n  T y p e  D e f i n i t i o n s\nT ypeScript allows you to define function types as reusable contracts, which\nis particularly powerful when working with higher -order functions or\ncallback patterns:\n// Define a function type  \ntype MathOperation  = (a: number, b: number) => number; \n \n// Use the function type  \nconst add: MathOperation  = (x, y) => x + y; \nconst multiply : MathOperation  = (x, y) => x * y; \nconst subtract : MathOperation  = (x, y) => x - y; \n \n// Function that accepts another function as parameter  \nfunction  applyOperation( a: number, b: number, operation : \nMathOperation ): number { \n    return operation (a, b); \n} \n \n// Usage examples  \nconst sum = applyOperation (10, 5, add);        // 15 \nconst product = applyOperation (10, 5, multiply ); // 50 \nThis approach to function typing creates a level of abstraction and\nreusability that Python developers might achieve with protocols or abstract\nbase classes, but with less boilerplate and more direct integration into the\ntype system.\nV a r i a b l e  T y p e  A n n o t a t i o n s :  E x p l i c i t  D e c l a r a t i o n s\na n d  T y p e  I n f e r e n c e\nOne of T ypeScript\'s most elegant features is its ability to balance explicit\ntype annotations with intelligent type inference. This means you can be as\nexplicit or as concise as the situation demands, allowing for both clarity and\nbrevity .\nE x p l i c i t  T y p e  A n n o t a t i o n s\nWhen you want to be completely clear about a variable\'s type, explicit\nannotations provide unambiguous documentation:\n// Explicit primitive type annotations  \nlet userName : string = "johndoe" ; \nlet userAge : number = 30; \nlet isLoggedIn : boolean = false; \n \n// Explicit array type annotations  \nlet favoriteColors : string[] = ["blue", "green", "red"]; \nlet luckyNumbers : number[] = [7, 13, 21]; \n \n// Explicit object type annotation  \nlet userProfile : { id: number; name: string; email: string } \n= { \n    id: 1, \n    name: "John Doe" , \n    email: "john@example.com"  \n};\nT y p e  I n f e r e n c e :  T y p e S c r i p t \' s  I n t e l l i g e n c e  a t  W o r k\nT ypeScript\'s type inference system is sophisticated enough to determine\ntypes from context, reducing the need for explicit annotations while\nmaintaining type safety:\n// TypeScript infers these types automatically  \nlet inferredString = "Hello')
```

Model Predictive Control\nDynamic Modeling and Econometrics \nin Economics and Finance \nV olume 31 \nSeries Editors \nStefan Mittnik, Department of Statistics, Ludwig Maximilian University of \nMunich, Munich, Germany \nWilli Semmler, New School for Social Research, Bielefeld University, Germany \nNew School for Social Research, New Y ork, USA\nIn recent years there has been a rapidly growing interest in the study of dynamic \nnonlinear phenomena in economic and ﬁnancial theory, while at the same time \neconometricians and statisticians have been developing methods for modeling such phenomena. Despite the common focus of theorists and econometricians, both \nlines of research have had their own publication outlets. The new book series is designed to further the understanding of dynamic phenomena in economics and ﬁnance by bridging the gap between dynamic theory and empirics and to provide \ncross-fertilization between the two strands. The series will place particular focus on monographs, surveys, edited volumes, conference proceedings and handbooks on:\n\x81Nonlinear dynamic phenomena in economics and ﬁnance, including equilibrium, disequilibrium, optimizing and adaptive evolutionary points of view; nonlinear \nand complex dynamics in microeconomics, ﬁnance, macroeconomics and applied ﬁelds of economics.\n\x81Econometric and statistical methods for analysis of nonlinear processes in \neconomics and ﬁnance, including computational methods, numerical tools and \nsoftware to study nonlinear dependence, asymmetries, persistence of ﬂuctuations, \nmultiple equilibria, chaotic and bifurcation phenomena.\n\x81Applications linking theory and empirical analysis in areas such as macrody-\nnamics, microdynamics, asset pricing, ﬁnancial analysis and portfolio analysis, \ninternational economics, resource dynamics and environment, industrial orga-\nnization and dynamics of technical change, labor economics, demographics, population dynamics, and game theory. \nThe target audience of this series includes researchers at universities and research and \npolicy institutions, students at graduate institutions, and practitioners in economics, \nﬁnance and international economics in private or government institutions. \nAll titles in this series are peer-reviewed. The series is indexed in Scopus.\nAris Daniilidis · Lars Grüne · Josef Haunschmied · \nGernot Tragler \nEditors \nModel Predictive Control \nEngineering Methods for Economists\nEditors \nAris Daniilidis \nInstitute of Statistics and Mathematical \nMethods in Economics TU Wien Vienna, Austria \nJosef Haunschmied \nInstitute of Statistics and Mathematical Methods in Economics TU Wien Vienna, Austria Lars Grüne \nDepartment of Mathematics University of Bayreuth Bayreuth, Germany \nGernot Tragler \nInstitute of Statistics and Mathematical Methods in Economics TU Wien Vienna, Austria \nISSN 1566-0419 ISSN 2363-8370 (electronic) \nDynamic Modeling and Econometrics in Economics and Finance \nISBN 978-3-031-85255-8 ISBN 978-3-031-85256-5 (eBook) \nhttps://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-031-85256-5 \n© The Editor(s) (if applicable) and The Author(s), under exclusive license to Springer Nature \nSwitzerland AG 2025 \nThis work is subject to copyright. All rights are solely and exclusively licensed by the Publisher, whether the whole or part of the material is concerned, speciﬁcally the rights of translation, reprinting, reuse of illustrations, recitation, broadcasting, reproduction on microﬁlms or in any other physical way, and \ntransmission or information storage and retrieval, electronic adaptation, computer software, or by similar or dissimilar methodology now known or hereafter developed. \nThe use of general descriptive names, registered names, trademarks, service marks, etc. in this publication does not imply, even in the absence of a speciﬁc statement, that such names are exempt from the relevant \nprotective laws and regulations and therefore free for general use. \nThe publisher, the authors and the editors are safe to assume that the advice and information in this book are believed to be true and accurate at the date of publication. Neither the publisher nor the authors or the editors give a warranty, expressed or implied, with respect to the material contained herein or for any \nerrors or omissions that may have been made. The publisher remains neutral with regard to jurisdictional claims in published maps and institutional afﬁliations. \nThis Springer imprint is published by the registered company Springer Nature Switzerland AG \nThe registered company address is: Gewerbestrasse 11, 6330 Cham, Switzerland \nIf disposing of this product, please recycle the paper.\nPreface \nThe book consists of independent chapters written by renowned experts in the ﬁeld \nof Model Predictive Control (MPC, in short). The topic of each contribution is the \ndescription of a current new development or a review of a subﬁeld in MPC, or a \ncombination of both with a particular care to make it accessible to a broad audience, \nincluding economics, ﬁnance, and environmental sciences-related audience. While \nrooted in engineering, the choice of topics, which feature uncertain and stochastic MPC variants, learning and neural network approaches, easy-to-use numerical imple-\nmentations as well as multi-agent systems and scheduling and coordination tasks, is particularly suited to illustrate the potential of using MPC in non-engineering sciences and applications. \nThe ﬁrst chapter (“Multi-horizon MPC and Its Application to the Integrated \nPower and Thermal Management of Electriﬁed V ehicles”) provides an overview of several recent applications of MPC to coordinated power and thermal management \nin connected and automated electriﬁed vehicles. In this chapter, the authors demon-strate how a multi-horizon formulation of MPC, which includes a shorter receding horizon phase and a longer shrinking horizon phase, can effectively address various \nchallenges in these applications. Additionally, it summarizes perspectives on creating forecasts and utilizing multi-horizon MPC for optimizing energy efﬁciency, based on several case studies. \nThe second chapter (“Data/Moment-Driven Approaches for Fast Predictive \nControl of Collective Dynamics”) reviews feedback control synthesis for large-scale particle systems within the framework of MPC. The high-dimensional nature of \ncollective dynamics poses challenges for traditional MPC algorithms, which rely on fast online dynamic optimization at each time step. To address these challenges, the \nauthors propose two alternatives to MPC. At the ﬁrst stage, they discuss the use of supervised learning techniques for the ofﬂine approximation of optimal feedback laws, while at the second stage, they review a method based on sequential lineariza-\ntion of the dynamics that utilizes macroscopic quantities of the particle ensemble. Both approaches eliminate the need of solving optimal control problems online, allowing for rapid, real-time feedback synthesis in large-scale particle systems.\nv\nvi Preface\nThe third chapter (“Finite-Dimensional Receding Horizon Control of Linear \nTime-V arying Parabolic PDEs: Stability Analysis and Model-Order Reduction”) \ndeals with the stabilization of a class of linear time-varying parabolic partial differ-ential equations employing receding horizon control (RHC, in short). This chapter \nconsiders the squared\n\x021-norm control cost, which allows stabilizing optimal control \nwith a low number of active actuators over time. The authors investigate the stabi-lizability of RHC and enhance the efﬁciency of numerical computations. Data-\ndriven model-order reduction (MOR) approaches are incorporated within the RHC framework. \nThe fourth chapter “Solving Hybrid Model Predictive Control Problems via a \nMixed-Integer Approach” presents a method to efﬁciently solve hybrid MPC prob-lems for a class of discretized hybrid control systems. The proposed method reformu-lates the optimal control problem associated with hybrid MPC into a mixed-integer \nquadratic problem (MIQP), which can be solved using established algorithms. The approach involves converting the discretized hybrid control system into a mixed logical dynamical (MLD) system. To arrive at such a system, an intermediate step \nconverting the discretized hybrid control system into a discrete-time control system with set-valued dynamics is formulated. \nThe ﬁfth chapter “nMPyC—A Python Package for Solving Optimal Control Prob-\nlems via Model Predictive Control” introduces nMPyC, a Python-based package designed to numerically solve optimal control problems without requiring in-depth \nknowledge of MPC theory. The package integrates well-known optimization inter-faces like CasADi and SciPy, offering an intuitive syntax for problem formulation. With this package, users only need to specify the key components of the control \nproblem—such as the cost function, system dynamics, and constraints—while the tool handles the optimization process automatically. \nThe chapter “Controllability of Continuous Networks and a Kernel-Based \nLearning Approximation” deals with residual deep neural networks, which are formulated as interacting particle systems leading to a description through neural differential equations, and, in the case of large input data, through mean-ﬁeld neural \nnetworks. The chapter presents theoretical results on the controllability of the linear microscopic and mean-ﬁeld dynamics through the Hilbert Uniqueness Method and proposes a computational approach based on kernel learning methods to solve \nnumerically, and efﬁciently, the training problem. \nThe chapter “Economic Model Predictive Control as a Solution to Markov Deci-\nsion Processes” studies Economic Model Predictive Control (EMPC, in short). \nEMPC has been proposed as a computationally tractable way of building optimal \npolicies for dynamic systems. When stochasticity is present, EMPC is close to the \nMDP framework. In this context, EMPC can be constructed as a tractable heuristic \nto provide approximate solutions to MDPs. However, there is arguably a knowledge gap in the literature regarding these approximate solutions and the conditions for \nan EMPC scheme to achieve closed-loop optimality. The main contribution of this chapter is to clarify this approximation in a clear manner, outline the conditions under which EMPC can deliver optimal policies, and explore some of the implications of \nthese ﬁndings.\nPreface vii\nThe ﬁnal chapter “Reinforcement Learning with Guarantees” presents a theo-\nretical foundation that allows to combine MPC and Reinforcement Learning (RL) \nwith the aim of introducing strict stability and safety guarantees in RL and obtaining optimality for the real system even though MPC is based on an approximate model. \nMarkov Decision Processes (MDPs, in short) formalize many relevant problems and have been addressed using various techniques, including RL and MPC. Each approach has its own advantages and disadvantages, but RL and MPC have both \nproven highly successful within their respective domains. RL makes it possible to obtain optimality for the real system, without an explicit need for a model. On the other hand, MPC does require a model, but provides strict stability and safety guar-\nantees and promotes explainability. This chapter provides an overview of results that support the theory behind combining RL and MPC, enabling to leverage the strengths of both approaches. \nWe are convinced that this book will be particularly interesting for applied mathe-\nmaticians and all researchers who are interested in up-to-date approaches in optimal and predictive control, be it from engineering or from other disciplines. The editors \nwish to express their gratitude to all authors (main contributors of this book) as well as to the anonymous referees for their constructive remarks that, in some occasions, \nled to signiﬁcant improvements in individual chapters. \nVienna, Austria \nApril 2025 Aris Daniilidis \nLars Grüne \nJosef Haunschmied \nGernot Tragler\nContents \n1 Multi-horizon MPC and Its Application to the Integrated \nPower and Thermal Management of Electriﬁed Vehicles ........... 1 \nQiuhao Hu, Mohammad Reza Amini, Ilya Kolmanovsky, \nand Jing Sun \n2 Data/Moment-Driven Approaches for Fast Predictive Control \nof Collective Dynamics .......................................... 29 \nGiacomo Albi, Sara Bicego, Michael Herty, Y uyang Huang, \nDante Kalise, and Chiara Segala \n3 Finite-Dimensional Receding Horizon Control of Linear \nTime-Varying Parabolic PDEs: Stability Analysis \nand Model-Order Reduction .................................... 55 \nBehzad Azmi, Jan Rohleff, and Stefan V olkwein \n4 Solving Hybrid Model Predictive Control Problems \nvia a Mixed-Integer Approach ................................... 83 \nIman Nodozi and Ricardo G. Anand Department of Engineering Cybernetics, Norwegian University of Science and Technology (NTNU), Trondheim, Norway \nBehzad Azmi Department of Mathematics and Statistics, University of Konstanz, \nKonstanz, Germany \nSara Bicego Department of Mathematics, Imperial College London, South Kensington Campus—SW72AZ, London, UK \nSébastien Gros Department of Engineering Cybernetics, Norwegian University \nof Science and Technology (NTNU), Trondheim, Norway \nMichael Herty IGPM, RWTH Aachen University, Aachen, Germany \nQiuhao Hu Department of Naval Architecture and Marine Engineering, University of Michigan, Ann Arbor, MI, USA \nYuyang Huang Department of Mathematics, Imperial College London, South \nKensington Campus—SW72AZ, London, UK \nDante Kalise Department of Mathematics, Imperial College London, South Kensington Campus—SW72AZ, London, UK \nIlya Kolmanovsky Department of Aerospace Engineering, University of Michigan, Ann Arbor, MI, USA \nLisa Krügel Chair of Applied Mathematics, Mathematical Institute, Universität \nBayreuth, Bayreuth, Germany \nIman Nodozi Department of Electrical and Computer Engineering, University of California, Santa Cruz, CA, USA\nxi\nxii Contributors\nDirk Reinhardt Department of Engineering Cybernetics, Norwegian University \nof Science and Technology (NTNU), Trondheim, Norway \nJan Rohleff Department of Mathematics and Statistics, University of Konstanz, \nKonstanz, Germany \nRicardo G. Sanfelice Department of Electrical and Computer Engineering, University of California, Santa Cruz, CA, USA \nShambhuraj Sawant Department of Engineering Cybernetics, Norwegian University of Science and Technology (NTNU), Trondheim, Norway \nJonas Schießl Chair of Applied Mathematics, Mathematical Institute, Universität Bayreuth, Bayreuth, Germany \nChiara Segala Faculty of Informatics, Universitá della Svizzera italiana—USI, Lugano, Switzerland \nJing Sun Department of Naval Architecture and Marine Engineering, University \nof Michigan, Ann Arbor, MI, USA \nGiuseppe Visconti Department of Mathematics “G. Castelnuovo”, Sapienza University of Rome, Roma, Italy \nStefan Volkwein Department of Mathematics and Statistics, University of Konstanz, Konstanz, Germany \nMario Zanon IMT School for Advanced Studies Lucca, Lucca LU, Italy\nChapter 1 \nMulti-horizon MPC and Its Application \nto the Integrated Power and Thermal \nManagement of Electriﬁed Vehicles \nQiuhao Hu , Mohammad Reza Amini , Ilya Kolmanovsky , \nand Jing Sun \nAbstract The chapter provides an overview of several recent applications of \nmodel predictive control (MPC) to coordinated power and thermal management in connected and automated electriﬁed vehicles. Such applications of MPC face signif-\nicant challenges due to the need to accommodate different timescales of faster power and slower thermal dynamics, interactive constraints, and the need to generate and \naccommodate long-term vehicle speed and traction power forecasts in the optimiza-tion. It will be shown that a multi-horizon formulation of MPC consisting of a shorter \nreceding horizon phase and a longer shrinking horizon phase can help address these \nchallenges. Perspectives on creating forecasts and exploiting multi-horizon MPC for \nenergy efﬁciency optimization based on several case studies are summarized. \nKeywords Model predictive control ·Multi-horizon model predictive control ·\nReal-time optimization ·Energy management ·Thermal management ·Integrated \npower and thermal management ·Hybrid electric vehicles ·Electric vehicles ·\nBattery charging ·Fast charging ·Connected vehicles ·Long term forecasting ·\nLong term preview ·Long prediction horizon ·Uncertainty mitigation ·\nComputational complexity reduction\nQ. Hu · M. R. Amini · J. Sun \nDepartment of Naval Architecture and Marine Engineering, University of Michigan, Ann Arbor, \nMI, USA \ne-mail: qhhu@umich.edu \nM. R. Amini e-mail: \nmamini@umich.edu \nJ. Sun \ne-mail: jingsun@umich.edu \nI. Kolmanovsky (B) \nDepartment of Aerospace Engineering, University of Michigan, Ann Arbor, MI, USA \ne-mail: ilya@umich.edu \n© The Author(s), under exclusive license to Springer Nature Switzerland AG 2025 \nA. Daniilidis et al. (eds.), Model Predictive Control, Dynamic Modeling and \nEconometrics in Economics and Finance 31, \nhttps://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-031-85256-5_1 1\n2 Q. Hu et al.\n1.1 Introduction \nOver the past few decades, Model Predictive Control (MPC) [ 22, 41] has emerged \nas a vibrant discipline for control of constrained linear and nonlinear systems \nwith numerous applications, including mobility and transportation. Despite many \nadvances, the application of MPC to control integrated systems, where heteroge-\nneous physical entities are combined to form a highly functional union, still remains \none of the important research frontiers. In such integrated systems MPC must exploit \ncomplementary characteristics of integrated components and account for the compo-\nnent constraints and their operating environment in achieving safe and optimal performance. Such desired performance can be achieved by leveraging the tight \nphysical coupling among the multiple components involved, calling for integrated optimization solutions over multiple domains and timescales. \nMany integrated systems have dynamics evolving over different timescales. \nExamples of such systems are abound in chemical process industry [ 47], control \nof electrical power systems and microgrids [ 14], in mining industry [ 11], in elec-\ntriﬁed ground vehicles [ 3, 4, 33], and in aircraft [ 16, 34]. When MPC is utilized \nfor control of such a multi-timescale system, the slower dynamics often dictate the controller design, as they need to be optimized over a relatively long prediction \nhorizon to achieve the best performance. At the same time, due to the presence of faster dynamics and the need to handle the transients, the sampling period has to be kept short. Consequently, the computational footprint of the resulting MPC solutions \ncan be very large. Furthermore, an accurate preview of operating conditions needs to be available over a long horizon, which represents another formidable challenge for implementing MPC. \nThe singular perturbation theory [ 35] can be utilized for controlling systems with \ndynamics evolving over multiple timescales. In such an approach, a system with explicit timescale separation in the dynamics is decomposed into two reduced-order \nsubsystems with different timescales, see [\n6, 12, 13, 17]. Then “fast” and “slow” \nMPCs are designed and applied to control fast and slow dynamics, respectively. \nHowever, a standard requirement in the singular perturbation theory is for the system \nto have an isolated equilibrium manifold for the fast dynamics, which may not always be the case. Moreover, the timescale decomposition strategy may not be applicable \nto systems with constraints which are dynamically changing at different (slow and fast) rates. \nAn alternative approach for dealing with multi-timescale systems is the hierar-\nchical MPC (H-MPC) [ 3, 4, 10, 18, 34, 49]. H-MPC computes the optimal reference \nvalues for the “slow” dynamics over a relatively long prediction horizon. Then, a tracking problem is solved over a much shorter prediction horizon with fast sampling \nto track the planned references and compute the control commands. Unlike the decen-tralized fast-slow MPC approach based on the singular perturbation theory, H-MPC typically requires and leverages the communication between the slow and fast MPCs \nto deal with the coupled constraints.\n1 Multi-horizon MPC and Its Application to the Integrated Power and Thermal ... 3\nIn [3, 4], an H-MPC solution was developed that exploited two receding horizon \nMPCs. In the upper (scheduling) layer, an economic MPC was used over a long \nprediction horizon with a slow sampling rate. In the lower (piloting) layer, a tracking MPC was used over a much shorter prediction horizon and with a fast update rate. The \nproposed H-MPC was applied to the integrated battery and cabin thermal manage-ment in electriﬁed connected and automated vehicles (CA Vs), demonstrating its effectiveness in handling coupled constraints. \nWhile the developed H-MPC framework in [ 3, 4] allows for optimizing multi-\ntimescale dynamics over different receding horizons, it may not be the best solution for “mission-centric” applications in which a system needs to accomplish a speciﬁc \nmission/task (e.g., reach a destination) while having access to a limited onboard energy source and minimizing energy use. Electriﬁed vehicles, aircraft, and ships are a few examples that fall into this category. For mission-centric applications, the \ncontroller is required to not only optimize the performance over a ﬁnite receding horizon but also accurately account for the “cost-to-go” beyond the receding horizon until the end of the mission in order to achieve the overall mission objectives and \noptimal performance. \nIn particular, terminal constraints, such as the battery charge sustainability condi-\ntions for hybrid electric vehicles at the end of the trip, often need to be enforced by the power and thermal management systems. In conventional economic MPC design with a receding horizon, this charge sustainability constraint is often enforced by adding a \nterminal penalty term to the stage cost of MPC to penalize the battery state-of-charge (SOC) deviation from its reference value—which is often set to be a constant value— \nby the end of the prediction horizon [\n8, 15]. Given the ﬁnite horizon of the MPC, such \na terminal penalty term in the stage cost limits the electric battery SOC operating \nrange and may force the Hybrid Electric V ehicle (HEV) powertrain to operate in a narrow and less efﬁcient region [\n25]. The effectiveness of this approach in enforcing \nthe charge sustainability highly depends on the weighting of the terminal cost, which has to be calibrated. To alleviate this issue, one can pre-compute the optimal SOC references ofﬂine, for example, using Dynamic Programming (DP) [\n50]), and then \ndesign a ﬁnite-horizon tracking MPC to follow the optimal SOC trajectory. Such an approach requires prior knowledge about the entire vehicle speed proﬁle, which is not always available and is subject to uncertainties. \nIn this chapter, a multi-horizon MPC (MH-MPC) control strategy is described \nwhich addresses the aforementioned challenges and facilitates the design of MPC for mission-centric operation and for systems that exhibit multi-timescale dynamics. \nThe approach considers the prediction horizon from the current time instant to the end of the mission duration and subdivides it into two phases: (i) a shorter receding \nhorizon phase and (ii) a longer shrinking horizon phase. The preview information over these two horizons can have different levels of accuracy, different ﬁdelity models may be employed, and sampling rates can also be different. Typically, a more accurate \npreview and faster sampling rate to capture fast dynamics are necessary over the short receding horizon, while a less accurate preview and slower sampling are used over the longer shrinking horizon phase. In effect, the optimization over the shrinking \nhorizon phase provides estimates of the cost-to-go, thereby informing the terminal\n4 Q. Hu et al.\ncost for the receding horizon phase to account for the overall mission cost. We note \nan earlier work related to MH-MPC strategy that replaces the end of the trip road \nsegments with a single virtual terminal segment in HEV energy management [ 28]. \nThere is also a connection of MH-MPC to MPC approaches which exploit adaptive \ntime meshing, see, e.g., [ 40]. \nIn the forthcoming sections of this chapter, we will describe two practical applica-\ntions that illustrate the use of the proposed MH-MPC. The ﬁrst application centers on the integrated power and thermal management in HEVs and follows [\n24]. Through \nthis application, we aim to elucidate the rationale behind adopting multiple prediction \nhorizons and highlight how estimating the “cost-to-go” over the shrinking horizon \nphase can enhance optimal performance. The second application pertains to the fast \ncharging of electric vehicles, and the treatment follows [ 26]. We will exemplify the \nutilization of MH-MPC with weight adaptation in this setting, even when the precise \nend of the charging duration is not predetermined. \n1.2 Integrated Power and Thermal Management (iPTM) \nSystem of Hybrid Electriﬁed Vehicles \nThe efﬁcient power and thermal management (PTM) of electriﬁed vehicles, including \nthat of the internal combustion engine, exhaust aftertreatment, battery, electric motors, and cabin temperature, can signiﬁcantly impact the overall fuel economy \nand driving range, especially in extreme cold and hot ambient conditions [\n32, 33]. \nThis is because (i) the actuators used for thermal management (e.g., compressor, pumps, and fans) can consume a considerable amount of power by more than \n2.5 kW [\n30], and (ii) the efﬁciencies of the vehicular power and thermal systems are \noften temperature-dependent and are degraded when the temperature falls outside the optimum range. For HEVs, with the engine and electric battery as the two main \nsources of energy, thermal management priority varies as the ambient temperature changes. Under cold ambient conditions, engine thermal management and cabin heating are the main thermal loads. In hot summer weather, on the other hand, engine \nthermal management, as well as cabin and battery cooling, dominate thermal loads. \nFigure 1.1 shows the power and thermal loops of a common power-split HEV . As \nshown in Fig. 1.1, a part of the generated heat during the combustion process (the \nrest of the fuel energy is converted to either mechanical work or wasted through the exhaust gases) is absorbed by the engine coolant. If there is any demand for cabin \nheating, the stored thermal energy in the coolant is then utilized to heat the cabin air at the heater cores. This means cabin heating reduces the coolant temperature, forcing the engine to idle to generate heat once the coolant temperature drops to below a \ncertain threshold (e.g., 40–50 \n◦C[21])—even if there is no demand for the drive. \nFigure 1.1 also shows the air conditioning (A/C) refrigerant loop, including the A/C \ncompressor, expansion valve, and evaporator. The electric-driven A/C compressor, \nradiator fan, coolant pump, valves, and A/C blower are the actuators of the engine and\n1 Multi-horizon MPC and Its Application to the Integrated Power and Thermal ... 5\nFig. 1.1 The schematic of a power-split HEV with power and thermal loops. Reprinted with \npermission from [ 24] \ncabin thermal management system, consuming battery power for their operation. The \noperation of these actuators is considered as the main auxiliary load on the battery in an HEV . \nThere have been numerous studies dedicated to the development of energy \nmanagement strategies (EMS) for HEVs using both (i) ofﬂine approaches, \ne.g., Dynamic Programming (DP) [ 9, 27, 36], Pontryagin’s Minimum Principle \n(PMP) [ 31, 43–45], and (ii) online approaches, e.g., equivalent consumption mini-\nmization strategy (ECMS) [ 19, 29, 46] and MPC [ 7, 8, 20]. These approaches have \nbeen extended to PTM with consideration of thermal dynamics and constraints. \nIn [21], multi-dimensional DP was adopted to study the impact of engine efﬁciency \ndegradation and cabin heating requirements in cold winter on the energy efﬁciency \nof an HEV . The results presented in [ 21] showed that such thermal loads signiﬁcantly \ninﬂuence the optimal energy ﬂow within the vehicle PTM system. Similarly, in [ 51], \nan integrated PTM (iPTM) strategy was developed based on DP to improve the fuel economy during the cold-start phase. Although DP provides a globally optimal \nsolution, it is infeasible for real-time implementation due to the high computational \ncomplexity of multi-dimensional optimization. \nThe emergence of connected and automated vehicles (CA Vs) technologies in \nrecent years has provided unique opportunities for situational awareness [ 48], \nallowing for vehicle speed forecasting. Exploiting these forecasts b')
