257\nHow to unlock these benefits in three easy steps  ������������������������������������������������������������  257\nOther Books You May Enjoy   263\nIndex   267\nPreface\nIn today’s extremely interconnected environment, REST APIs are the lingua franca for different \nsoftware to communicate with each other. The industry has tried different communication sys -\ntems in the past, such as SOAP, CORBA, and custom-made solutions, but REST has been the de \nfacto standard. Its simplicity and ability to leverage the underlying technologies, such as the HTTP \nprotocol, have positioned REST as the go-to option for most developers.\nREST is a solid option, but REST can be executed with many languages, such as Python, Ruby, \nPHP, Rust, and so on. But we are going to talk about how to do it with Go, but why Go? Go is a \nmodern, robust, fast, and easy-to-learn language, making it ideal for REST APIs. Its native com -\npilation makes it an amazing candidate for the speed requirement of modern APIs; its strong \ntyping makes it perfect for ensuring safety and robustness in your services, and its simplicity \nensures that incorporating new developers, even if they need to learn the language, is not going \nto become a nightmare.\nThis book covers REST and Go, but I’m a firm believer in understanding the foundations of things, \nso we will also discuss topics such as the HTTP protocol used under the hood, cache approaches, \nobservability, security, and much more. It provides you with the skills to develop REST APIs in Go \nand teaches you why we follow that approach and what the technological concepts are behind it.\nI want to focus on the concepts and keep things as simple and close to the Go standard library as \npossible, covering specialized libraries only when no built-in package provides that for us. How -\never, we will also provide examples for those who prefer different approaches to doing things, \nsuch as using query builders, ORMs, frameworks, or testing assertion libraries, giving you the \nfinal say on where you want to put the fine-grain control, and where you want to delegate to \nlibraries that make your life easier.\nPreface xvi\nWho this book is for\nThis book is for developers who want to gain a practical understanding of how to build REST APIs \nin general and, more specifically, using the Go programming language. While knowledge about \nGo is not strictly needed to get value from this book, some minimal understanding is required \nto get the most out of it.\nWhat this book covers\nChapter 1 , Introduction to APIs , goes through the fundamental concepts of REST APIs and explores \nother alternatives such as SOAP and GraphQL.\nChapter 2 , Exploring REST APIs , covers the tools needed to explore and analyze existing APIs, such \nas cURL and Postman. It enables you to explore and test your own APIs in the following chapters.\nChapter 3 , Building a REST Client , exposes the tools provided by Go’s standard library for accessing \nAPIs, and how you can build your own client library for consuming an existing API.\nChapter 4 , Designing Your REST API , introduces the main concepts of REST APIs such as routes, \nresources, and actions, and the semantics associated with them in the REST API ecosystem.\nChapter 5 , Authentication and Authorization , explores the needs and methods used for enforcing \nproper authentication in authorization in your API, exposing concepts such as JWT or the au -\nthentication HTTP header.\nChapter 6 , Data Persistency , provides the knowledge needed to create the persistency layer of your \napplication, including SQL database usage, the repository pattern, and building queries using \nthe Squirrel query builder.\nChapter 7 , API Security , goes through the different security features provided by the HTTP protocol \nand leveraged by the REST APIs. The chapter covers things such as data validation, sanitization, \nand password handling.\nChapter 8 , API Performance , focuses on making your API fast, including topics such as cache control \nfrom the HTTP protocol, the Go profiler, and building and understanding benchmarks in Go code.\nChapter 9 , Deploying Your API , examines the different options for deploying REST APIs, from de -\nploying your API in bare metal to a more sophisticated approach such as Kubernetes or function \nas a service.\nPreface xvii\nChapter 10 , Testing , exposes the different tools that Go provides for testing, and the different kinds \nof tests that you can build, from unit to integration tests, exploring all of them. We also explore \nthe testify assertion library as an alternative to the Go standard library testing tools.\nChapter 11 , Documenting with OpenAPI , shows the value of properly documenting your REST API \nwith the standard tooling out there provided by the OpenAPI initiative. It includes generating \ndocumentation and exposing that to tools such as Postman.\nChapter 12 , Metrics, Logs, and Traces , introduces the concepts of observability for your REST API \napplication. This chapter explores the data that we collect (metrics, logs, and traces). It also ex -\nplores the tools that help you understand the collected data, such as Jaeger and Grafana.\nChapter 13 , Using GORM , offers an alternative approach for the storage layer, building it with \nGORM instead of the standard library, or the Squirrel library.\nChapter 14 , Using the Echo Framework , explores other ways of generating your API that are not \nbased on the standard library; instead, it uses a third-party framework.\nTo get the most out of this book\nYou will need Go 1.23 or greater installed in your computer to follow the examples. Also, we are \ngoing to extensively use tools such as cURL to verify the correct behavior, and in some chapters, \nwe will explore approaches that require tools such as Docker, Grafana, or Node.js, among others.\nSoftware/hardware covered in the book Operating system requirements\nGo 1.23 or greater Windows, macOS, or Linux\ncURL\nPostman\nIf you are using the digital version of this book, we advise you to type the code yourself or \naccess the code from the book’s GitHub repository (a link is available in the next section)�  \nDoing so will help you avoid any potential errors related to the copying and pasting of code �\nDownload the example code files\nThe code bundle for the book is hosted on GitHub at https://github.com/PacktPublishing/\nModern-REST-API-Development-in-Go . We also have other code bundles from our rich catalog \nof books and videos available at https://github.com/PacktPublishing . Check them out!\nPreface xviii\nDownload the color images\nWe also provide a PDF file that has color images of the screenshots/diagrams used in this book. \nYou can download it here: https://packt.link/gbp/9781836205371\nConventions used\nThere are a number of text conventions used throughout this book.\nCodeInText : Indicates code words in text, database table names, folder names, filenames, file \nextensions, pathnames, dummy URLs, user input, and Twitter handles. For example: “With this \nsimple Create  call, we are inserting our User  model into the database. Not only that, but because \nwe add the gorm.Model  embedding, GORM, after creating the record, also automatically sets the \nID, CreatedAt , and UpdatedAt  fields.\nA block of code is set as follows:\nuser := User{\n  Username: "johndoe" ,\n  Password: "password123" ,\n  Role: "user",\n}\nresult := db.Create(&user)\nif result.Error != nil {\n  log.Fatal(result.Error)\n}\nWhen we wish to draw your attention to a particular part of a code block, the relevant lines or \nitems are set in bold:\nfunc (r *Repository) DeleteList(id uint) error {\n  result := r.db.Where( "id = ?" , id).Delete(&ShoppingList{})\n  if result.Error != nil {\n    return result.Error\n  }\n  return nil\n}\nPreface xix\nBold : Indicates a new term, an important word, or words that you see on the screen. They help us connect our systems, \nautomate processes in those systems, and adapt those systems to our needs instead of our needs \nto those systems. All that is done thanks to APIs.\nThis book will explore how to build APIs in Go from scratch. We’ll start with the basics of an API \nand how to explore it. Then, we’ll go through the incremental process of creating an API, from \nadding the basic endpoints to incorporating things such as security and telemetry. As an optional \nexercise, we’ll use a web framework instead of the standard library and an ORM instead of SQL \nqueries.\nIn this chapter, we’ll cover the following topics:\n• The different kinds of APIs\n• The basic concepts around REST APIs\n• How to build a very basic API in Go\nBut let’s start from the beginning. What exactly is an API? AnnotatedDocument(extractions=[], text='\nThe AI Engineer ’s Guide to\nSurviving the EU AI Act\nNavigating the EU Regulatory\nRequirements\nLarysa Visengeriyeva\n\nThe AI Engineer ’s Guide to Surviving the EU AI Act\nby Larysa Visengeriyeva\nCopyright © 2025 Larysa Visengeriyeva. All rights\nreserved.\nPublished by O’Reilly Media, Inc., 1005 Gravenstein\nHighway North, Sebastopol, CA 95472.\nO’Reilly books may be purchased for educational, business,\nor sales promotional use. Online editions are also available\nfor most titles (http://oreilly .com ). For more information,\ncontact our corporate/institutional sales department: 800-\n998-9938 or corporate@oreilly .com .\nAcquisitions Editor: Nicole Butterfield\nDevelopment Editor: Sara Hunter\nProduction Editor: Kristen Brown\nCopyeditor: Rachel Head\nProofreader: Kim Cofer\nIndexer: Krsta Technology Solutions\nCover Designer: Susan Brown\nCover Illustrator: Monica Kaamsvaag\nInterior Designer: David Futato\nInterior Illustrator: Kate Dullea\nJuly 2025: First Edition\nRevision History for the First Edition\n2025-06-27: First Release\nSee http://oreilly .com/catalog/errata.csp?\nisbn=9781098172497  for release details.\nThe O’Reilly logo is a registered trademark of O’Reilly\nMedia, Inc. The AI Engineer’s Guide to Surviving the EU AI\nAct, the cover image, and related trade dress are\ntrademarks of O’Reilly Media, Inc.\nThe views expressed in this work are those of the author\nand do not represent the publisher’s views. While the\npublisher and the author have used good faith efforts to\nensure that the information and instructions contained in\nthis work are accurate, the publisher and the author\ndisclaim all responsibility for errors or omissions, including\nwithout limitation responsibility for damages resulting from\nthe use of or reliance on this work. Use of the information\nand instructions contained in this work is at your own risk.\nIf any code samples or other technology this work contains\nor describes is subject to open source licenses or the\nintellectual property rights of others, it is your\nresponsibility to ensure that your use thereof complies with\nsuch licenses and/or rights.\n978-1-098-17249-7\n[LSI]\nPreface\nThe European Union’s Artificial Intelligence Act, which\ncame into full effect in August 2024, represents a\nwatershed moment in the global regulation of artificial\nintelligence. As the first comprehensive legal framework\nfor AI, its stated purpose is clear: to foster innovation and\ndevelopment within the EU while effectively mitigating the\npotential risks posed by AI systems. This ambitious\nregulation establishes a uniform legal framework governing\nthe development, placement on the market, putting into\nservice, and use of AI systems across the EU.\nNavigating the complexities of the EU AI Act might, at first\nglance, seem like a task exclusively for legal teams and\npolicy experts. The Act is indeed complex, featuring 113\narticles that address highly technical issues, complemented\nby 13 annexes that detail implementation specifics and 180\nrecitals (introductory statements providing context and\nguidance for interpretation). However, as the practical\nrequirements of the EU AI Act reveal, achieving and\nmaintaining compliance with the Act is fundamentally an\nengineering problem . This is the core message of this book.\nOperationalizing EU AI Act compliance goes far beyond\nlegal interpretation. It requires establishing roles,\nprocesses, structures, and AI engineering practices. Post-\nmarket compliance, for instance, directly necessitates the\nimplementation of machine learning operations (MLOps)\npractices such as monitoring and alerting. Successfully\nachieving EU AI Act compliance is linked to understanding\nthe design, development, and maintenance of AI systems.\nCompliance is not a legal stamp applied at the end of the\ndevelopment lifecycle, but a continuous process that must\nbe engineered into the core of AI systems from the\nbeginning. The Act’s foundation on the concept of\n“trustworthy AI” mandates that systems be lawful, ethical,\nand robust throughout their entire lifecycle. This\nnecessitates embedding ethical and compliance aspects\ndirectly into the AI system development process.\nThis book serves as your guide to tackling EU AI Act\ncompliance as the engineering challenge it is. We’ll explore\nvarious practical methodologies and frameworks essential\nfor this task, including:\nAI engineering\nDeﬁned as the application of software engineering\nprinciples to the end-to-end lifecycle of AI systems—\nincluding design, development, deployment, and\nmaintenance.\nCRISP -ML(Q)\nThis structured machine learning development process\nprovides a blueprint for designing, developing, and\nmaintaining AI systems with compliance in mind. Its\nemphasis on quality assurance and continuous risk\nmanagement throughout the AI lifecycle is directly aligned\nwith the EU AI Act’s risk-based approach. CRISP-ML(Q)\nrequires documentation of the entire development process,\nincluding risk management measures, which is crucial for\nmeeting the Act’s technical documentation and transparency\nobligations.\nMLOps Stack Canvas\nThe MLOps Stack Canvas is a comprehensive and practical\nframework designed to guide organizations in architecting\nand managing their machine learning operations\ninfrastructure. The canvas is structured around three core\ndomains: Data and Code Management, Model Management,\nand Metadata Management. It provides a holistic view of the\ncomponents necessary for successful ML deployment. By\naligning with the CRISP-ML(Q) process model, the canvas\nensures that each phase of the ML lifecycle is addressed,\nfrom data sourcing and versioning to model deployment and\nmonitoring. It emphasizes critical aspects such as\nreproducibility, reliability, and eﬃciency, helping teams to\nplan infrastructure costs, select appropriate tools, and\nestablish robust workﬂows. Serving as both a strategic and\nan operational tool, the MLOps Stack Canvas facilitates\nclearer communication among stakeholders for all ML and\nAI initiatives across the organization.\nSMACTR (Scoping, Mapping, Artifact Collection, T esting,\nand Reﬂection)\nIntroduced as an internal audit framework to guide the\npractical implementation of ethical AI development\nthroughout its lifecycle, SMACTR promotes a proactive and\npreventive approach for AI development. Embedding audit\nprocesses into the design and development phases allows\nengineers to anticipate and address potential risks before\ndeployment, aligning perfectly with the Act’s emphasis on\nrisk mitigation. SMACTR’s focus on generating detailed\ndocumentation at each stage is also essential for meeting the\nAct’s technical documentation requirements for high-risk AI\nsystems.\nThe synergy between CRISP-ML(Q) and AI engineering\noffers a powerful framework for addressing EU AI Act\ncompliance. Furthermore, the integration of SMACTR with\nthe CRISP-ML(Q) methodology provides a robust and\nauditable process for responsibly developing AI systems.\nThis combination allows for proactively engineering\ncompliance into the ML lifecycle, from data collection\nthrough monitoring, rather than treating it as an\nafterthought.\nThe book also explores how these engineering principles\napply across the Act’s risk classifications—prohibited, high\nrisk, limited risk, and low risk. While high-risk systems face\nthe most stringent requirements, Article 50 introduces\ntransparency obligations that apply to all AI systems\ndesigned to interact directly with humans, regardless of\ntheir risk level. These obligations, such as informing users\nof AI interaction and marking synthetic content, demand\npractical engineering solutions for proactive compliance.\nAligning AI engineering practices with SMACTR and\nCRISP-ML(Q) provides a structured and automated\napproach to managing the AI system lifecycle for\ntransparency.\nThis book also addresses the particular challenges posed by\ngeneral-purpose AI (GPAI) and generative AI (GenAI), late\nbut significant additions to the Act. The concept of\ngenerative AI operations (GenAIOps) is introduced as an\nextension of traditional MLOps principles to handle the\nunique complexities of GPAI and generative AI applications.\nApplying AI engineering principles to implement\ntransparency obligations for GPAI and integrating CRISP-\nML(Q), SMACTR, and GenAIOps are crucial for navigating\nthis evolving landscape.\nWho Should Read This Book\nThis book is intended for AI engineers, MLOps\npractitioners, data scientists, AI product managers, and\nanyone involved in the hands-on development and\ndeployment of AI systems. It demonstrates how, through\nthe application of robust methodologies, disciplined\ndocumentation, and continuous integration of ethical\nconsiderations, AI teams can build systems that are not\nonly technically innovative but also demonstrably\ncompliant, trustworthy, and aligned with societal\nexpectations. I have tried to make this book as actionable\nas possible by introducing a comprehensive framework and\npractical checklists for aligning AI engineering practices\nwith the EU AI Act articles throughout the CRISP-ML(Q)\nlifecycle.\nAs ImageNet creator Fei-Fei Li, known as the Godmother of\nAI, noted recently: “Now more than ever, AI needs a\ngovernance framework.” This book provides the practical\nengineering foundation for implementing such a\nframework, enabling practitioners to build trustworthy AI\nsystems that meet the stringent requirements of the EU AI\nAct through empirical validation, risk-aware development,\nand collaborative practices. The law sets the requirements,\nbut it is the engineering that delivers compliance.\nNavigating This Book\nThis book has been designed as a reference for you, a guide\nto practicing proactive EU AI Act compliance through AI\nengineering and integrating it into the AI lifecycle, from\ndata collection through monitoring. Each chapter is fairly\nself-contained, with appropriate references to other\nchapters identified. The chapters are organized as follows:\nChapter\xa01, “Understanding the AI Regulations”,\nprovides a foundational understanding of the EU AI\nAct and the need for trustworthy AI systems. It\noutlines seven essential requirements for building\nsuch systems: human agency and oversight;\ntechnical robustness and safety; privacy and data\ngovernance; transparency; diversity, non-\ndiscrimination, and fairness; societal and\nenvironmental well-being; and accountability. This\nchapter describes the structure of the Act, including\ndefinitions, key players, risk classifications, and the\nimplementation timeline, and provides an overview\nof this significant regulatory framework.\nChapter\xa02, “AI Engineering: A Proactive Compliance\nCatalyst”, explains how combining CRISP-ML(Q)\nwith AI engineering helps organizations meet the\ncompliance requirements of the EU AI Act. CRISP-\nML(Q) guides the AI lifecycle through distinct\nphases, such as data preparation and model\nevaluation, while MLOps principles including\nautomation, versioning, testing, and monitoring\nprovide the operational backbone for ensuring AI\nsystems are reliable, reproducible, and continuously\ncompliant. In this chapter, you will also learn about\nthe MLOps Stack Canvas as a framework for\ndefining the necessary technical infrastructure,\ncovering data, code, and model management, to\nsupport proactive compliance engineering.\nChapter\xa03, “Data and AI Governance and AI\nEngineering”, explains the critical roles of data\ngovernance and AI governance within the context of\nthe EU AI Act. In this chapter, you will learn about\nhow these governance concepts can be practically\nintegrated into the AI system development lifecycle\nto ensure trustworthy and compliant AI.\nChapter\xa04, “AI System Assessment and Tailoring AI\nEngineering for Different Risk Levels”, focuses on\nthe crucial initial steps for organizations to achieve\ncompliance with the EU AI Act. You will learn about\ncreating an inventory of your existing AI systems\nand how to classify their risk level to determine the\napplicable obligations. The chapter also explains the\ndifferent roles organizations can take (provider or\ndeployer), which further tailors compliance\nrequirements.\nChapter\xa05, “AI Engineering for High-Risk AI\nSystems”, offers a comprehensive guide to\nimplementing the EU AI Act’s requirements for high-\nrisk AI systems through AI engineering practices. It\nbreaks down key articles of the Act (Articles 9–15),\nfocusing on topics like risk management, data\ngovernance, documentation, recordkeeping,\ntransparency, human oversight, accuracy,\nrobustness, and security. You will learn how to map\nthe Act’s legal requirements to specific quality\nattributes and how to integrate them into the CRISP-\nML(Q) lifecycle. This chapter shows why\ndocumentation and metadata management are\ncrucial for demonstrating compliance and ensuring\nthe trustworthiness of high-risk AI systems.\nChapter\xa06, “AI Engineering for Limited-Risk AI\nSystems”, focuses on how to develop AI systems that\nmeet the EU AI Act’s transparency obligations,\nwhich differ from the stricter conformity\nassessments required for high-risk systems. Here,\nyou will learn about integrating the SMACTR\nframework with the CRISP-ML(Q) lifecycle. This\nchapter also highlights the emerging role of AI\ngovernance platforms and various technical tools in\nfacilitating compliance and responsible AI\ndeployment.\nChapter\xa07, “Toward Trustworthy General-Purpose AI\nand Generative AI”, explains how the Act aims to\nbalance AI innovation with risk mitigation,\nintroducing concepts like GPAI and systemic risk. It\noutlines the specific transparency obligations for\ngenerative AI systems, such as informing users of AI\ninteractions and marking synthetic content, and\ndetails the regulations for GPAI models, including\ndocumentation and risk management requirements\nfor providers and deployers. You will also learn\nabout GenAIOps, a framework for operationalizing\nthe transparency and compliance aspects of GenAI\ndevelopment and deployment by integrating them\nwith established methodologies like CRISP-ML(Q)\nand the SMACTR framework.\nConventions Used in This Book\nTIP\nThis element signifies a tip or suggestion.\nNOTE\nThis element signifies a general note.\nWARNING\nThis element indicates a warning or caution.\nO’Reilly Online Learning\nNOTE\nFor more than 40 years, O’Reilly Media  has provided technology and\nbusiness training, knowledge, and insight to help companies succeed.\nOur unique network of experts and innovators share their\nknowledge and expertise through books, articles, and our\nonline learning platform. You can access\nthis page at https://oreil.ly/AI-engineer-EU -AI-Act .\nFor news and information about our books and courses,\nvisit https://oreilly .com .\nFind us on LinkedIn: https://linkedin.com/company/oreilly-\nmedia .\nWatch us on YouTube: https://youtube.com/oreillymedia .\nAcknowledgments\nBringing this book to life has been a truly fulfilling\nexperience. I’ve had so much support from so many people\nthroughout the process of writing the book—thank you so\nmuch to everyone who helped make it a reality!\nI would like to give an especially big thank you to the\nbook’s technical reviewers: Una Galyeva, Katharine Jarmul,\nJanna Lipenkova, Anil Sood, and Debmalya Biswas. Their\ntime and effort in reading through the initial draft and\nproviding comments, suggestions, and corrections were\ninvaluable, and they made significant contributions to\nimproving the book’s overall quality.\nEveryone at O’Reilly has been fantastic to work with\nthroughout the book’s lifecycle, starting with Nicole\nButterfield, who immediately saw the potential of the core\nmessage that EU AI Act compliance is fundamentally an\nengineering challenge. Sara Hunter worked intensively\nwith me to shape and edit the book, and when I was ready\nto move to the production process, Kristen Brown was just\namazing. A big thank you to the entire O’Reilly team,\nincluding copyeditor Rachel Head, proofreader Kim Cofer,\nindexer Ben Hurst, illustrator Kate Dullea, and the cover\ndesign team of Monica Kaamsvaag and Susan Brown. You\nare all heroes!\nChapter 1. Understanding\nthe AI Regulations\nAs people, organizations, and the public sector increasingly\nrely on AI to drive decision making, the technology must be\ntrustworthy. The EU AI Act aims to provide a legal\nframework for developing, deploying, and using AI\ntechnologies within the European Union, emphasizing\nsafety, transparency, and ethical considerations. It is a\nregulatory framework for artificial intelligence that\nincludes specific requirements for AI systems of different\nrisk categories within the EU. This book is focused on\nunderstanding and implementing the regulatory\nrequirements set by the European Union’s legislation on\nartificial intelligence. Please note that the guidance it\nprovides is not intended as a substitute for obtaining\nprofessional legal advice.\nThis chapter begins by outlining the motivation behind the\nEU AI Act, emphasizing the idea of “trustworthy AI,” and\nidentifying the essential requirements for ensuring AI is\ntrustworthy. It then describes the structure of the EU AI\nAct, including its definitions, key stakeholders, risk\nclassifications (prohibited, high risk, limited risk, and\nminimal risk), and the implementation timeline. Finally, it\nbriefly compares the EU AI Act with other international\nregulations and standards related to AI.\nWARNING\nThe author is not a lawyer, and this book does not provide legal\nadvice. The intersection of law and artificial intelligence is a complex\nsubject that requires expertise beyond the scope of AI, data science,\nand machine learning. Legal considerations surrounding AI systems\ncan be complex and far-reaching. If you have any legal concerns\nrelated to the AI systems you are working on, seek professional legal\nadvice from qualified experts in the field.\nThe Motivation for the EU AI Act:\nTrustworthy AI\nAs AI becomes increasingly intertwined with our daily lives,\none of the challenges we face is learning to navigate the\nuncertainty that comes with it. This uncertainty is inherent\nto AI. AI models’ predictive accuracy has long been\nconsidered a core evaluation criterion when building an AI\nsystem. However, with the widespread use of AI in critical\nareas such as human resources, transportation, finance,\nmedicine, and security, there is a growing need for these\nsystems to be trustworthy—and traditional predictive\naccuracy alone is not sufficient to build trustworthy AI\napplications.\nTo better understand trustworthy AI, let’s start with its\ndefinition. Trustworthy AI  is an umbrella term that refers to\nartificial intelligence systems that are designed and\ndeveloped with principles such as fairness, privacy, and\nnon-discrimination in mind, and with robust mechanisms to\nensure reliability, security, and resilience. Within the AI\ncommunity, this term is used interchangeably with\nresponsible AI , ethical AI , reliable AI , and values-driven AI .\nTrustworthy AI systems must be adaptable to diverse and\nchanging environments and robust against various types of\ndisruptions, including cyber threats, data variability, and\noperational changes. They should operate transparently\nand be held accountable, with continuous monitoring and\nevaluation to respect human rights, including privacy and\nfreedom from discrimination, and to ensure adherence to\ndemocratic values.\nTrustworthy AI is a complex term that incorporates a long\nlist of concepts and principles, which are visualized in\nFigure\xa01-1. These concepts lay the foundation for\nunderstanding the EU AI Act.\nTrustworthiness in AI is grounded in the three pillars of\nlawfulness , ethics , and robustness . First, AI systems should\nbe lawful, meaning they must comply with all relevant\nregulations to ensure a fair market, promote economic\nbenefits, and protect citizens’ rights. Second, they must be\nbuilt on ethical principles and values, incorporating input\nfrom all stakeholders and establishing appropriate\nfeedback mechanisms. Finally, they must be robust,\naccounting for potential risks and ensuring safety at every\nstage of development and deployment.\n\nFigure 1-1. The foundation and seven requirements of trustworthy AI\nStanding on these three pillars are seven key requirements\nthat AI systems must implement to be deemed trustworthy:\n1. Human agency and oversight\n2. Technical robustness and safety\n3. Privacy and data governance\n4. Transparency\n5. Diversity, non-discrimination, and fairness\n6. Societal and environmental well-being\n7. Accountability\nThese come directly from the requirements outlined in the\nEthics Guidelines for Trustworthy AI developed by the\nEuropean Commission’s High-Level Expert Group on AI (AI\nHLEG). In the following sections, I provide an explanation\nof each.\nHuman Agency and Oversight\nHuman agency and oversight are crucial in developing and\noperating AI systems. Human agency  refers to the ability of\nindividuals to make informed decisions and maintain\ncontrol. AI systems should support this by providing\ntransparency, interpretability, and mechanisms for control\nand intervention that enable humans to understand and\ninfluence the system’s decisions and actions.\nHuman oversight  involves establishing governance\nprocesses and mechanisms that allow for human\nmonitoring, evaluation, and intervention in the operation of\nAI systems. This includes ensuring transparency and\ninterpretability of the systems’ decision-making processes\nbased on the results produced by the AI models.\nAdditionally, AI systems should provide human control\nmechanisms (e.g., the ability to override, adjust, or shut\ndown the system when necessary). As depicted in Figure\xa01-\n2, I distinguish between four different modes of human\noversight:\nHuman-in-command\nThis mode represents the highest level of human control,\nwhere humans maintain ultimate authority and\nresponsibility over the AI system. Humans must explicitly\nauthorize any AI system action. They are at the core of\ndecision-making processes and always make the ﬁnal\ndecisions. In this mode, humans oversee the AI system’s\noverall activity and can decide when and how to use the\nsystem in speciﬁc situations. For example, the “AI cockpit”\nconcept demonstrates the human-in-command approach by\nproviding a central user interface that enables operators to\nmonitor AI systems’ eﬀects on diﬀerent user groups,\nevaluate results against technical and ethical criteria, and\nexercise the authority to disable the systems when\nnecessary.\nHuman-in-the-loop\nAI systems require human interaction at critical points in\nthe decision-making process to add a layer of human\nexperience and contextual understanding that AI currently\nlacks. Often, this involvement entails guiding the direction of\ndecisions based on AI-generated predictions. The level of\nintervention and control might depend on the risk level of\nthe system. Medical AI applications, for example, will\ntypically require more intensive human intervention and\ncontrol than less critical AI applications.\nHuman-on-the-loop\nThe human plays an observer role, monitoring the actions of\nthe AI system and intervening if necessary.\nHuman-out-of -the-loop\nThe AI system operates independently, without human\nintervention or real-time supervision. This scenario is\npossible when decisions based on the system’s predictions\ncan be programmatically implemented. Often, this mode is\napplied in trading applications that require data processing\nat speed, where human oversight is not realistic.\nFigure 1-2. The diﬀerent levels of human oversight in AI\nThe modes described here are each suitable for different AI\nuse cases. Regardless of the one you use, proper\nimplementation of the human agency and oversight\nrequirement is crucial for designing and developing ethical\nAI systems that benefit from the strengths of AI while\nmitigating potential risks.\nTechnical Robustness and Safety\nTo be trustworthy, AI systems need to be accurate, reliable,\nand able to repeat their results. They should also have a\n“backup plan” if something goes wrong. In computer\nscience, robustness  means a system can keep working\ncorrectly even when there are mistakes, unusual inputs, or\nunexpected situations. For AI, robustness means that a\nmachine learning (ML) model can keep making good,\nreliable predictions despite facing different conditions,\ntricky inputs, or changes in the type of data it sees.\nIn simpler terms, a robust ML model can still give you the\ncorrect answers even if the information it receives is\nsomewhat noisy, messy, or comes from a different source\nthan the model was originally trained on. If these\ndifferences cause its performance to drop suddenly, then\nit’s not considered robust anymore. Achieving robustness is\nessential for building trust in AI—especially in areas like\nself-driving cars, cybersecurity systems, and healthcare,\nwhere a single mistake can have serious consequences.\nThere are three core layers to an AI system: the data it’s\ntrained on, the algorithm it uses, and the software that puts\nthem to use. Correspondingly, there are three levels of\nrobustness:\nData robustness  requires training the system on a\nwide variety of data types and scenarios. This allows\nthe model to “learn” to handle different situations\nand ensures it is less likely to fail when confronted\nwith new or unexpected conditions. For example, a\nself-driving car’s AI should be trained on data from\ndifferent weather conditions to handle rain, snow,\nand fog. In cybersecurity, an intrusion detection\nsystem should learn from many different types of\nnetwork traffic and attack methods, so it can catch\nnew threats as they emerge.\nAlgorithmic robustness  focuses on defending against\nattacks that try to trick the AI model itself. Some\nattacks occur at the moment of decision making by\nslightly altering the input to confuse the model.\nOthers are executed during the training phase by\nintroducing “poisoned” data that causes the model\nto make incorrect predictions later. A robust\nalgorithm should be able to handle noisy or altered\ninputs, changes in data types, and even deliberately\nmanipulated inputs designed to fool it. For example,\na robust language model can deal with misspellings,\nslang, or strange grammar without losing accuracy.\nSystem-level robustness  looks at the bigger picture\n—the entire lifecycle of the AI product, from how\ndata is collected to how the model is built, tested,\nand finally used in the real world. By examining\nevery step, we can spot risks before they lead to\nserious problems. This holistic approach ensures the\nentire AI pipeline, and its interaction with other\nsystems, remains steady and secure.\nIn research, we often differentiate two types of robustness:\nNon-adversarial robustness\nCan the model handle everyday challenges, like noisy inputs\nor slight changes in the environment, without losing\naccuracy?\nAdversarial robustness\nCan the model resist “tricks” that attackers use intentionally\nto confuse it, such as subtly modiﬁed inputs designed to\nproduce the wrong answer?\xa0\nTo measure how robust an AI model is, practitioners and\nresearchers look at a variety of measurements:\nAccuracy\nHow many predictions the model gets right.\nError rate\nHow many incorrect predictions the model makes.\nSensitivity (recall)\nThe model’s ability to correctly identify items that have a\ngiven condition (true positives). For example, if we are\nimplementing a binary classiﬁer, of all the cases that should\nbe labeled “positive,” how many are correctly identiﬁed?\nSpeciﬁcity\nThe model’s ability to correctly identify items that do not\nhave a given condition (true negatives). For example, of all\nthe cases that should be labeled “negative,” how many are\ncorrectly rejected?\nRobustness curves\nGraphs that show how the model’s performance changes\nwhen we introduce challenges, like more noise, missing\ninformation, or unusual inputs.\nAll of these measurements can help us understand the\nmodel’s strengths and weaknesses. However, the best way\nto measure robustness typically depends on the specific\nproblem and requirements of the AI system.\nPrivacy and Data Governance\nThe second requirement of trustworthy AI systems is that\nthey must ensure full respect for privacy and personal data\nprotection, in line with Articles 7 and 8 of the Charter of\nFundamental Rights of the European Union.\nMany AI systems, such as recommender systems and\nsystems that provide personalized predictions, require\nsome amount of personally identifiable information (PII).\nHandling of PII is a subject to regulations within the EU\nand the US, under the General Data Protection Regulation\n(GDPR) and the California Consumer Privacy Act (CCPA).\nBy examining how an AI system collects, stores, processes,\nand shares personal or sensitive data, privacy impact\nassessments  (PIAs) help organizations spot areas where\nprivacy might be at risk and guide them in modifying the\nsystem, implementing safeguards, and complying with\nrelevant privacy laws and regulations. PIAs should ideally\nbe conducted early in the design process, to proactively\nidentify, assess, and mitigate potential privacy risks. This is\nan example of privacy by design , an engineering philosophy\nthat involves embedding privacy protections into every\nphase of an AI system’s lifecycle—from data collection and\npreprocessing to model training, deployment, and\nmaintenance. This ensures that privacy isn’t just treated as\na legal afterthought or a check-the-box compliance step at\nthe end of the development process, but as a fun')
```

257\nHow to unlock these benefits in three easy steps  ������������������������������������������������������������  257\nOther Books You May Enjoy   263\nIndex   267\nPreface\nIn today’s extremely interconnected environment, REST APIs are the lingua franca for different \nsoftware to communicate with each other. The industry has tried different communication sys -\ntems in the past, such as SOAP, CORBA, and custom-made solutions, but REST has been the de \nfacto standard. Its simplicity and ability to leverage the underlying technologies, such as the HTTP \nprotocol, have positioned REST as the go-to option for most developers.\nREST is a solid option, but REST can be executed with many languages, such as Python, Ruby, \nPHP, Rust, and so on. But we are going to talk about how to do it with Go, but why Go? Go is a \nmodern, robust, fast, and easy-to-learn language, making it ideal for REST APIs. Its native com -\npilation makes it an amazing candidate for the speed requirement of modern APIs; its strong \ntyping makes it perfect for ensuring safety and robustness in your services, and its simplicity \nensures that incorporating new developers, even if they need to learn the language, is not going \nto become a nightmare.\nThis book covers REST and Go, but I’m a firm believer in understanding the foundations of things, \nso we will also discuss topics such as the HTTP protocol used under the hood, cache approaches, \nobservability, security, and much more. It provides you with the skills to develop REST APIs in Go \nand teaches you why we follow that approach and what the technological concepts are behind it.\nI want to focus on the concepts and keep things as simple and close to the Go standard library as \npossible, covering specialized libraries only when no built-in package provides that for us. How -\never, we will also provide examples for those who prefer different approaches to doing things, \nsuch as using query builders, ORMs, frameworks, or testing assertion libraries, giving you the \nfinal say on where you want to put the fine-grain control, and where you want to delegate to \nlibraries that make your life easier.\nPreface xvi\nWho this book is for\nThis book is for developers who want to gain a practical understanding of how to build REST APIs \nin general and, more specifically, using the Go programming language. While knowledge about \nGo is not strictly needed to get value from this book, some minimal understanding is required \nto get the most out of it.\nWhat this book covers\nChapter 1 , Introduction to APIs , goes through the fundamental concepts of REST APIs and explores \nother alternatives such as SOAP and GraphQL.\nChapter 2 , Exploring REST APIs , covers the tools needed to explore and analyze existing APIs, such \nas cURL and Postman. It enables you to explore and test your own APIs in the following chapters.\nChapter 3 , Building a REST Client , exposes the tools provided by Go’s standard library for accessing \nAPIs, and how you can build your own client library for consuming an existing API.\nChapter 4 , Designing Your REST API , introduces the main concepts of REST APIs such as routes, \nresources, and actions, and the semantics associated with them in the REST API ecosystem.\nChapter 5 , Authentication and Authorization , explores the needs and methods used for enforcing \nproper authentication in authorization in your API, exposing concepts such as JWT or the au -\nthentication HTTP header.\nChapter 6 , Data Persistency , provides the knowledge needed to create the persistency layer of your \napplication, including SQL database usage, the repository pattern, and building queries using \nthe Squirrel query builder.\nChapter 7 , API Security , goes through the different security features provided by the HTTP protocol \nand leveraged by the REST APIs. The chapter covers things such as data validation, sanitization, \nand password handling.\nChapter 8 , API Performance , focuses on making your API fast, including topics such as cache control \nfrom the HTTP protocol, the Go profiler, and building and understanding benchmarks in Go code.\nChapter 9 , Deploying Your API , examines the different options for deploying REST APIs, from de -\nploying your API in bare metal to a more sophisticated approach such as Kubernetes or function \nas a service.\nPreface xvii\nChapter 10 , Testing , exposes the different tools that Go provides for testing, and the different kinds \nof tests that you can build, from unit to integration tests, exploring all of them. We also explore \nthe testify assertion library as an alternative to the Go standard library testing tools.\nChapter 11 , Documenting with OpenAPI , shows the value of properly documenting your REST API \nwith the standard tooling out there provided by the OpenAPI initiative. It includes generating \ndocumentation and exposing that to tools such as Postman.\nChapter 12 , Metrics, Logs, and Traces , introduces the concepts of observability for your REST API \napplication. This chapter explores the data that we collect (metrics, logs, and traces). It also ex -\nplores the tools that help you understand the collected data, such as Jaeger and Grafana.\nChapter 13 , Using GORM , offers an alternative approach for the storage layer, building it with \nGORM instead of the standard library, or the Squirrel library.\nChapter 14 , Using the Echo Framework , explores other ways of generating your API that are not \nbased on the standard library; instead, it uses a third-party framework.\nTo get the most out of this book\nYou will need Go 1.23 or greater installed in your computer to follow the examples. Also, we are \ngoing to extensively use tools such as cURL to verify the correct behavior, and in some chapters, \nwe will explore approaches that require tools such as Docker, Grafana, or Node.js, among others.\nSoftware/hardware covered in the book Operating system requirements\nGo 1.23 or greater Windows, macOS, or Linux\ncURL\nPostman\nIf you are using the digital version of this book, we advise you to type the code yourself or \naccess the code from the book’s GitHub repository (a link is available in the next section)�  \nDoing so will help you avoid any potential errors related to the copying and pasting of code �\nDownload the example code files\nThe code bundle for the book is hosted on GitHub at https://github.com/PacktPublishing/\nModern-REST-API-Development-in-Go . They help us connect our systems, \nautomate processes in those systems, and adapt those systems to our needs instead of our needs \nto those systems. All that is done thanks to APIs.\nThis book will explore how to build APIs in Go from scratch. We’ll start with the basics of an API \nand how to explore it. Then, we’ll go through the incremental process of creating an API, from \nadding the basic endpoints to incorporating things such as security and telemetry. As an optional \nexercise, we’ll use a web framework instead of the standard library and an ORM instead of SQL \nqueries.\nIn this chapter, we’ll cover the following topics:\n• The different kinds of APIs\n• The basic concepts around REST APIs\n• How to build a very basic API in Go\nBut let’s start from the beginning. What exactly is an API? W elcome to your next br eakthr ough.\nACHAPTER 1: DEFINITION\nAND HIST ORY OF AI\nAGENTS\nrtificial Intelligence (AI) agents are systems designed to perform\ntasks autonomously , simulating human-like abilities such as learning,\nreasoning, and decision-making. These agents can perceive their\nenvironment, process information, and take action to achieve specific goals.\nWhat distinguishes AI agents from traditional software applications is their\ncapacity for independent operation, allowing them to adapt their behaviors\nbased on experiences or data inputs rather than strictly following predefined\ninstructions.\nThe development of AI agents traces back to the mid-20th century , rooted\nin the broader field of artificial intelligence. Pioneers like Alan T uring and\nJohn McCarthy laid the foundational concepts that would evolve into\nintelligent systems capable of autonomous actions. T uring’ s influential\n1950 paper , "Computing Machinery and Intelligence," posed the\nprovocative question of whether machines could think, sparking enduring\ndiscussions in the field. McCarthy , who coined the term "artificial\nintelligence" in 1956, or ganized the Dartmouth Conference—a pivotal\nevent that ignited interest and research in machine learning and cognitive\ncomputing.\nAs researchers delved into these ideas, they developed various models of\nintelligence. Early AI agents were primarily rule-based systems that relied\non hardcoded instructions and expert knowledge. While these systems\nexhibited some level of autonomy , they lacked the adaptability we see in\nmodern AI. T ake this example, early chess-playing programs could evaluate\npotential moves based on predetermined strategies but often struggled when\nfaced with unexpected scenarios.\nThe late 20th century marked a turning point with the advent of machine\nlearning. Rather than being confined by explicit rules, these systems began\nto learn from data. This transformation was driven by advancements in\ncomputational power and the availability of lar ge datasets. Researchers\ndeveloped algorithms enabling AI agents to enhance their performance over\ntime through experience—a concept central to fields like reinforcement\nlearning.\nReinforcement learning has been particularly influential in shaping\nautonomous systems. Drawing inspiration from behavioral psychology , this\napproach trains agents using rewards and penalties based on their actions\nwithin an environment. This method has led to significant breakthroughs in\ngame-playing AI; a notable example is DeepMind’ s AlphaGo, which\ndefeated world champion Go players by employing complex strategies\nlearned through countless simulated games. These advancements not only\nhighlight AI agents\' ability to learn but also demonstrate their potential to\nsurpass human expertise in specific domains.\nThe evolution of AI agents also reflects changing societal needs and\ntechnological capabilities. In the 1980s and 1990s, there was a sur ge of\ninterest in expert systems—AI programs designed to mimic human\ndecision-making in fields like medicine or finance. However , their reliance\non extensive rule sets limited their flexibility and adaptability . As\nresearchers recognized that true autonomy required dynamic adaptation, the\nfield began embracing more generalized approaches to intelligence.\nT oday , AI agents are embedded in many aspects of daily life—from virtual\nassistants like Siri and Alexa to advanced robotics used in manufacturing\nand logistics. The current landscape is characterized by a blend of\ntechniques such as deep learning, natural language processing, and\ncomputer vision, all contributing to increasingly sophisticated autonomous\nbehaviors.\nUnderstanding this historical context is crucial for appreciating how far AI\nagents have come—and where they might be headed next. The progression\nfrom simple rule-based systems to complex adaptive agents reflects not\nonly technological advancements but also our evolving understanding of\nintelligence itself. Each milestone along this journey has laid a foundation\nfor creating intelligent systems that can think independently , learn from\ntheir environment, and act ef fectively within it.\nAs we explore the capabilities and architectures of these agents further , it\nbecomes evident that the journey is ongoing; each innovation opens new\npossibilities for automation and artificial intelligence, continually\nchallenging our perceptions of what machines can achieve autonomously .\nT ypes of AI Agents\nAI agents can be classified into several distinct types, each tailored to\naddress specific tasks and operate under dif ferent intelligence paradigms.\nUnderstanding these classifications provides a valuable framework for\nassessing their capabilities and applications, which is crucial not only for\nthe design of autonomous systems but also for leveraging their potential in\nreal-world scenarios.\nAt a foundational level, AI agents can be divided into three main categories:\nreactive agents, deliberative agents, and hybrid agents. Reactive agents\nfunction based on predefined rules or conditions, responding to\nenvironmental stimuli without any internal representation of that\nenvironment. A straightforward example of this is a simple chatbot that\ngenerates replies based on keywords in user input. Lacking memory of past\ninteractions or the ability to learn from them, reactive agents operate strictly\nwithin the confines set by their developers. While ef fective for basic tasks,\nthey fall short in complex decision-making contexts.\nIn contrast, deliberative agents utilize an internal model of their\nenvironment to engage in higher -level reasoning and decision-making.\nThese agents assess potential actions based on predicted outcomes,\nexemplified by autonomous vehicles that employ sensors to map\nsurroundings and algorithms to forecast traf fic patterns. Such systems rely\non sophisticated techniques like planning algorithms and knowledge\nrepresentation methods, enabling them to navigate dynamic environments\nadeptly . This complexity underscores the need for advanced architectures\ncapable of processing vast amounts of data and adapting accordingly .\nHybrid agents combine the strengths of both reactive and deliberative\napproaches, creating more robust systems. T ake this example, a robot\ndesigned for warehouse logistics might use reactive strategies to manage\nimmediate obstacles while employing deliberative methods for broader\nroute optimization. This duality enhances performance by facilitating rapid\nresponses to unforeseen events while maintaining long-term planning\ncapabilities.\nAnother important classification distinguishes between goal-based agents\nand utility-based agents. Goal-based agents prioritize achieving specific\nobjectives set by their creators, navigating their environment with a clear\ntar get in mind. An example is an AI agent programmed to play chess; it\nevaluates moves based solely on the goal of winning the game. While\nstraightforward, this approach can become limiting when confronted with\ncomplex scenarios that involve multiple competing goals.\nUtility-based agents build upon this idea by assigning values to various\noutcomes based on preferences or risk assessments. Rather than merely\npursuing goals, these agents strive to maximize utility or satisfaction from\navailable options in a given situation. For example, an investment AI\nevaluates stocks by analyzing potential gains against risks before deciding\nwhere to allocate resources, focusing on choices that yield the best overall\nreturn on investment.\nRecent advancements in machine learning have introduced another\ncategory: learning agents. These systems enhance their performance\nthrough experience, continuously adapting their strategies based on\nfeedback from their interactions within the environment. Reinforcement\nlearning exemplifies this approach well—agents learn optimal behaviors\nthrough trial and error , gradually refining their actions over time based on\nrewards or penalties received after each interaction.\nAdditionally , there are specialized types of AI agents designed for specific\napplications—such as multi-agent systems that consist of multiple\ninteracting agents working together or competing to solve complex\nproblems. In these settings, ef fective communication among agents\nbecomes essential; protocols must be established for smooth information\nexchange and coordinated actions.\nConsider swarm intelligence—an approach inspired by natural systems like\nflocks of birds or schools of fish. Here, individual agents follow simple\nrules while collectively displaying complex behaviors that emer ge from\nlocal interactions among them. This principle has been ef fectively applied\nacross various fields such as robotics and network optimization, leading to\nef ficient yet resilient solutions.\nAs we explore the diverse types of AI agents further , it becomes evident\nthat each type serves unique purposes dictated by specific tasks and\nenvironments. The choice between reactive versus deliberative structures or\nbetween goal-based and utility-based frameworks can significantly\ninfluence an agent’ s ef fectiveness in real-world applications.\nUnderstanding these distinctions is crucial for anyone looking to design or\nimplement AI systems ef fectively . Each type possesses inherent strengths\nand weaknesses that must be carefully evaluated when developing solutions\naimed at addressing modern challenges across industries—from healthcare\ninnovations driven by intelligent diagnostics to financial systems equipped\nwith predictive analytics capabilities.\nAs we refine our understanding of these classifications, we move closer to\nconstructing more advanced autonomous systems capable not only of\nperforming tasks but doing so with increasing sophistication—enabling\nthem to think critically about their actions and adaptively learn from their\nexperiences as they navigate the complexities of the world around them.\nReal-world Applications\nThe real-world applications of AI agents are as varied as the challenges\nthey seek to address. These intelligent systems are making significant\nstrides across numerous sectors, including healthcare, finance,\ntransportation, and entertainment. By introducing ef ficiencies, enhancing\ndecision-making processes, and creating innovative user experiences, AI\nagents are not just a fleeting technological trend; they signify a fundamental\ntransformation in our interactions with machines and the ways in which\nthey assist us.\nIn healthcare, for example, AI agents are revolutionizing diagnostics and\ntreatment plans. T ake radiology , where advanced image recognition\nalgorithms analyze medical images with exceptional accuracy , often\nspotting anomalies that might escape human detection. A prime illustration\nis Google\'s DeepMind, which developed an AI system capable of\ndiagnosing eye diseases from retinal scans. This technology accelerates the\ndiagnostic process and improves accuracy , facilitating timely interventions\nthat can preserve patients\' sight.\nBeyond diagnostics, AI agents are also redefining personalized medicine.\nBy examining extensive datasets that encompass genetic information,\ntreatment histories, and lifestyle factors, these systems can recommend\ncustomized treatment plans for individual patients. This precision medicine\napproach not only enhances patient outcomes but also optimizes resource\nallocation within healthcare systems.\nIn the realm of finance, AI agents play a pivotal role in algorithmic trading.\nThese agents analyze vast amounts of market data in real time, executing\ntrades based on established criteria or adapting to market trends. T ake this\nexample, companies like Renaissance T echnologies utilize sophisticated\nmachine learning algorithms that draw on historical data to predict future\nstock movements. Such automated trading strategies enhance profitability\nwhile also adding liquidity to the markets.\nFraud detection is another critical area where AI agents excel in finance. By\nscrutinizing transaction patterns and identifying anomalies, these systems\nhelp institutions mitigate risks related to financial fraud. PayPal, for\nexample, employs machine learning algorithms that learn from past\ntransaction data to proactively flag suspicious activity . This capability\nsignificantly reduces fraud incidents and bolsters customer trust in digital\npayment systems.\nT ransportation is yet another sector under going significant transformation\ndue to AI agents. The advent of autonomous vehicles marks a substantial\nadvancement in this field. Companies like T esla and W aymo are developing\ncars equipped with advanced sensors and machine learning algorithms that\nallow them to navigate complex environments safely . These vehicles\nleverage real-time data from their surroundings to make instantaneous\ndecisions—such as changing lanes or stopping for pedestrians—ultimately\nenhancing road safety and ef ficiency .\nLogistics and supply chain management have also reaped substantial\nbenefits from AI implementation. Intelligent systems optimize delivery\nroutes for trucks, reducing fuel consumption and ensuring timely deliveries.\nT ake this example, UPS utilizes AI-driven routing software that analyzes\ntraf fic patterns and weather conditions to dramatically improve delivery\nef ficiency .\nIn entertainment, AI agents have transformed the way we consume content\nthrough personalized recommendations. Streaming platforms like Netflix\nuse sophisticated algorithms to analyze user behavior and suggest movies or\nshows tailored to individual tastes. By continually refining these\nsuggestions based on viewer feedback, these platforms boost user\nengagement and satisfaction.\nGaming has embraced AI agents as well, creating more immersive\nexperiences for players. Non-player characters (NPCs) powered by\nadvanced AI demonstrate realistic behaviors that adapt according to player\nactions, enriching gameplay complexity and enjoyment. The development\nof adaptive game mechanics reflects a growing trend toward dynamic and\nresponsive gaming environments.\nAnd, emer ging fields such as agriculture are increasingly harnessing\nintelligent systems to enhance sustainability and productivity . Precision\nagriculture employs drones equipped with AI algorithms to monitor crop\nhealth and optimize resource use—minimizing waste while maximizing\nyields.\nWhile these diverse applications highlight the transformative potential of AI\nagents across various domains, it is essential to acknowledge the ethical\nimplications that accompany their deployment. Issues related to data\nprivacy , algorithmic bias, and job displacement must be addressed as we\nintegrate these technologies more deeply into society .\nReflecting on the myriad ways AI agents operate within real-world contexts\nunderscores one clear truth: their potential is vast and lar gely untapped.\nEach application not only showcases current capabilities but also hints at\nfuture innovations waiting to unfold. Embracing this technology\nnecessitates a commitment to ethical practices while fostering an\nenvironment where creativity flourishes alongside responsible development\n—ultimately paving the way for a future where intelligent agents\nsignificantly enhance human life across all dimensions.\nOverview of Autonomy in Systems\nAutonomy in systems marks a significant departure from traditional\nreactive approaches, ushering in proactive decision-making capabilities.\nThis evolution is characterized by systems that can operate independently ,\nadapting their behavior based on environmental feedback and internal\nobjectives. As we delve into the intricacies of autonomy , we will uncover\nhow it empowers AI agents to perform ef fectively in real-world scenarios,\nthereby enhancing their utility and impact.\nAt the heart of autonomous systems is their ability to perceive and respond\nto their environment. This involves complex sensory integration, where data\nfrom various sources—such as cameras, microphones, and sensors—is\nprocessed in real time. T ake this example, consider autonomous vehicles\nequipped with an array of sensors that gather information about surrounding\ntraf fic, road conditions, and potential obstacles. The vehicle\'s AI analyzes\nthis data using algorithms tailored for real-time decision-making, enabling\nit to navigate city streets and adjust speed according to traf fic patterns. This\nimmediate responsiveness is crucial for ensuring both safety and ef ficiency .\nBuilding on this foundational perception is the framework for decision-\nmaking. Autonomous systems employ various algorithms to evaluate\noptions based on predefined goals. Reinforcement learning, a subset of\nmachine learning, exemplifies this process by allowing agents to learn\noptimal actions through trial and error . A practical example can be seen in\nrobotic vacuum cleaners that, initially programmed with basic navigation\nprinciples, utilize reinforcement learning to refine their cleaning paths over\ntime. By avoiding obstacles more ef fectively and optimizing coverage,\nthese devices illustrate how learning enhances performance.\nThe depth of autonomy is also significantly influenced by the complexity of\nthe tasks involved. Systems can vary widely , from simple rule-based\ndecision-making to advanced cognitive functions that emulate human\nreasoning. T ake this example, automated customer service agents often rely\non scripted responses tailored to user queries. In contrast, more\nsophisticated AI systems leverage natural language processing (NLP) to\ngrasp context and sentiment, enabling them to deliver personalized\nresponses that improve user experience.\nGoal-setting capabilities are another vital aspect of autonomy . Autonomous\nsystems are typically designed with specific objectives that guide their\nactions and decisions. In the realm of industrial automation, manufacturing\nrobots operate under clearly defined goals—such as maximizing production\nwhile minimizing downtime. These robots continuously assess their\nperformance against these tar gets, adjusting their actions as needed; if a\nmachine identifies an inef ficiency in its workflow , it can modify its\noperations to resolve the issue without human intervention.\nAs we explore autonomy further , ethical considerations become\nincreasingly important. The design and implementation of autonomous\nagents must navigate a landscape rich with moral implications. For\nexample, in healthcare applications where AI assists in diagnosing diseases\nor recommending treatment plans, it is critical to ensure that these systems\nare free from bias. Biased algorithms can lead to unequal treatment\noutcomes among dif ferent demographic groups, necessitating rigorous\ntesting protocols to validate fairness.\nT ransparency in decision-making processes is another crucial factor that\nfosters trust among users and stakeholders alike. When an AI agent makes a\nrecommendation or takes action based on its learned experiences or data\nanalysis, stakeholders should understand the reasoning behind those\ndecisions. This transparency not only mitigates risks but also promotes\naccountability—an essential component as autonomous systems\nincreasingly influence vital aspects of daily life.\nIntegrating autonomy across various sectors showcases both its benefits and\nchallenges, spanning applications from smart homes with ener gy-ef ficient\nAI assistants to complex financial models predicting market trends\nautonomously . Each implementation provides unique insights into the\nfunctioning of autonomous agents while emphasizing areas that require\ncareful consideration regarding ethical practices and social impact.\nunderstanding the role of autonomy within AI systems reveals its\ntransformative potential alongside its responsibilities toward society at\nlar ge. As technology continues to evolve at an unprecedented pace, striking\na balance between innovation and ethical considerations will be crucial for\nshaping a future where autonomous systems enrich human lives while\naligning with our values and societal norms. The journey toward achieving\ntrue autonomy will not only redefine our interactions with machines but\nalso challenge us to reflect on what it means for these intelligent entities to\noperate within our world responsibly .\nPython as a T ool for AI Development\nPython has emer ged as an exceptional tool for developing AI agents,\nespecially in the field of autonomous systems. Its simplicity and versatility\nhave made it the go-to language for both experienced developers and\nnewcomers. But what exactly makes Python so attractive in this context?\nOne key factor is its extensive ecosystem of libraries tailored specifically\nfor AI and machine learning.\nLibraries like T ensorFlow , PyT orch, and scikit-learn provide powerful tools\nthat simplify the development process. T ake this example, T ensorFlow\nenables ef ficient implementation of complex neural networks, making it\nparticularly well-suited for deep learning applications. When building a\nneural network for image recognition, a developer can utilize pre-built\nfunctions within T ensorFlow to define layers, activation functions, and loss\nmetrics, all without getting lost in the intricacies of lower -level\nprogramming. A typical implementation might look like this:\n```python\nimport tensorflow as tf\nfrom tensorflow .keras import layers, models\nmodel = models.Sequential([\nlayers.Conv2D(32, (3, 3), activation=\'relu\', input_shape=(28, 28, 1)),\nlayers.MaxPooling2D((2, 2)),\nlayers.Flatten(),\nlayers.Dense(64, activation=\'relu\'),\nlayers.Dense(10, activation=\'softmax\')\n])\nmodel.compile(optimizer=\'adam\',\nloss=\'sparse_categorical_crossentropy\',\nmetrics=[\'accuracy\'])\n```\nThis example highlights how easy it is to set up a convolutional neural\nnetwork (CNN) with just a few lines of code. This user - friendly nature\nencourages experimentation and rapid iteration—an essential aspect when\ndeveloping AI agents that need to learn from extensive datasets.\nAs we move from libraries to frameworks, our ability to create\ncomprehensive applications expands. Frameworks like Flask or FastAPI\ncan streamline the process of deploying an AI model as a web service. T ake\nthis example, if you\'ve built an image classification model using\nT ensorFlow , FastAPI allows you to expose this functionality via an API\nendpoint quickly:\n```python\nfrom fastapi import FastAPI\nfrom pydantic import BaseModel\nimport numpy as np\napp = FastAPI()\nclass Image(BaseModel):\ndata: list\n@app.post("/predict")\nasync def predict(image: Image):\n\\#\\# Convert the input image data into the format required by the model\ninput_data = np.array(image.data).reshape((1, 28, 28, 1))\nprediction = model.predict(input_data)\nreturn "predicted_class": np.ar gmax(prediction)\n```\nThis snippet demonstrates how Python simplifies the connection between\ncomplex AI models and user interfaces or other software components.\nAnd, the extensive community support surrounding Python cannot be\noverstated. W ith numerous forums like Stack Overflow and dedicated\ncommunities such as T ensorFlow’ s GitHub page or PyT orch forums\navailable for troubleshooting and knowledge sharing, developers are never\nalone on their journey . This collaborative environment fosters innovation\nand accelerates learning—a crucial aspect for anyone looking to dive into\nAI.\nAnother significant advantage of Python is its ability to handle data\nmanipulation ef fortlessly through libraries like pandas and NumPy . Data\npreprocessing is often one of the most critical steps in machine learning\nprojects. By utilizing pandas for tasks such as cleaning data or transforming\nit—like addressing missing values or encoding categorical variables—you\nprepare your AI agents to train on high-quality datasets:\n```python\nimport pandas as pd\n\\#\\# Load dataset\ndata = pd.read_csv(\'data.csv\')\n\\#\\# Handle missing values by filling them with the mean\ndata.fillna(data.mean(), inplace=T rue)\n\\#\\# Convert categorical variables using one-hot encoding\ndata = pd.get_dummies(data)\n```\nMastering data manipulation enables more ef fective feature engineering—a\nvital step that significantly influences your model’ s performance.\nAs we delve deeper into Python\'s capabilities for artificial intelligence\ndevelopment, we recognize that its ef fectiveness lies not only in its syntax\nbut also in its philosophy: readable code leads to maintainable projects over\ntime. Many successful open-source initiatives have adopted Python due to\nthis principle.\nAlso, Python\'s adaptability goes beyond basic functionality; it can integrate\nsmoothly with other languages like C++ or Java when performance\nbecomes critical—a necessity in production-grade systems where speed is\nparamount.\nT o wrap things up, while navigating the world of AI development with\nPython presents challenges—such as grasping complex algorithms or\ndebugging—it also of fers invaluable resources that can simplify these\nprocesses significantly . ofStyle\nfor Contract Drafting.\nThe first edition appeared in2004. Iwrote itwhile\nstill apracticing corporate attorney, soinmy\nattempts tomaster contract language Iwas\nnecessarily something ofadilettante. That changed\nin2006, when Imade thestudy ofcontract drafting\nmylivelihood. That focus meant that thesecond\nedition, which came out in2008, was avery\ndifferent book—entirely redesigned, significantly\nexpanded, extensively rewritten.\nSince then I’ve been engaged inthesame range of\nactivities that gave risetothesecond edition. I’ve\ncontinued toblog, originally atAdamsDrafting, then\natThe Koncise Drafter, now atAdams onContract\nDrafting. Blogging hasallowed metoairnew ideas,\nchew over problems, hear ofnew developments, and\nengage with like-minded readers toanextent that\notherwise would have been impossible. Since the\nsecond edition waspublished, I’ve posted more than\n800blog items, many ofthem representing myfirst\ntreatment of issues addressed in this edition.\nI’ve continued giving public seminars intheUnited\nStates with West LegalEdcenter andinCanada with\nOsgoode Professional Development, aswell as\n40\nin-house seminars atcompanies, law firms, and\ngovernment agencies. I’ve also presented public\nseminars inAustralia, Malaysia, Thailand, and\nSwitzerland; thathasgiven meabetter sense ofhow\ndrafting usages vary, ordon’t, from country to\ncountry. And I’ve continued teaching atthe\nUniversity ofPennsylvania Law School. I’ve\nlearned agreat deal from seminar participants and\nmy students.\nOne new development isthat I’ve launched\nKoncision Contract Automation. By 2011,\ndocument-assembly technology and my\nunderstanding ofmysubject had both developed\nenough toallow metotake ababy step toward\ncommoditizing contract language. Koncision’s\nproof-of-concept product is a\nconfidentiality-agreement template, available for\nfree. Compiling the language forthat template\nallowed metotestsome ofmyrecommendations\nand explore new issues.\nThanks tothewealth ofmaterial Ihadtodraw on,\nthere ismuch that isnew inthis edition. Ialso\nmoved some sections, and Iretooled much ofthe\nprose. Here’s an overview of the changes:\n•Introduction . Extensively revised.\n•Chapter 1 (The Characteristics of Optimal Contract\nLanguage) . New; includes some topics previously\naddressed in the introduction, in what is now chapter 7,\nand in what is now chapter 17.\n•Chapter 2 (The Front of the Contract) . Significantly\nexpanded by integrating new material throughout.\n41\n•Chapter 3 (Categories of Contract Language) . Greatly\nexpanded by integrating new material throughout; by\nadding new sections on language of belief, language of\nintention, and language of recommendation; and by\nadding, from what is now chapter 13, an expanded\ndiscussion of represents and warrants .\n•Chapter 4 (Layout) . Significantly expanded by integrating\nnew material throughout.\n•Chapter 5 (The Back of the Contract) . Slightly expanded.\n•Chapter 6 (Defined Terms) . Slightly expanded.\n•Chapter 7 (Sources of Uncertainty in Contract Language) .\nSignificantly expanded by integrating new material\nthroughout and by adding sections on antecedent\nambiguity and failure to address an issue.\n•Chapter 8 (“Reasonable Efforts” and Its Variants) . Slightly\nexpanded.\n•Chapter 9 (“Material” and “Material Adverse Change”) .\nSlightly expanded.\n•Chapter 10 (References to Time) . Significantly expanded by\nintegrating new material throughout.\n•Chapter 11 (Ambiguity of the Part Versus the Whole) .\nSignificantly expanded by adding a section on the\nambiguity that arises when one of a series linked by oris\nmodified by a conditional clause, and by adding a case\nstudy. Revised the sections on the effect of adjectives and\ncumulation of attributes.\n•Chapter 12 (Syntactic Ambiguity) . Significantly expanded\nby adding a section on closing modifiers with offsetting\ncommas and a section on the serial comma.\n•Chapter 13 (Selected Usages) . Expanded by more than\ntwo-thirds (in terms of the number of paragraphs).\n•Chapter 14 (Numbers and Formulas) . Slightly expanded.\n42\n•Chapter 15 (Provisions Specifying Drafting Conventions) .\nLargely unchanged.\n•Chapter 16 (Typography) . The section on fonts rewritten;\notherwise largely unchanged.\n•Chapter 17 (Drafting as Writing) . Shorter, because some\nsections were moved to chapter 1.\n•Chapter 18 (Amendments) . Largely unchanged.\n•Chapter 19 (Letter Agreements) . Largely unchanged.\n•Chapter 20 (Corporate Resolutions) . Largely unchanged.\nACKNOWLEDGMENTS\nIowe agreat debt tothereaders ofmyblog, atits\ndifferent addresses, who have offered mecomments\nand suggestions. My commenter hall offame\nincludes Michael Fleming, Eric Goldman, andChris\nLemens. Steven H.Sholk hasbeen aprolific source\nofleads. Mark Anderson hasprovided avaluable\nEnglish-law perspective. And Glenn D.West has been\ngracious enough tochime inwhen I’ve asked forthe\nbenefit ofhisunmatched expertise insome key\nareas.\nIowe particular thanks toGregory M.Harris.
