My daughter, Sydney,\nstands tobefarmore accomplished and engaged\nthan herfather. The support ofJoanne’s parents,\nSteve and Toni Kourepinos, continues tobe\ninvaluable. ButMax thePekingese, myindustrious\nassistant while Iwrote thesecond andthird editions,\nis no longer with us.\nAlthough mymother hassince passed away, I’ll\nrepeat the paragraph that rounded out the\nacknowledgments in the second edition:\nOne agreeable aspect ofwhat Inow doisthat I’m\nunexpectedly revisiting, inanaltogether more\nconcerted way, theaffinity forEnglish usage that\nwas routinely ondisplay around theAdams family\ndinner table. My mother Florence, mylate father\nCharles, and mysiblings living (Charles, Jr.and\nChristine) and departed (Adrian, Louise, and\nAndrew) created afertile mix ofintellect and\nidiosyncrasy. I thank them for it.\n45\nTHE SCOPE AND ORGANIZATION OFTHIS\nMANUAL\nThis manual offers guidelines forclear andconcise\ncontract language. Ifyoudraft contracts, thismanual\nwillhelp youensure thatthey’re clearer andshorter\nand that they express the transaction more\naccurately, allowing you and your organization to\nsave time and money, reduce risk, and compete\nmore effectively. Ifyou review ornegotiate\ncontracts, this manual will help you determine\nwhether deal points arearticulated inaway that\nmakes sense and will help you spot and address\npotential sources ofuncertainty. Ifyou interpret\ncontracts—for example, ifyou’re involved in\ndispute resolution—this manual willhelp youassess\nmeaning and determine what iscausing any\nconfusion.\nThis manual should beofusetoreaders inevery\ncontract ecosystem—a solo orsmall-firm general\npractitioner handling abroad range ofcontracts,\nfrom leases to separation agreements; a\ncontract-management professional responsible for\nnegotiating contracts with customers; abig-law\nassociate drafting mergers-and-acquisitions\ncontracts; anin-house lawyer overhauling the\ncompany’s template sales contracts; aparalegal\nreviewing confidentiality agreements acompany is\n46\nasked tosign; ajudge trying tomake sense ofa\nconfusing contract provision.\nThere aresome things this manual doesn’t do.It\ndoesn’t address what you should sayinacontract.\nInstead, itaddresses how tosay clearly and\neffectively whatever you want to say.\nItdoesn’t address contracts between businesses and\nindividual consumers. Instead, it’s intended for\nthose who draft, review, negotiate, orinterpret\ncontracts between parties who aresophisticated or\narerepresented bylegal counsel. Forsimplicity, this\nmanual refers tosuch contracts as“business\ncontracts.”\nItdoesn’t attempt asynthesis ofcurrent contract\nusages. Instead, itrecommends theclearest andmost\nconcise usages over those that have nothing but\ntradition going for them. Ifarecommendation\ndeparts markedly from what istraditional, that fact\nis noted.\nTokeep this manual concise, itdoesn’t contain\nfootnotes, itcites authorities sparingly, and itcuts\nshort some explication. Itdoesn’t offer a\nbibliography, because itattempts toaddress, in\nsufficient detail forthose seeking practical guidance,\nthefullrange ofissues relating tothelanguage and\nlayout of contracts.\nThe final chapter deals with corporate resolutions.\nThey aren’t contracts, but lawyers who draft\ncontracts areoften called ontodraft corporate\n47\nresolutions, which present issues analogous tothose\nthat arise in drafting contracts.\nAppendix 1contains three versions ofacontract: the\n“Before” version; the“Before” version, annotated\nwith footnotes toshow itsdrafting shortcomings;\nand the“After” version, redrafted consistent with\ntherecommendations contained inthismanual. The\ndifference between the“Before” version and the\n“After” version shows thecumulative effect ofa\nrigorous approach todrafting usages, bigandsmall.\nReaders might find that the footnotes inthe\nannotated “Before” version provide aquick way to\nfind those parts ofthismanual thatdiscuss issues of\nparticular interest tothem. And the“After” version\nshows what acontract would look likeifthedrafter\nwere to follow the recommendations in this manual.\nToillustrate theanalyis, thismanual contains many\nexamples ofcontract language. Except asindicated,\nthey’re not offered as models.\nWHY A MANUAL OF STYLE?\nAmanual ofstyle serves asaresource forany\nperson ororganization seeking greater clarity and\nconsistency inwritten usages. That’s thecase for\nany kind ofwriting, butforthefollowing three\nreasons amanual ofstyle should prove especially\nuseful tothose who draft, review, negotiate, or\ninterpret contracts.\nFirst, compared toother ki')

Failing that,\nsearch Craigslist for some used options.\n2One that specializes in startups. They cost the same as the big consumer banks, but tend to be more\nunderstanding of startup issues and have better customer service. I’ve always used Silicon Valley Bank,\nwhich has the additional benefit  of really excellent networking events that include its venture capitalist\nclients.Preface\nAnointed in the Elevator\nI became the CEO of my first startup in an elevator. I didn’t seize the reins of\npower in a bloodless coup. There was no passionate discussion or debate. I\nwasn’t even planning to discuss the topic when it happened. My cofounder\nmerely informed me that we were being stupid as the doors were about to open,\nand I was going to be the CEO from that moment forward.\nWhen Charles and I first decided to found Ontela, we were unclear about\nwho exactly was going to do what. My background was as a “program manager”\nand my cofounder’s was as a “developer.” Neither of us had ever managed more\nthan a handful of people. So we decided on what we thought was a perfect com-\npromise: I would be the President, and Charles would be CEO.\nNeedless to say, neither one of us had any idea how to do these jobs.\nBut from the start, we had a ridiculous amount of work to do. We knew little\nabout startups—I’d worked at one, but not as a founder; Charles had been a part\nof Expedia since its inception as a division within Microsoft, but that wasn’t\nexactly seed-stage territory. Besides doing familiar things like writing specs and\ncode, we had logistical (“Should we rent furniture or buy it?”1), operational\n(“Which bank should we use?”2), legal (“Should we incorporate in Bermuda? My\nix\nwww.it-ebooks.info\n3Y our uncle-lawyer had no idea what he was talking about—you should always incorporate in Delaware.\n4At least, that’s what we thought. There are other ways. Cheezburger, Inc. ’s CEO Ben Huh once told me, “I\ndon’t want to be another Asian dude running around Silicon Valley in glasses and slacks. ” He wore big,\nbright white glasses. Having some sort of personal branding to your dress style is not a terrible idea.uncle is a lawyer and says it’ll save on taxes.”3), and other conundrums of startup\nlife to solve.\nCharles was an accomplished software developer. I was a marginally compe-\ntent software dabbler. So I did design, recruiting, pitching, fundraising, business\nplan writing, financial  modeling, and many of the aforementioned odds and\nends. I also managed the output of a motley assortment of a half-dozen develop-\ners in various far-off  lands, who had—for a total cost of less than $500—built our\nfirst proof-of-concept prototype, and were continuing to maintain it while Charles\nworked on doing it the “Right Way" (i.e. something marginally more scalable\nthan a few shell scripts and a dodgy shared FTP server located in an indetermi-\nnate part of Southeast Asia).\nWe continued in this manner for a while, spending nights and weekends\nbanging away on the nascent project. And after a few months, we decided to dou-\nble down on our ignorance: we quit our jobs to work on Ontela full time. As soon\nas that die was cast, I began to sink more and more energy into fundraising. And\nit was early in February of 2006 when our fateful elevator ride occurred.\nWe were in the lobby of the Millennium Tower in downtown Seattle, WA,\njust north of the historical district of Pioneer Square, where we would eventually\nfound our company. It’s just one street south that the century-old cobblestones\nand purple glass sidewalks appear, but the Millennium Tower was clearly on the\nmodern side of the divide.\nWe passed muster with the lobby guardian, wearing our finest  khaki slacks\nand button-up shirts–the semi-casual entrepreneur’s standard-issue uniform.4\nWe stepped into the elevator, which quietly streaked to the building’s top floor.\nMy ears were still popping when Charles turned to me and gave me an\nintent look. “Hey Dan... when they ask what our jobs are, you’re the CEO and\nPresident. I’m the CTO. OK?”\nDing. The doors swung open. I furrowed my brow and opined on this unex-\npected development. “Um. Huh?”\nCharles stepped into the gap and the elevator doors banged on his foot.\n“We’re deluding ourselves. You’re the CEO. You’re doing the fundraising, the\nlegal stuff.  You’re the face of the company. You’re selling the vision. I’m buildingx | Preface\nwww.it-ebooks.info\nit. You’re the CEO, I’m the CTO.” Then he stepped out. I jumped after him as\nthe doors slid closed, narrowly missing me on the way. We walked up to the\nreceptionist and introduced ourselves. And that’s how I received the qualifica-\ntions,  training, and authority to be the CEO of Ontela.\nNeedless to say, I had no idea what I was doing.\nFiguring It Out\nMy CTO, friend, cofounder, and fellow employee Charles Zapata looked up at me\nfrom the other side of a Costco folding table, across an old inkjet printer. “I fig-\nured  out what we’re supposed to be doing here,” he said.\nEverything we said sounded resonant and carried an aura of importance.\nThat’s because we were in my basement, which was tiled and empty.\nIt was a few months after we’d quit our jobs to work on Ontela full time.\nCharles was coding feverishly; I had a multitude of tasks, but at the top of the list\nwas working every angle I could imagine to raise outside capital. I didn’t know it\nat the time, but I was the better part of a year away from anything resembling\nsuccess.\nIt had been a long day. By silent mutual agreement, we were both surfing  the\nInternet.\n“Entrepreneurship is the pursuit of opportunity without regard to the resour-\nces currently controlled,” Charles told me. “It’s from Professor Howard Steven-\nson at Harvard Business School. He’s right. That’s what we’re supposed to be\ndoing.”\nI nodded sagely, and went back to reading Boing Boing .\nThe quote sounded sort of faux-meaningful. It kind of rattled around in my\nbrain, but I didn’t really make much of it at the time.\nSince then, I’ve been the founder and CEO of five companies: Ontela (now\nPhotobucket), in the mobile imaging space; Sparkbuy (acquired by Google), a\ncomparison shopping site; Google Comparison Inc., a wholly owned subsidiary\nof Google; Robot Turtles LLC , a profitable  board game company and, at the time,\nthe most-backed board game in Kickstarter history; and now Glowforge Inc. ,\nwhich designs, manufactures, and sells 3D laser printers.\nOver the course of those five very different  companies, I learned the truth.\nStevenson was right. In fact, if you’re a startup CEO, this was as close as you\nwere going to find to a job description.\nThis book is dedicated to those who sit in the hot seat. If you’re doing it\nright, the opportunities are legendary. You will become Google, and organize thePreface | xi\nwww.it-ebooks.info\nworld’s information. You will become Apple, and equip the world with tricorders.\nYou will become Facebook, and restructure the way human beings communicate\nwith one another.\nEven if you don’t get there, you’re swinging for the fences. Strike that: you’re\nswinging for the moon, and there’s a real (if remote) chance that you’re going to\nknock it out of the sky.\nBut right now you have nothing.\nThat’s your job. To make something out of nothing. To pull together all the\ndisparate threads: recruiting (with no money), fundraising (with no reputation),\nand delivering joy (with no product).\nYou must fabricate the chicken and the egg.\nSimultaneously.\nIt’s one hell of a ride.\nMaybe Y ou Should Do That\nEvery year, O’Reilly Media, the publisher of this book, invites a hundred or so\nassorted geeks, technology influencers,  and miscellaneous interesting folk to an\napple orchard. Well, it used to be an apple orchard. Now it’s O’Reilly’s world\nheadquarters, a friendly complex on the outskirts of downtown Sebastopol, CA.\nThis is FOO camp: a weekend getaway for assorted “Friends of O’Reilly.”\nThe first time I attended, I fell for what I now believe to be an elaborate\nprank. In the invitation to the event, O’Reilly recommends that FOO campers\nactually camp. Literally pitch a tent on the lawn. This seemed like a fine idea until\n9 a.m. rolled around and the interior temperature of my borrowed bivouac top-\nped 100 degrees. I woke up drenched in sweat and rolled out into the sunshine.\nMy misery was only momentarily abated when someone walked up and blocked\nthe blinding rays. That someone was tech luminary Robert Scoble, who cheer-\nfully wished me a great morning as I staggered to my feet, blinded by perspira-\ntion, and headed off to find a shower.\nOne of the nice benefits  of a well-curated conference is that you can strike up\na conversation with just about anyone in the coffee  line and not regret it later. As\nI dragged myself to the coffee  line after not nearly enough sleep, the person\npumping an Aervoid full of Peet’s coffee  into a paper cup was none other than \nLaurie Petrycki, O’Reilly’s publisher at the time. I brightened up.\n“I’ve got a question for you!” I said. I open a lot of conversations that way.\n“What’s that?” she said.xii | Preface\nwww.it-ebooks.info\n5I misunderstood it.\n6M.C. Cornelis, et al., “Coffee,  CYP1A2 Genotype, and Risk of Myocardial Infarction, ” Journal of the Ameri-\ncan Medical Association  295 (2006): 1135–1141.“Every startup I’ve ever founded, worked at, visited, or seen on TV has had a\nbig stack of O’Reilly books in the background. We buy them by the bushel. You\nare the de facto literary brand of choice for tech companies, large and small.”\nLaurie nodded modestly.\n“So why don’t you publish books about them? We’re in an era where The\nSocial Network  is a hit movie and Shark Tank  is the most popular show on Friday\nnights. The most exciting companies of our age are your customers. It’s great\nthat you’re writing for them, but why aren’t you writing about  them?”\nShe asked me what I had in mind, exactly. Because you’re holding it in your\nhands, let me tell you.\nWhen I started my first company in 2005, there was no Techstars. There\nwasn’t a Founder Institute. Y Combinator was some crazy angel investor in Bos-\nton offering  companies $50K a pop if they shacked up with him, as I understood\nit.5 It was an era when few venture capitalists were blogging and no VCs were\nstarring in reality TV shows. The bubble had come and gone, and while tech\nstartups weren’t pariah territory, they weren’t the subject of major motion pic-\ntures yet either. I had only one solution to learning how business gets done in\nthe world of startups, and it involved coffee.  A lot of it. But I had a secret genetic\nsuperweapon.\nBefore being scolded into a corner by the U.S. Food and Drug Administra-\ntion, the good folks at the company 23andMe  sequenced my genome. For $99, it\nwas a great deal and I learned all kinds of trivia about myself. One thing I learned\nis that on chromosome 15, gene CYP1A2, position 75041917, where most folks\nhave a pair of cytosine molecules, I have two adenines. A mutation. Dr. M.C. Cor-\nnelis did some research on this6 and discovered that people with this particular\nmutation are fast caffeine  metabolizers—our bodies dispose of the stimulant\nmore effectively  and more rapidly than those of normal drinkers. Unlike my non-\nmutated counterparts, consuming caffeine  does not elevate my risk of heart\nattacks.\nThat probably explains why I didn’t keel over when, in the process of found-\ning my first company, I went from coffee  teetotaler to an average of five or more\ncoffee  meetings a day.Preface | xiii\nwww.it-ebooks.info\n7Since our conversation, two excellent books have come out: Startup CEO  by Matt Blumberg (Wiley, 2013)\nand The Hard Thing About Hard Things  by Ben Horowitz (HarperBusiness, 2014). Both books focus on\nthe later stages of growing and scaling a company, though; I’ve never been able to find  a resource for the\nCEO coping with the early stages of startup life.The coffee  meeting became the single most important resource for under-\nstanding how startups worked. Fundraising strategy? Financing terms? Investor\nreputations? Closing? I learned everything one cup at a time as I trundled around\nthe city, seeking knowledge from anyone who would give me the time: angel\ninvestors, venture capitalists, experienced entrepreneurs, and first-timers  like\nmyself.\nAs time passed, I added after-work drinks to my repertoire of beverage-\nfueled learning. I found that, after a few beers, a smashing startup success story\nwould often turn out to be more smash than success. The stories of perseverance\npaying off were joined by stories of entrepreneurs persevering all the way to fore-\nclosure and bankruptcy.\nIt took me far, far too long to learn how startups really work. I cannot thank\nenough those people who made the time to educate me—both those who partici-\npated in and supported my company, and those who turned me away because I\nwasn’t ready.\nBut I can pay it forward. I’ve listened to scores of startup CEOs talk about\ntheir jobs. They each describe it differently,  but one word comes up time and\nagain: lonely.\nStartup CEOs are strange creatures. They need to project a continual aura of\nsuccess and confidence.  But this puts a wall up. The truths of the position—mas-\nsive uncertainty, dramatic failures, crushing doubts, terrible guilt—are bottled up\ninside. At best, they’re whispered in late-night venting sessions between collea-\ngues with long-established confidences.  The skills necessary for success aren’t\ntalked about in polite company, because to ask the question would show weak-\nness.\nAnd the stories. The stories!\nStartup CEOs have one of the most unique vantage points on the industry.\nThey are the surfers on the tidal wave: doing their best to look like they are mas-\ntering the elements, when in fact they’re being swept along at the edge of\ndisaster.\nHow has it come to be, then, that no one has written the book on startup\nCEOs?7xiv | Preface\nwww.it-ebooks.info\nThat is what I asked Laurie, standing there with our steaming cups of coffee\non the lawn at FOO camp. And she stared at me for a moment and said... “Well,\nyou should do that.”\nIt took some persuading, but lo and behold, here we are.\nBut this book isn’t for startup CEOs. Well, that’s not strictly true—it certainly\nhas no shortage of advice or opinions. If you are running your own company,\nyou’ll have plenty of moments of shaking your fist at the book calling me an\nidiot, or nodding sympathetically as you see your own failures and successes\nreflected  in the stories told here.\nBut this is not an instruction manual. It’s not a how-to or a bible of any sort.\nQuite simply, there are too many ways to be a good startup CEO to be able to\nclaim any one way is the universal truth.\nInstead, this is a guidebook. It shares the directions, trials, tribulations, and\nstrategies of the startup CEO. It’s about the challenges she faces. It’s about the\nwar stories she knows. It’s about the ways startups are born, live, and die—as\ntold from the view at the top.\nI’ve divided it into five parts, reflecting  the life cycle of a company. Part I  is\nFounding: the time when you’re not quite sure who or what the company is.\nEverything is uncertain, data is thin to nonexistent, and the limitless possibilities\nare both enthralling and terrifying. This covers the period from the twinkle in\nyour eye up until the incorporation and financing.\nPart II  is Funding: one of the earliest and most difficult  challenges for a\nstartup CEO to surmount. Raising money is, much more often than not, a recur-\nring nightmare that ends in failure, with only a small percentage of startups ever\nbringing in outside capital. There are many strategies, and we’ll explore them in\ndepth.\nPart III  is Leadership: the big picture. What the job really means. How to\ncome up with a strategy. Pitfalls you’ll encounter as you build your executive\nteam. How to create a culture.\nPart IV  is Management: the nuts and bolts of the job. How to argue with\nyour team. When to play the CEO card(s). The downside of “big company” career\nsuccesses. How to manage your board.\nAnd finally,  Part V  is Endgame: a win or a loss; how to finish  your startup\nwith grace and create the best outcome for everyone involved.\nThis book is, in fact, a startup of its own, and it is intended for people who\nlove startups. It is a book for people whose hearts burn for the entrepreneurialPreface | xv\nwww.it-ebooks.info\nspirit. It’s a book for builders, creators, engines of progress: those who will build\nthe future, whether their current job is dreamer, architect, or bricklayer.\nIf you’re a student considering a day job step or a startup leap, this book is\nfor you.\nIf you’re slaving days and nights at a tiny company with a vision of greatness,\nwondering what your stock options are for and if what you’re doing will amount\nto anything, this book is for you.\nIf you’re a cofounder, watching your longtime friend and colleague trying to\nact like an old hand at the chief executive role while it’s utterly clear to you that\nchaos is about to engulf the company, this book is for you.\nAnd if you dream of someday sitting in the hot seat yourself, this book is\ndefinitely  for you.\nI hope this book leaves you a little more appreciative of, amused by, and pre-\npared for the role of startup CEO. But don’t kid yourself—no matter what you\nread or do, the first time you actually sit in the hot seat, you’re going to be faking\nit like the rest of us.\nGoodies\nThis book is everything I’ve learned to date about startups, CEOs, and entrepre-\nneurship. Inevitably, that means it’s out of date already. The scope of this book focuses on topics such as problem-solving, systems thinking, innovation, and the role of design in product design and services. This book is unique as it brings together “stories” from both academics’ and practitioners’ perspectives, enabling readers to view design thinking from many different perspectives that can be ap -\nplied in every-day life situations or for organizations when developing plans and policies. \nThis book would be essential reading for design engineers, industrial designers, \nand mechanical engineers who have interest in design thinking. \n\nDesign Thinking \nTheory and Practice \nEdited by\n Eujin Pei and Kurt Becker \n \n Designed cover image: Adobe Stock © \nFirst edition published 202 \nby CRC Press \n2385 NW Executive Center Drive, Suite 320, Boca Raton FL 33431 \nand by CRC Press \n4 Park Square, Milton Park, Abingdon, Oxon, OX14 4RN \nCRC Press is an imprint of Taylor & Francis Group, LLC \n© 202 selection and editorial matter, Eujin Pei and Kurt Becker; individual chapters, the contributors \nReasonable efforts have been made to publish reliable data and information, but the author and pub-\nlisher cannot assume responsibility for the validity of all materials or the consequences of their use. \nThe authors and publishers have attempted to trace the copyright holders of all material reproduced \nin this publication and apologize to copyright holders if permission to publish in this form has not \nbeen obtained. This book is dedicated to our wives, Ying and \nMariam, whose dreams we share and whose unwavering \nsupport that has made this journey of writing possible. \n\nvii \n \n \n \n \n \n  \n  \n \n  \n \n \n \n \n  Contents \nForeword .............................................................................................................. ix \nPreface ................................................................................................................ xiii \nAbout the Editors ................................................................................................ xv \nList of Contributors ........................................................................................... xvii \nAcknowledgments .............................................................................................. xix \nChapter 1 Introduction to Design Thinking ..................................................... 1 \nKurt Becker \nChapter 2 Pursuing an Innovation ................................................................. 15 \nSixto Cabrera \nChapter 3 Designing Ideas : A Dual Perspective ............................................ 27 \nJana Huck and Alexandre Sukhov \nChapter 4 Proposing a Design Thinking Framework to Develop \nCircular Products ........................................................................... 41 \nJ. A Case Study of Interdisciplinary Research on Aging ............................ 189 \nP.J. White, Audrey Patocs, Marla Beauchamp, and Parminder Raina \nIndex ................................................................................................................. 213 \nix \nForeword \nJohn Gero \nUniversity of North Carolina at Charlotte \nDesigning is a foundational activity of humans. It is the way humans intention -\nally change the world around them with the goal of making it better for their \npurposes. Until the 20th century, designing was restricted to large-scale products such as machines, ships, civil structures, and buildings. However, the develop -\nment of consumer goods and then software applications has changed the land -\nscape of design and noticeably broadened the scope of design. Designing has moved beyond its historical roles to encompass the design of organizations, and social and governmental systems. The range of design has spawned the creative industries, a catch-all phrase that encompasses designing well beyond its tradi -\ntional areas. The creative industries contribute around $1 trillion a year to the global economy. Designing is one of the foundations of the creative industries. Designing has become a signifcant activity in both the national and global econ-omy. As a consequence, it plays an important role in economic and social wealth generation. What is surprising is that given the ubiquity of designs and its contin -\nuous history from the earliest days of civilization (the Epic of Gilgamesh , written \naround 4,000 years ago, mentions designing), how recently designing has become an area of research. Designing had been learned through the master-apprentice model for generations. Formal research into designing began in earnest only post World War II, in the 1950s, although there were earlier individual efforts. This research has as its goal to produce a body of knowledge that would lead to the understanding of designing as a set of activities that could be formalized, taught, and potentially improved. The teaching required that there be knowledge to teach. \nThe knowledge of designing could be obtained through induction from obser -\nvations of designers in action. Or this knowledge could be generated through executing experiments that elucidated different aspects of designing. These two streams of knowledge generation intertwine with each feeding off the other. Experiments produced models of designing based on either behaviors of design -\ners or concepts from cognitive science, i.e., how the mind behaves. Both of them have been labeled “design thinking” and this has caused confusion as to which is being referred to. So, design thinking can be what designers do or it can be what happens in the minds of designers, which results in what designers do. The reader of this volume will fnd examples of both streams. \nWhat both these streams agree upon is that designing is a set of processes, \nwhere the processes can be individually delineated. The confuence of these \n \n \n \n   \n x Foreword \nprocesses produces designing and it is this set of processes that distinguishes \ndesigning from other human activities. \nThis current design thinking model of designing, derived from design prac -\ntice, distinguishes itself from earlier models through the inclusion of the user in the process under the label of “empathy”. This notion of empathy, imagining the world from multiple perspectives, was missing as a signifcant factor from earlier models of design thinking, such as Asimov’s 1962  model of engineering \ndesign  (Asimov, 1962 ) and in Pahl and Beitz’s 1984 model of engineering design \n(Pahl & Beitz, 1984). The highly infuential book by Herb Simon, The Sciences \nof the Artifcial ( Simon, 1969 ) also does not address this notion of empathy in its \ndescription of designing. It was an article in the Harvard Business Review  by Tim \nBrown, CEO of IDEO ( Brown, 2008 ), that initiated the widespread interest in \ndesign thinking. The article was expanded into a highly infuential book ( Brown, \n2009 ). Brown distilled what he had learned from his own design practice to pro -\nduce a near-prescriptive model of design thinking and framed it within a business perspective that elevated designing from an activity associated with producing products to one generating value for an organization. \nThe other stream, exemplifed by Lawson ( Lawson, 2005 ) and Cross ( Cross \n2023), focuses on the cognition of designing within a process model. The two \nstreams complement each other. \nThis volume brings together 12 contributions on design thinking that span \nboth streams and contribute to the literature in unique ways. In the introductory chapter, Becker outlines the foundational activities that go to make up design thinking. In Chapter 2 , Cabrera focuses on the way design thinking contributes to \ninnovation within the social context. In Chapter 3 , Huck and Sukhov dive more \ndeeply into the role of ideas and their sharing in design development. In Chapter \n4, Mesa and Ruiz-Paster, in contrast to Chapter 3 , expand the scope of designing \nto cover the circular economy within a design thinking framework. In Chapter 5 , \nMorcos brings the concept of the empathy aspect of design thinking to the start -\nup culture. In Chapter 6 , Mortati, Mariani and Rizzo bring design thinking to \nbear when developing public services, exemplifed by projects in the European Union. In Chapter 7 , Kelly and Grace pursue design thinking from a more \ncognitive aspect when considering a framework for the role of human design -\ners in an AI-human hybrid intelligence. In Chapter 8 , Genert, Stenftenagel and \nBlandon take a more traditional systems-subsystems view of design thinking as they develop a Multidisciplinary Design and Optimization model of their design -\ning activity. This model generally excludes direct human aspects. In Chapter 9 , \nKannengiesser and Gero look at the empirical data that supports the human cog -\nnitive behavior of the dual process modes of thinking when designing. In Chapter \n10, Hahri, Lubis and Pei look at Co-Design from a design thinking perspective. \nIn Chapter 11 , Palmås pursues an example of one of the processes that makes up \ndesign thinking, namely, the creation of ideas using metaphors and its place in design education. In Chapter 12 , White, Patocs, Beauchamp and Raina, fttingly \nas the fnal chapter of this volume, present results from a large scale seven-year study of using design thinking. \n \n \n \n \n xi Foreword \nThis volume brings material not normally found together in a single place. It \nbrings design thinking from a cognitive perspective along with design thinking \nfrom the empathy perspective. This shows both the breadth and depth of the two streams of design thinking and how they complement each other. Recent research on the brains of designers as they design has shown that both streams provide frameworks for what happens in the brain ( Gero & Milovanovic, 2023 ). \nREFERENCES \nAsimov, M.  (1962). Introduction to Design . Prentice-Hall. \nBrown, T.  (2008). Design Thinking . Harvard Business Review. \nBrown, T.  (2009). Change by Design: How Design Thinking Transforms Organizations \nand Inspires Innovation . HarperBusiness. \nCross, N.  (2023). Design Thinking: Understanding How Designers Think and Work , 2nd \nEdn. Bloomsbury VA. \nGero, J. S. & Milovanovic, J.  (2023). Design thinking and design neurocognition, in K. \nStraker & C. Wrigley (eds), Research Handbook on Design Thinking . Edward Elgar, \npp. 7–24. https://doi.org/10.4337/9781802203134 \nLawson, B. (2005). How Designers Think , 4th Edn. Routledge. \nPahl, G. & Beitz, W. (1984). Engineering Design: A Systematic Approach . Springer. \nSimon, H. A. (1969). Sciences of the Artifcial . MIT Press. \n\nxiii \n Preface \nDesign Thinking: Theory and Practice is more than a guide. It is a call to action \nthat invites designers, engineers, policy-makers, educators, and innovators to rethink their approaches and embrace a mindset that prioritizes adaptability, empathy, and collaboration. To be successful in today’s highly technological and globally competitive world, one requires the use of a different set of skills. This book seeks to empower readers to leverage design thinking not only as a tool for innovation but also as a means to create a better and more sustainable world. Through case studies, theoretical frameworks, and empirical research, it chal -\nlenges readers to consider whether and how design thinking can sustain long-term relevance in evolving contexts. As the chapters unfold, readers are invited to jour -\nney through the diverse dimensions of design thinking. From understanding its foundational elements to witnessing its integration with modern disciplines such as using artifcial intelligence, product design, and circular economy principles, the narrative of this book highlights the versatility of this approach. The book offers insights into how design thinking can drive change across industries and domains. In essence, this book presents a comprehensive exploration of design thinking principles, methodologies, and applications, extending from the design of everyday products to strategies for addressing global challenges. \n\nxv \nAbout the Editors \nEujin Pei began his career as a product designer, with his work exhibited at the \nCooper Hewitt Smithsonian Design Museum in New York. He now works as a Full Professor and serves as Associate Dean for the College of Engineering, Design and Physical Sciences at Brunel University of London, UK. Since 2015, Eujin has been the Director of the BSc Product Design Engineering course, where he leads its development and ensures graduates are equipped with the skills to excel in a rapidly evolving industry. Before this, he served as Director for Postgraduate Research, where he played a key role in maintaining academic standards and promoting excellence among PhD students. Eujin is a Fellow of the Institution of Engineering Designers (FIED), and his professional qualifcations include being a Chartered Engineer (CEng), Chartered Environmentalist (CEnv), and Chartered Technological Product Designer (CTPD). His work refects a strong commitment to innovation, sustainability, and the advancement of technology in design and engineering. \nKurt Becker  spent his career as an engineering and technology educator and \nhas extensive international experience working on engineering education and \ntechnical training projects funded by the Asian Development Bank, World Bank, and U.S. Agency for International Development (USAID). Countries where he has worked include Armenia, Bangladesh, Bulgaria, China, Egypt, Indonesia, Macedonia, Poland, Romania, and Thailand. Kurt is Professor Emeritus in the College of Engineering at Utah State University (USU) where he spent 30 years working in the discipline of Engineering Education. He was Department Head and Founder of the Department of Engineering Education at USU. His research in engineering education spans more than three decades, and his work preparing PhD students to be the next generation of leaders and researchers demonstrates his commitment to the discipline. While in the College of Engineering at USU he stated the National Academy of Engineering endorsed, “Grand Challenges Scholars Program”. This program provides undergraduate engineering students with curricular and co-curricular activities while being mentored by engineering faculty. Kurt’s work in engineering education and design thinking is a tribute to his dedication to the feld of engineering and technology. \n\nxvii \nList of Contributors \nMarla Beauchamp \nMcMaster University, Canada \nKurt Becker \nUtah State University, USA \nNick Blanton \nLockheed Martin Missiles and Fire \nControl, USA \nSixto Cabrera Hardware Engineer, USA \nJaime Albero Mesa Cogollo \nUniversidad del Norte, Colombia \nGlenn Gebert \nLockheed Martin Missiles and Fire \nControl, USA \nJohn S. White \nSouth East Technological University, \nIreland \n\nxix \nAcknowledgments \nThis book is the brainchild of a chance meeting between two engineering educa -\ntors from different continents who care deeply about engineering and how design \nthinking is critical to the engineering discipline. This book would not have been possible without the invaluable contributions of authors and co-authors for sharing their knowledge so generously, and whose expertise and dedication have enriched its pages. A special thanks to Shatakshi Singh and Katya Seward from Taylor & Francis for their unwavering guidance and support in bringing this project to fruition. Thanks to our friends and family members also who have supported us along the way. This book is a testament to the collaborative spirit that underpins the very essence of design thinking. \n\n1 \n   \n \n   Introduction to1 Design Thinking \nKurt Becker \nToday, organizations pursue innovation to be successful. Innovation can increase \nthe bottom line, improve quality, or make processes more effcient and produc -\ntive ( Magistretti et al., 2021 ). Apple Inc. is a good example, as it has had a core \nvalue of innovation dating back to its formation, and the result is an array of products setting it apart from its competitors. Innovative goods and services are now synonymous with superior quality. The more an individual or organization demonstrates a fuller understanding of innovation, the greater is the propensity to attain innovation ( Kahn, 2018 ). \nThe one constant thing in today’s work environment is change, and the rate \nof change is increasing. The global economy drives change and has become the norm today. Companies and institutions that fail to adapt to change will lose their competitive edge ( Igbal, 2011 ; Çetinkaya et al., 2019 ). An example includes \nResearch in Motion Limited, the original company of BlackBerry Limited that was once a leader in wireless data technology, but it failed to innovate and keep up with the change in the industry. They are now struggling to regain market share. This is just one of many examples of companies that have failed due to lack of innovation. While innovation is often thought to start the design process, the innovative solution is discovered towards the end of the design process. This is because using the design thinking process requires the user to think deeply about the design solution and that the context is well understood. \n1.1 DESIGN THINKING \nTo complement innovation, the concept of design thinking has gained momentum in recent years. In many organizations, it has become a crucial element in the stra-tegic planning and decision-making processes. Design thinking is a methodology for innovation focused on understanding and solving complex customer problems. It emphasizes the importance of empathy for the user, a hands-on approach to problem-solving, and the iteration of solutions that address specifc user needs (Nakata, 2020 ). According to Brown (2008) , design thinking has emerged as a \nhuman-centred approach to innovation based on the ways that designers think and work. By incorporating design thinking into their business strategies, companies can gain a competitive edge by creating products and services that truly resonate \nDOI: 10.1201/9781003487524-1 \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n \n  \n 2 Design Thinking \nwith their target audience ( Nakata & Hwang, 2020 ; Jaskyte & Liedtka, 2022 ). \nDesign thinking is a practical, creative problem-solving method that leads to solu -\ntions with the intent of an improved future result ( Foster, 2021 ). \nDesign thinking is a method often associated with creativity and developing \nnew ideas. It is used in problem-solving and development of solutions for complex \nissues. In early use, design thinking was used in the development of specifca -\ntions for a product or service to defne how the product or service would be used by the customer or client. Over time, it has evolved and become a new discipline (Brown, 2009 ). \nDesign thinking is a process that involves using many different skill sets and \napproaches, and is not just about creating new products or services. It is about defning new ideas, creating new ways of doing things, and implementing inno -\nvative solutions to complex problems ( Auernhammer and Roth, 2021 ). Design \nthinking is a multidisciplinary approach that draws on the skills and knowledge of experts from a range of felds. Working together in a collaborative way, these experts identify underlying patterns of problems and create new and innovative solutions to those problems ( Verganti et al., 2021 ). \nTim Brown, CEO of IDEO, defnes design thinking as a methodology to \nunderstand user demands and requirements, challenge the assumptions that defne issues that are not easily understood, and create innovative solutions by consider -\ning conditions and resources. According to Dell’Era et al. (2020) , design thinking \nis accepted as a formal creative problem-solving method with the intent to foster innovation. This description has been based on how practicing designers claim to approach problems, rather than on how design has been defned in academic literature ( Brown 2008 ; Pande & Bharathi, 2020 ; Cross, 2023 ). \nAt its core, design thinking is a problem-solving approach that is analytical \nand creative and combines logic, intuition, and systemic reasoning to explore the possibilities of what could be and create desired outcomes that beneft the end user (i.e. the customer). According to Kim & Park (2021) , a key aspect of design \nthinking is that it is human-centred; it cares about the people who will utilize what is created and seeks to understand their wants, needs, and experiences. This way, it is possible to understand the extent of the problem and create a deep understanding of the people the solution aims to help. Design thinking also allows for greater collaboration between teams increasing the chance of creat -\ning a successful product or service. This is because, from the outset, it creates a culture that welcomes the sharing of ideas, and it decreases risk as the proposed solutions are well thought out. Figure 1.1  shows designers using the design think -\ning process. \n1.2 ORIGINS AND KEY CONTRIBUTORS \nDesign thinking has evolved since the 1960s, drawing from diverse infuences and approaches, and has paralleled this conceptual development as a framework of methodologies supporting complex problem thinking ( Henriksen, et al., 2017 ). \nAccording to Jamal et al. (2021) , “design thinking is a human-centric, structured, \n \n   3 Introduction to Design Thinking \nFIGURE 1.1 Designers using design thinking. (Google image – purchased) – web site \nhttps://stock.adobe.com/plans . \ncollaborative problem-solving approach that produces innovative products, pro -\ncesses, or experience solutions to address wicked problems” (p. 3). \nThe design thinking process practiced today was created through the com -\nbined efforts of many prominent engineers, economists, and designers over the \nspan of decades. Designers such as Herbert Simon, L.J.K. Smith, and John Chris Jones are identifed as early design thinkers, having theorized about the pro -\ncesses by which designers go about their work ( Thawani, 2023 ). According to \nThawani, they did this intending to describe these processes in such a way that non-designers could understand and potentially apply them to their professional work contexts. Discussions about design thinking processes and practices were fomented by early adopters like Richard Buchanan, Nigel Cross, and Tom Inns. More modern discussions and applications of design thinking have increased exponentially in recent years, and more high-profle stakeholders in contempo -\nrary design thinking include Tim Brown of IDEO, Roger Martin of the Rotman School, and David Kelley of the Stanford d.School ( Muratovski, 2021 ). \n1.3 DESIGN THINKING PROCESS \nDesign thinking utilizes elements like empathy and experimentation to arrive at innovative solutions. This process often involves much collaborative work in which interdisciplinary teams work together to solve problems and arrive at solutions. The design thinking process is best thought of as a system of overlap -\nping spaces rather than a sequence of orderly steps. More specifcally, the design thinking process is a method of generating useful and creative ideas and is also outlined as a cognitive, strategic, and practical process by which design concepts are engineered. There are several variations of the design thinking process in use today, and they have three to seven phases. However, all variations of the design thinking process are similar and consist of three main phases: Defne, Design, and Test ( Brown, 2008 ; Knight et al., 2020 ). \n  \n \n \n \n \n \n  4 Design Thinking \nFIGURE 1.2 Double diamond design thinking model. (Google stock photo) \nA commonly used version of design thinking consists of six chronological \nsteps: empathize , defne , ideate , prototype , test, and implement . Designers look -\ning for solutions to a problem, using this process, frst understand it better (empa -\nthize) and then use the understanding to get a clearer problem statement (defne). \nThe outcome is a never-ending process of problem-solving through creating and evaluating. \nToday, many different models and schools of thought exist for design think -\ning. Whether it is d.school at Stanford University, IDEO U, MIT Sloan, or any of the many other schools, the design thinking process consists of multiple phases. These range from the four-phase double diamond model (see Figure 1.2 ) to the \nfve-phase design thinking model (see Figure 1.3 ), to the six-phase design think -\ning model (see Figure 1.4 ). \nAlthough there is no single model of the design thinking process, for this chap -\nter, it can be explained by a fve-phase model. The following section discusses each phase of the design thinking process. \n1.3.1 PHASE 1. E MPATHIZE : UNDERSTANDING  USER NEEDS \nThe frst phase of the design thinking process is to empathize. During this frst step, the designer should empathize with the person who will use the product. This involves observation, engaging with, and learning from the person in his or her environment. This phase starts with observing and interviewing the end user about the problem or product needs. This phase constructs a situation map of the user’s experience. After understanding the real problem or project needs, get an agree -\nment with the user to make sure this is a real problem. This enables the designer to identify the needs to solve the user’s problem ( de Kervenoael et al., 2020 ). \n \n 5 Introduction to Design Thinking \nFIGURE 1.3 Five-phase process of design thinking. \nFIGURE 1.4 Six-phase process of design thinking. (Google stock photo) \n  \n  \n    6 Design Thinking \n1.3.2 PHASE 2. D EFINE: FRAMING THE  PROBLEM \nThe problem with much of design today is that it is poorly understood, undefned, \nor confusing. A clear statement on what the problem is provides more informa -\ntion than numerous vague statements. The “defne” stage is crucial to the rest of the process. This is where you, as a designer, can understand and articulate the problem. This may involve several sessions, with the design team present, to brainstorm on what has been learned in the empathi')

Apart from the exceptions specifically \nforeseen by law, no part of this publication may be copied, \nstored in a computerised file or publicly circulated, by means \nof printing, copying, microfilming or any other medium, without \nthe express written permission of the publisher. \nUITGEVERIJ LANNOOCAMPUS \nERASME RUELENSVEST 179 BUS 101 \n3001 LEUVEN \nBELGIUM \nWWwW.LANNOOCAMPUS.BE \n\nForeword \nThe clarity of COSTA \nBusinesses have been through some turbulent times since the 1990s. There’s \nhardly a single large or medium-sized enterprise that has avoided the need to \nrestructure, drastically alter its strategy or change course. Countless small busi- \nnesses have even been affected too. The driving force has come both from the out- \nside world — the “new consumer” and tighter environmental rules — and from new \ncompetitors, whose emergence has forced companies to innovate and change. \nFor many businesses, plotting a new course is easier said than done. Change di- \nrection by all means, but toward what destination? And how will that impact cur- \nrent operations? Managers, CEOs and even shareholders look to specialist change \nmanagers to point them in the right direction. \nYet the message doesn’t always get through: the experts seem to contradict each \nother. And it’s obvious from the plethora of models and methods that change is ex- \ntremely difficult and the results far from certain. Recognition of that fact —- which \nwas also affecting us as consultants — prompted us to investigate why consultancy \nin the field of change turns out so often to be a source of confusion. \nIn the course of that research, we discovered something that surprised us. It’s \ntrue that there are many flaws in the way consultants talk about business archi- \ntecture, and that these often undermine the message they’re trying to get across. \nBut there’s also a way to make it all easier: to express more clearly — not only for \nconsultants, but for managers and CEOs too — how a business functions, and how \nto detect problems and challenges faster and address them more efficiently. We’ve \ncombined those insights in a model we call “COSTA”. \nThe key thing the COSTA Model wants to teach you is that you can manage your \ncompany with concepts. That in itself isn’t new: everyone uses concepts when \ninitiating business change. It’s just that up to now, no one has seen the common \nthread linking them all. That’s the first immediate benefit of the COSTA Model: it \nprovides an overall concept, within which a business can be examined and under- \nstood in its entirety. Better still, the COSTA Model enables you to define your busi- \nness concepts properly. That’s actually a real milestone, because businesses and \n\n6 A COMPLETE AND CONSISTENT BUSINESS \na \nconsultants have so far tended to use concepts that vary according to the problems \nthey are addressing. Those concepts often turned out to be contradictory or else \nfailed to offer useful guidance, as they were unable to deal with all the business’s \nproblems, despite the fact that those problems were frequently interrelated. \nDifficulties also existed where there were no problems as such, and the aim was \nsimply to gain a clearer overview of business operations. The terminology used \nwasn’t understood everywhere in the same way; and the representation of pro- \ncesses or relationships varied internally from one department to another, with \nthe result that managers risked misunderstanding each other when discussing \nchange or reorganization. \nThe COSTA Model provides a solution to all these problems. It integrates exist- \ning concepts, introduces new ones and combines them all in a coherent whole. \nConsequently, you'll find ideas in this book that are familiar, but which the COSTA \nModel strips down to their essence. And there are new terms, concepts and defini- \ntions as well. \nSomething else we’ve tried to do with the COSTA Model is to highlight previously \ninvisible connections, so that more effective solutions can be thought up for the \nchallenges that businesses face. COSTA is a comprehensive architecture model \nand is therefore able to approach “businesses” and “business operations” in their \nentirety. \nOnly when you apply the COSTA Model will you truly understand why certain \nchange processes you initiated in the past didn’t work, or at least failed to deliver \nwhat you were expecting. At the same time, COSTA allows you to launch future \narchitecture processes based on our model that do work and yield the desired \nresults. \nWe didn’t just dream up the COSTA Model: it’s the fruit of a process in which we \nhave been engaged as consultants for some years. Not in some ivory tower, but on \nthe ground. The model is underpinned by conceptual ideas that have been tested \nin practice. \nThe COSTA Model creates clarity: more so, possibly, than I’ve achieved in this \nforeword. I therefore suggest you have a quick browse through the first part of \nthis book, in which we introduce the COSTA Model, to get to know the concepts \nbehind it. COSTA is a toolbox you can use to design dozens of different business \n. \n\nFOREWORD 7 \narchitectures. At the end of the day, though, they’re still just designs: it’s up to you \nto flesh them out substantively and creatively. \nKoen Knaepen \n\nDigitized by the Internet Archive \nin 2023 with funding from \nNo Sponsor \nhttps://archive.org/details/completeconsiste0000knae \n\nContents \nForeword The clarity of COSTA 5 \nPART I. Business ‘ Business | \\ \nArchitecture | ot \n| Undertake || Abstraction | \ni ce i — | \n+ | | | Organize | | Concept | \n(eo eae Added Value | | Reality | \n\\ Behe oe \nLs y \nFigure 1.1 What is a business? \nWhat is a business? \nBefore we present the COSTA Model, it would be useful to pause for a moment to \nconsider the world to which the model applies. COSTA is an architecture model \nthat makes it easier for you to lead and organize your business. But what is a busi- \nness? And what do we mean in this context by “architecture”? This first chapter \nwill begin by defining a number of concepts and ideas. \nTo discover precisely what business architecture means, you first need a clear un- \nderstanding of exactly what constitutes a business. There’s no need to get bogged \ndown in a philosophical debate, though: what we’re interested in is a rational defi- \nnition of what you and I mean by the term “business”. \nWhat a business is (or seeks to be) is often set down these days in a “mission state- \nment” drawn up by enterprises themselves. The mission is an ideal translation of \nthe goals, resources and commitments a business has defined for itself. Mission \n\n14 A COMPLETE AND CONSISTENT BUSINESS \n- \nstatements often seem vague, precisely because they attempt to express an ideal \nsituation. They nevertheless have some relevance, and so provide managers, em- \nployees, customers and stakeholders with a certain frame of reference. \nTake NOKIA’s mission statement: Nokia is a major player in the field of mobile \ntelephony hardware, so it comes as little surprise that their mission begins with \nthe words “Connecting People”. Nokia’s mission statement continues: \n“Our goal is to build great mobile products that enable billions of people world- \nwide to enjoy more of what life has to offer. Our challenge is to achieve this in an \nincreasingly dynamic and competitive environment.” \nThose are quite some promises and commitments! What Nokia does specifically \nis build mobile and smartphones that enable people to communicate. But is it \nthe only business to do so? If you do a quick web search for the term “connecting \npeople”, you'll find quite a few companies make a similar claim, even ones active \nin different fields. A dating agency can also plausibly claim to connect people. \nPhone companies too. Even something as general as “sport” claims to bring peo- \nple together. \nWhat’s striking about all mission statements is that certain elements recur all the \ntime: an ambitious goal is formulated and reference is made to the customer. The \nsportswear manufacturer Nike promises “to bring inspiration and innovation to ev- \nery athlete in the World”, while Microsoft declares that “We work to help people and \nbusinesses throughout the world realize their full potential”. \nA mission statement is one thing, but it doesn’t turn a business into... a business. \nThat takes something else. You still need to translate your mission into activities, \nwhich in turn requires you to do two things. \nFirst of all you have to undertake something to realize the “planned” activities in \nreality, and at the same time you have to organize your business. Undertaking and \norganizing are two core tasks of every venture. You have to perform those tasks in \norder to achieve a result. That result is then the added value that it creates for the \nworld around it or its users. \nCreating added value is a third requirement for us to be able to speak of a “busi- \nness”. What’s the added value of an iPad? On the one hand, it’s flexible Internet \naccess, wherever you are. But on the other, it’s also the image of yourself you create \nthrough ownership and use of your tablet. Added value can assume strange forms. \n\nTHE “FLOOR PLAN” OF A BUSINESS ARCHITECTURE 15 \nAnd it can even be entirely irrelevant. A business might set itself the goal of selling \nrefrigerators to Inuit homes. But will it get very far? \nAdded value can be totally different even for a comparable enterprise. SN Brussels \nand Ryan Air are both airlines, but their added value could hardly be more differ- \nent. The first aims to offer high-quality travel, while the second focuses chiefly on \ncheap journeys. Yet both enterprises seek to profit from the added value they offer. \nThey do so with the resources they have: resources that are frequently scarce. \nWe now have all the elements we need to come up with a conclusive definition of \nprecisely what a business is: \nA business is an organization that undertakes an activity to transform an idea us- \ning scarce resources into added value. \nEnterprise versus organization \nThat definition refers again to the two elements “enterprise” (undertaking) and \n“organization”. In practice, the two terms are used interchangeably to refer to a \nbusiness. However, for the purposes of this book on business architecture, we \nneed to give the two terms back their original meaning. Because enterprise and \norganization are actually two different things. Let us explain, beginning with the \nconcept of “enterprise”. \nAn enterprise is a (long-term) project with a goal and a certain degree of risk. \nActivities are performed regularly or systematically within the enterprise that re- \nquire a certain effort or investment and which bring that goal closer to achieve- \nment. \nIn short, an enterprise is an action or combination of actions. An organization is \nsomething very different: \nAn organization is a structure of arrangements between people who collaborate \nwith one another. They do this within the scope of activities for which the organi- \nzation has been established. These arrangements can relate to all aspects neces- \nsary to the performance of the activities. \n\n16 A COMPLETE AND CONSISTENT BUSINESS \ns \nThe two definitions or descriptions clarify the distinction. One way of putting it \nis to say that the enterprise needs the organization to achieve its activities. The \ntwo are therefore inextricably linked. You might retort that it’s entirely possible to \nhave an enterprise without an organization. But the likelihood of that enterprise \nsucceeding would be considerably reduced, as would any return it might generate. \n\na What is business architecture? \n( Business Business \nArchitecture | \n| \n| ies WEES Teri iT - — / S \n| | co oa P \n| | Undertake || t Abstraction \n|}; Organize || Concept \naaa aa 3 \nBB i-xacahee |_| Added Value | \nFigure 2.1 What is business architecture? \nHaving briefly described what a business is, we can now turn to the concept of \n“business architecture”. The first way to clarify this is through comparison with \nconventional architecture. Numerous parallels can be made between building ar- \nchitects and business architects. \nArchitecture is all about the design of buildings: it refers to building styles, con- \nstruction techniques and even to the finished structure itself. But it’s also a way of \nachieving a concrete reality based on abstract plans and calculations. Conventional \narchitects basically develop an abstract idea from which real buildings are then \nconstructed. \nBusiness architects essentially do the same thing. They too draw up a plan of how \na business could look in reality. The only difference is that their plans are a little \nmore complex than those for a building, because they have to draw both the enter- \nprise and the organization. That can be a pretty tough brief. \nWe further explore the parallel with building architecture below, to achieve a better \nunderstanding of business architecture. We do so based on two questions: \n1. What do architects do? What do they deliver? \n2. How do they do it? What process do they follow? \n\n18 A COMPLETE AND CONSISTENT BUSINESS \nWhat do architects do? \nConventional architects will, at the very least, draw up the plan of a home some- \none wants to build. This plan presents the general outlines. It reflects the concept \nof the future house. A plan like this serves a number of purposes. To begin with, \narchitects use it to show their clients how their dream home will look in real- \nity. They do so by drawing a realistic representation (albeit still schematic) of the \nhome. It is now clear to the clients what form their home will take, but also how \nthey will live in it. \nThe architect’s plan is also a means of communicating with other parties. Clients \nuse it to get a loan from their bank. And possibly to persuade wavering family \nmembers. They show off the plans to friends and use them to inform other inter- \nested parties. \nLastly, the architect’s plan helps the contractor draw up a quotation and carry out \nthe works. A plan by itself, however, is obviously not enough for all that. Contrac- \ntors also need specifications before they can start actual building work. All the \nsame, the plans tell builders what they need to take into account. \nBusiness architects likewise draw up a future “house” for the business, once again \nusing broad outlines. They set down the essentials of the organization and enter- \nprise, following which, the business’s employees can get down to work. \nA business architecture is a collection of concepts used within a business. They \nare presented in such a way that the various interested parties can make use of \nthem. Like the plan of a house, that of a business architecture is a useful means of \ncommunication for various purposes and target groups. It’s helpful to both inter- \nnal and external stakeholders: management, employees, but also the authorities, \nshareholders and sometimes even customers. \nWhat process do architects follow? \nHow do conventional architects arrive at the plans for a house? There’s obviously \nan entire process behind it. It certainly isn’t just a matter of sitting down at the \ndrawing board or CAD-CAM computer and starting to draw. Architects are ex- \n\nWHAT IS BUSINESS ARCHITECTURE? 19 \npected to translate needs into feasible plans. To do that, they have to take account \nof two important questions: what does the client want, and what can he or she \nafford? They have to combine the client’s needs with limiting conditions such as \nbudget, available space and zoning regulations. And they also have to deal with \nquestions concerning the technical possibilities and materials. Can different tech- \nniques — perfectly good in themselves — be reconciled within the same plan? Ar- \nchitects therefore have to align a complex range of interacting factors if they are to \ncome up with a feasible design. \nWhat’s more, they might have to deal with more than one client at a time: the hus- \nband, for instance, might have different expectations to the wife or the children. \nThat’s part of the architect’s task too: how to reconcile the needs of the different \nfuture users? And will those needs stay the same over time? Children leave home, \nparents get older. So perhaps the client’s needs will also change over the years. \nCan that be taken into account during the actual design phase? \nArchitects integrate the answers to all these questions in their final plan, and pres- \nent them in visual form. You'll find the same idea in business architecture. Busi- \nness architects likewise seek an answer to the same two basic questions: what \nare the needs, and what are the possibilities? In this case too, these seemingly \nstraightforward questions trigger a whole series of secondary ones. Business ar- \nchitects therefore have to base themselves on an analysis of their clients’ needs, \nwhile also possessing an in-depth knowledge of what’s possible. \nThey can learn a great deal from conventional architecture, but they also have to \ncontend with one major handicap: unlike their counterparts in the construction \nsector, business architects have few models or standards on which to draw. Thanks \nto the models and standards that exist within house-building, you can look at any \nset of construction plans and interpret them correctly. There’s no difference, for \ninstance, in the way they indicate a door, plumbing or stairs. Standards of this \nkind haven’t yet been defined in business architecture. That complicates the job of \nbusiness architects, who have to devote a lot of their time to developing and intro- \nducing the standards needed to describe concepts within your business or sector. \nThe COSTA Model you will discover in this book is an attempt at standardiza- \ntion that will be useful across the field. It names and categorizes every possible \naspect of business operations, enabling business architects to make their meaning \nclearer. A “door”, as it were, will always be represented in the same way. \n\n20 A COMPLETE AND CONSISTENT BUSINESS \nThe power of a good concept \nBusiness architecture therefore aims to represent the concept of your business in \nthe same way as a house plan does. How does a concept come about? People often \nthink that a concept or idea appears out of nowhere. That’s not entirely true. Think \nabout the time you or one of your acquaintances had a house built. How did you \nagree the final plans with the architect? You probably began with a little reconnais- \nsance. You looked around your neighbourhood or town to get an impression of \nother houses. You visited friends and family, and maybe a show house put up by \na building firm. In each case, the aim was to form a picture of your future home. \nWhen you talked to your architect, you generally didn’t describe specific concepts \n— you illustrated your ideas based on these examples. It wouldn’t have been ter- \nribly helpful if the “plans” for your new home had amounted to no more than a \nscrapbook of the pictures and drawings you collected during your reconnaissance. \nA scrapbook is no use to a contractor. “Combine the roof from house A with the \nstyle of house B and the interior layout of house C.” That may help explain your \nneeds, but it’s not a good way to build a house. Nevertheless, that’s precisely how \nmany businesses approach things. They don’t describe concepts, they give exam- \nples — an approach that will inevitably produce rickety structures. \nTake a look at the diagram below. \nConcept \nExample \nFigure 2.2 Communicating a concept using an example \nThe concepts are shown above the dotted line. What you see below the line are \nthe examples that were used to describe the concept. The result is totally different \n® \n\nWHAT 1S BUSINESS ARCHITECTURE? 21 \nto what was originally intended. How do you get from the original concept to the \nfinal one? The process often goes like this: \n— Person A has a concept in mind. Something “round”, for instance. \n— He “translates” his concept into an eloquent example. That’s what he wants to \ndo. \n— Listener B receives the concept. She interprets it in her own way, basing herself \non Person A’s example. \n— Listener B understands that the concept is something “round”. She communi- \ncates the idea in turn to Designer C, once again using an example. \n— Designer C takes that example and comes up with a concept of his own. Now it \nbecomes something “rectangular”. \nIn this way, the concept communicated by means of examples evolves into some- \nthing very different to what was initially intended. The process described above is \nobviously very simplistic. But anyone with experience of developing and imple- \nmenting projects in businesses knows that this is indeed often how it works. The \namount of wasted time and energy is naturally immense. \nThe problem is that concepts are too rarely described in terms of their essential \nfeatures: people get bogged down in unnecessary detail, with the result that the \nessence gets lost. Architects don’t design the bathroom, the furniture or even the \nbuilding materials in their drawings — they limit themselves to the broad outlines. \nDetails belong to the next stage, that of implementation. \nExample Concept \nFigure 2.3 Communicating a concept using a description \nA simple description of your concept helps you avoid confusion about the essence \nof the idea. There might still be a bit of noise in the communication, but the basic \n\nPaes A COMPLETE AND CONSISTENT BUSINESS \na \nconcept will always come through unscathed. A concept is a representation of the \nreality based on a few key outlines. \n— Person A describes the concept based on its essential features: b/a = 0.59555. \n— Person A can provide an example of this, but it isn’t necessary. Person C uses \nthe same features and understands immediately what is intended. \n— No matter how many intermediaries there are, the same essential features are \npassed on each time. The end result will match the original concept very closely. \nThe analogy with an architect’s plans doesn’t hold up entirely: conventional archi- \ntects ultimately make a plan in which the house is represented as faithfully as pos- \nsible. It becomes a scale model of how the house should ultimately turn out. The \nrelative measurements are indicated as accurately as possible. Other details, in \ncontrast, are represented in a more logical, schematic way: doors, pipes, colours, \nand so forth. \nYou mostly present business concepts schematically. A faithful representation, \nsuch as you find in the plan of a house, would be too restrictive for your company: \nit would leave no room for specific, individual elaboration. We can illustrate this \nwith an example: \nWhat is the essence of banking? You can explain the concept of “banking” in a \nvariety of ways. You could say that “the banker mediates between the investor \nand the borrower”. That’s a description based on the most essential features. In \nbanking practice, however, this can obviously be done in different ways. What we \nmean by “investment” is open to interpretation. In real estate? In risk? In insur- \nance? A description with an example always entails a limitation. \n& Language is a collection of concepts. Words are merely signifiers representing particu- \nlar concepts. The word “chair” evokes a different image for each person, but the same \nconcept. It has four legs, a seat and a back. At the same time, however, the variations \non the “chair” concept are infinite. \nSHHSSHHHHHHEHHHHTHHHOHHHHHHHHHHHHHHESHHHHHHEHHHEHEESHSEHHHTEHHHEHSETETHEEHHOSSHOOOTHTOS ESOS EEE ESTES EOO®E \nA concept like “chair” is easy to grasp. It refers to something concrete, which \nanyone who speaks the language can picture. We therefore refer to it as a con- \ncrete verbal signifier. But there are also conceptual verbal signifiers. In that case, \na concrete representation is much harder, if not impossible, to achieve. It is a \nconvention that we agree and that we assume that everyone understands in the . \n\nLogical \nReality based 23 \nLogical Model Conceptual Model \nE.g. Underground railway \n= description of what we want \nthrough its essential \ncharacteristics \nPhysical Model Functional Model \nE.g. Building plan Eg. User manual \nExhaustive Essential \n\n24 A COMPLETE AND CONSISTENT BUSINESS \nit serves its purpose, which is to provide an insight into the organization of \nthe business. \nSchematic charts always need a key, which you have to know before you can \ninterpret the map of the underground. You have to understand, for instance, \nthat the red line refers to a particular train number or route. In the same way, \nyour business architecture is the key to the chart of your business, and for the \nenterprise and organization behind it. \nsame way. “Border” is a conceptual verbal signifier: it’s something that only people \nunderstand. Animals know nothing of national borders, unless they are prevented \nfrom crossing one because of a physical barrier such as a fence or barbed wire. To \nhuman beings, the border between Belgium and the Netherlands is something \nrecognizable (although no one knows they’ve crossed the border unless its precise \nlocation is physically indicated somehow). \nThe business world revolves almost exclusively around conceptual verbal signifi- \ners. Which is precisely why it is so difficult to give them the same meaning for ev- \neryone. It’s almost impossible without examples. So you end up investing consid- \n7 7” 6 \nerable time in defining concepts like “customer”, “segment”, “competence” and so \non. It takes a lot of time and eats up energy to come up with an adequate explana- \ntion. You might get no further than presenting them based on examples, which is \ntheir immediate weakness. Because, as you have read, examples frequently serve \nto distort the originally intended concept. \n\n=» Introduction to COSTA \n‘fe \\ \n5 § an ee ea tan ee ee a ee oe, Se ee eo ae Oo aQ Oo oO (an) or \n| What / >. \nHw | COSTAModel | \nw")
