See the Terms and Conditions (https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/terms-and-conditions) on Wiley Online Library for rules of use; OA articles are governed by the applicable Creative Commons License\n\nixForeword\nYour first year as CEO is arguably the most decisive \nperiod in your entire CEO journey. It is when you \nmake your mark as a leader—  signaling to your \norganization and the market that you were not only the \nright choice, but that you have what it takes to lead the busi-\nness to even greater heights.\nIt is a time when you showcase your intentions as CEO \nand set the stage for the bold moves that you will ultimately \nbe remembered for— and will shape your legacy for years to \ncome. A fast and impactful start is critical.\nYet, as I know from my own experience becoming CEO \nof Wolters Kluwer 21 years ago and as a board member \nwitnessing other CEOs in transition, your first year is also \na time when your resilience and adaptability is truly put \nto the test.\nIf you can quickly get to a point of strength, navigating \nyour organization’s complex dynamics and earning the trust \nof your many stakeholders, you will prove your mettle and \nrise above. If you can’t, you may find that you spend the rest \nof your tenure trying to recover.\nWhen I became CEO, my burning platform was clear: \nto reverse Wolter Kluwer’s poor performance and lead the \ncompany forward in the digital age.\xa0 I was excited by the \nchallenge ahead. And having already run Wolter Kluwer’s \nAmerican division for a few years, I was clear on where we \nneeded to go. I knew, however, that rallying the organiza-\ntion would not be so straightforward.\nThere were, of course, skeptics who didn’t believe \nin my vision and doubted that Wolters Kluwer could be \n Downloaded from https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/ by ibrahim ragab - Oregon Health & Science Univer , Wiley Online Library on [01/10/2024]. See the Terms and Conditions (https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/terms-and-conditions) on Wiley Online Library for rules of use; OA articles are governed by the applicable Creative Commons License\n\nx Foreword\ntransformed But I knew two things to be true: First, I had \nbeen given a chance. And second, I didn’t have long to prove \nI could do it.\nOne of my biggest pieces of advice is to surround your -\nself with the right people. Know your strengths and the \nstrengths of those around you. Find people who have skills \nthat you don’t. And bring people together to make sure they \nfunction as a team. Communicate clearly— and often. The \nmantra of “Say it and say it again” is mission-  critical for \nevery CEO in transition.\nAnd always remember that no matter what you think \nbefore becoming CEO, you’ll never be 100% ready. Be hum-\nble enough to acknowledge that and commit to becoming \na life- long learner.\xa0However successful you are, there will \nalways be something new to discover.\nSince I began as CEO, the world has changed signifi-\ncantly. Navigating this complex moment in history will \nrequire transformational CEOs who have the vision and \npassion to make an immediate impact. I cannot think of a \nbetter moment for Russell Reynolds Associates (RRA) to \nshare the inside track on how to get off to a strong start as a \nnew CEO and set up yourself for long- term success.\nTy Wiggins has made it his career to help new CEOs \ndeliver a strong start. He distills his rich experience in this \ntrusted step- by- step guide that will help you tackle the \nissues you face during your transition. Whatever your situ-\nation, his advice works. This is a critical book for all new \nCEOs— and those who work with them— and it could not \nhave come at a more important time.\nA CEO transition is not for the faint of heart. But those \nwho are willing, and able, to embrace both the opportu-\nnities and challenges that lay before them will leave an \n Downloaded from https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/ by ibrahim ragab - Oregon Health & Science Univer , Wiley Online Library on [01/10/2024]. See the Terms and Conditions (https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/terms-and-conditions) on Wiley Online Library for rules of use; OA articles are governed by the applicable Creative Commons License\n\nForeword xi\nindelible mark on their organizations. So, step boldly into \nthis transformative journey. Although it will be demanding, \nthe rewards will be immeasurable— for you, your team, and \nyour business.\nNancy McKinstry, CEO and Chairman of the  \nExecutive Board, Wolters Kluwer\n Downloaded from https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/ by ibrahim ragab - Oregon Health & Science Univer , Wiley Online Library on [01/10/2024]. See the Terms and Conditions (https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/terms-and-conditions) on Wiley Online Library for rules of use; OA articles are governed by the applicable Creative Commons License\n\n1Introduction\nI \nf you are reading this book as a newly announced or \nappointed CEO, congratulations!\nYou are about to embark on something that only a very \nsmall percentage of the population ever gets to experience—  \n to lead an organization and all of the people who call it  \ntheirs.\nPerhaps you’re already in the role. Perhaps you’re get -\nting ready to start. It may be your first time being a CEO. It \nmay be your third. But whatever your circumstances, this is \na time of great celebration.\nThis moment is about recognizing years of hard work \nand ambition (and, I’m sure, a fair bit of sacrifice along the \nway). It’s a time when you are at your most energized to \nexecute your vision of a brighter future for your organiza-\ntion. And, of course, for you and your family, too.\nEnjoy this feeling. You’ve earned it. And the reality is \nthat you’ll need to keep returning to this place when times \nget tough. Commonly, that is a when, not an if.\nMuch of what they say about the CEO role is true: it’s \ncomplex, confusing, and often exhausting. This is the most \nchallenging and loneliest role in business (just how lonely \nis often what takes CEOs by surprise). The scope, gravity, \nresponsibility, accountability, and exposure of the CEO role \nare unparalleled.\nI probably don’t need to remind you that, as CEO, you’re \nnot only responsible for your organization’s strategy, but \nalso for its financial viability, and long-term sustainability. \nYou’re also responsible for the livelihood of its employees \n Downloaded from https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/ by ibrahim ragab - Oregon Health & Science Univer , Wiley Online Library on [01/10/2024]. See the Terms and Conditions (https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/terms-and-conditions) on Wiley Online Library for rules of use; OA articles are governed by the applicable Creative Commons License\n\n2 Introduction\n(and their families), and in many cases, your suppliers and \npartners (and their families, too).\nYou have no doubt read that CEO turnover is at an all-\ntime high. Our research shows CEOs across the S&P\xa0500 and \nFTSE 350\xa0lasted an average of 7.6 years in their role in 2022, \ncompared to 9.2 years in 2018.1 And you’re no doubt famil-\niar with the numerous stories of CEOs who didn’t make it \npast the two-year mark at organizations like HSBC, Procter \n& Gamble, Apple, Boeing, and Hewlett-Packard.\nIf CEOs fail, especially early in their tenure, the conse -\nquences can be vast. When Fiona Hick, the CEO of Fortescue \nMetals, one of Australia’s largest companies, stepped down \nafter six months in the role, it immediately wiped off 4% of \nthe share price (approximately $1.5 billion at the time).2\nThe tremendous amount of pressure and enormous \nresponsibility is why so many people don’t seek the role, \nand are happy to stay as a key contributor to someone else’s \nstrategic vision— and that’s fine. We can’t all be in charge.\nIf the responsibility of the role scares you, that’s normal. \nIf it didn’t, I would be concerned.\nThe trick is to now walk the tightrope over the complex \nweb of feelings and experiences you’ll inevitably face as a \nnew CEO, nimbly stepping between decisiveness, confi-\ndence, self-awareness, and empathy to execute your vision \nand unlock the most life-affirming move of your career.\nWhy I\xa0Wrote This Book\nAs cliché as it will sound\xa0.\xa0.\xa0.\xa0because people told me I should.\nI have one of the best jobs. As a leadership advisor and \nglobal lead for the CEO & Executive Transition practice \nat Russell Reynolds Associates (RRA), my job is to guide \n Downloaded from https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/ by ibrahim ragab - Oregon Health & Science Univer , Wiley Online Library on [01/10/2024]. See the Terms and Conditions (https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/terms-and-conditions) on Wiley Online Library for rules of use; OA articles are governed by the applicable Creative Commons License\n\nWhy I\xa0Wrote This Book 3\nCEOs the world over through their first 12–18\xa0months in \ntheir new role. Sometimes I’m their coach; sometimes, their \ncheerleader. Often, I’m their venting partner. More often, I \nam their challenger and sounding board. I am always their \ntrusted advisor.\nThe work I do— and the stories I’m privy to— is a true \nprivilege. Having the opportunity to gain a backstage pass \nto so many CEO transitions, talking with leaders who are at \nthe top of their game at a time when they’re at their most \nvulnerable, has given me a unique vantage point into the \ngood, bad, and ugly of CEO transitions. That’s what I aim to \nshare in this book.\nWhy? Because for all the attention given to CEOs, there \nis very little advice on how to actually land well in the early \ndays and set yourself up for success. Walk past any airport \nbookstore and you’ll find countless books about what it \nmeans to be a leader or a CEO, or how to nail your first \n90\xa0days as a general executive. What you won’t find is a book \nthat specifically covers the challenge you face now: the CEO \ntransition (a period that, for the purpose of the book, covers \nyour first 12–18\xa0months in the role).\nSuccessfully navigating your first weeks and months as \nCEO sets the tone for how you’ll be perceived for years to \ncome. Yet, until now, few CEOs have shared their stories on \nwhat worked, what didn’t, and what they wish they’d done \ndifferently.\nFor years, I’d entertained the idea of writing a book to \ncompile the rich experiences I’d gleaned advising CEOs in \ntransition. It wasn’t until one of my CEO clients stressed the \npoint so emphatically that I was kick-started into action.\nWe’d been working together for a little over 14\xa0months, \nnavigating everything from an organizational restructure to \na significant business sale. It had turned out to be a very \n Downloaded from https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/ by ibrahim ragab - Oregon Health & Science Univer , Wiley Online Library on [01/10/2024]. See the Terms and Conditions (https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/terms-and-conditions) on Wiley Online Library for rules of use; OA articles are governed by the applicable Creative Commons License\n\n4 Introduction\nchallenging first year as CEO, and through the process, we’d \ndeveloped a relationship that was honest, trusting, and at \ntimes, frank.\nAs we finished up our last session together, he said, “The \nway you have supported and challenged my thinking, added \ninsights and examples at just the right time, and on more \nthan a couple of occasions, told me I was flat out wrong has \nbeen invaluable. You should share all of this in a book.”\nI’d heard this a few times already over the years and \ntold him so.\nHe waited all of 0.05 seconds and said in slightly richer \nlanguage: “So, get off your backside and do it.”\nSo, I did.\nWhat You’ll Find in\xa0This Book\nThis book is a combination of CEO experiences, observa-\ntions, and research. It is written to give you a wide and dis -\nparate view of the transition from the perspectives of the \nCEOs who have done it before you and from my perspective \nas an advisor, having worked intimately with 50-plus CEOs \nacross the world to ensure their success in real time.\nOver the following pages, you will find the rich stories—  \nwarts and all—  of global CEOs who have successfully man-\naged their transitions— and who have graciously agreed to \nbe featured in this book. CEOs like Whirlpool’s Marc Bitzer, \nwho shared the memorable piece of advice he’d received early \non that “you’ll have 10 really bad days as CEO—you just \ndon’t know when they happen.” Or Carol Tomé, the CEO of \nUPS, who said as CEO you stand at the crossroads of multiple \n Downloaded from https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/ by ibrahim ragab - Oregon Health & Science Univer , Wiley Online Library on [01/10/2024]. See the Terms and Conditions (https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/terms-and-conditions) on Wiley Online Library for rules of use; OA articles are governed by the applicable Creative Commons License\n\nWhat You’ll Find in\xa0This Book 5\ncontradictions. “You rarely get a clean shot at things. More \noften, you face a paradox, or a series of decisions and judg -\nment calls where you are picking the ‘least worst option.’” Or \nAndré Lacroix, CEO of Intertek, who told me that “there are \nvery few leaders who can really improvise on the spot effec-\ntively, so the rest of us have to be very well prepared.”\nTo supplement this experience, and solve for any con-\nfirmation bias I may have, I also completed detailed inter -\nviews with 35 CEOs across public, private, and private \nequity (PE) organizations in 2023. A concerted effort was \nmade to ensure these interviews were a mix of internal and \nexternal appointments, from a range of industry sectors \n(13), and with backgrounds across seven functional disci-\nplines. A third of the CEOs I spoke with were women. All \nCEOs were interviewed between 12 and 18\xa0months in the \nrole, so they had both the benefit of currency and hind-\nsight. Interviewing them at such a critical juncture in their \nrespective CEO journeys forced them to truly reflect on \ntheir approach, actions, and decisions during their transi-\ntions, and most importantly, answer off the cuff. These are \nsome of the unnamed stories you will read in the book. We \nalso surveyed almost 200 CEOs globally about their experi-\nences transitioning into the role.\nWhen I think about how to summarize a CEO transition, \nthe mental picture that often comes to mind is a parachutist \nflying through a tight canyon. Parachuting is not like driv -\ning a car. When you toggle left and right in a parachute, it \ndoesn’t create an instant or neat reaction. It creates a pen-\ndulum effect— and you need to work hard to keep adjusting \nto find the right momentum. This, to me, feels like the most \napt description of what it means to be a CEO in transition. \n Downloaded from https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/ by ibrahim ragab - Oregon Health & Science Univer , Wiley Online Library on [01/10/2024]. Instead, what I do with clients (and what I aim to replicate in this book) is work through the key expectations, decisions, challenges, risks, and opportunities that commonly arise.\nI have distilled this into 10 components that, in my expe-\nrience, make for a successful CEO transition (see Figure\xa0I.1).\nAPPROACHPRIORITIESAssess the\nState  of \nPlay\nEngage  \nthe Board\nSet Your Team (fast)\nUnderstand the CulturePrepare to  \nWinGet the\nMessaging\nRight\nAct \nDiscerningly\nManage\nYourself\nBurst Your\nBubbl e\nNavigate the\nCrashCEO \nTRANSITION \nSUCCESS\nFIGURE I.1  CEO transition success, Russell Reynolds Associates, 2024.\nWiggins244348_cintro.indd   6 19-03-2024   20:43:07\n Downloaded from https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/ by ibrahim ragab - Oregon Health & Science Univer , Wiley Online Library on [01/10/2024]. My recommendation is to read the book from start to \nfinish to get a feel for the learnings in their entirety, and then \ndip in and out to refresh on certain sections as challenges \narise during your transition. The book has been structured \nto allow for this to happen.\nChapter\xa01 looks at why the complexity and scope of the \nCEO role can make your transition feel like an Everest-scale \nchallenge. In that chapter, you learn how you will feel out of \nyour comfort zone at the exact moment you need to be per -\nforming at your best and why, regardless of what you believe \ngoing in, you will feel lonelier than you’ve ever been. You’ll \nalso read stories that speak to the weight of the responsibil-\nity you now face as CEO and how the demands of the role \nare always more than you expect. As PepsiCo’s CEO Ramon \nLaguarta told me, “When you’re running a business unit \nor region, you can always ask your boss to carry the ball \nthe last mile. But now you are the one who has to make the \nfinal call.”\nEngaging in robust transition planning will set you up for \nsuccess, but no two transitions are exactly the same. So, what \nshould your plan cover? In Chapter\xa02, you’ll find how differ -\nent factors can influence your early approach and success, \nincluding the influence of your predecessor (or ghost), the \npros and cons of being an internal successor versus an exter -\nnal hire, and the state the business is in when you arrive. You \nalso hear from Sanjiv Lamba, CEO of Linde plc, who had to \nthrow away his meticulously crafted 100-day plan just days \ninto the role, on why flexibility and agility is critical.\n Downloaded from https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/ by ibrahim ragab - Oregon Health & Science Univer , Wiley Online Library on [01/10/2024]. See the Terms and Conditions (https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/terms-and-conditions) on Wiley Online Library for rules of use; OA articles are governed by the applicable Creative Commons License\n\n8 Introduction\nAs a new CEO, you will never have complete visibility \nover what’s happening— nor will everyone always tell you \nthe truth. You’ll need to work quickly to plug the gaps you \nhave in context and detail. Chapter\xa03 looks at how to burst \nthe CEO Bubble that you will find yourself in, including the \nbenefit of being curious over being right, watching out for \nyour biases, and some more obvious but overlooked aspects \nof your ability and willingness to really listen.\nNext, the book takes a look at how to strike the right bal-\nance between patience and action. This is something that \nmany new CEOs struggle with. You will face considerable \npressure to move fast. While there is no formulaic answer \nto how quickly you should move, there are some aspects to \nstrongly consider. I raise these in Chapter\xa04, which looks at \nwhy it can help to find early wins or common pain points \nto address (or as Stephanie Tully at Jetstar Airways called \nthem, the symbolic acts). And why you need to be deliberate \nabout your bigger moves.\nWhat you say and how you say it sets the tone for much \nof your transition. Take time to get your messaging right. As \nDoug Mack, former CEO of Fanatics, said: “The CEO title has \nan unbelievable ability to move teams and organizations— \n it has to be taken with great humility and care.” Many have \nreferred to CEOs as being, among so many other things, the \nChief Communications Officer. This is true in part. What \nyou say during your transition and how you say it will either \nbe an accelerator of your success or the complete opposite—\nas discussed in Chapter 5.\nSuch is the pressure of the role and the challenge of the \ntransition that it is common for new CEOs to experience \na crash during their first 12\xa0months. They face a knock in \nconfidence or a series of challenges that make them think \n Downloaded from https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/ by ibrahim ragab - Oregon Health & Science Univer , Wiley Online Library on [01/10/2024]. See the Terms and Conditions (https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/terms-and-conditions) on Wiley Online Library for rules of use; OA articles are governed by the applicable Creative Commons License\n\nWhat You’ll Find in\xa0This Book 9\nhard about what they have gotten into. In Chapter\xa06, Alan \nBeacham, CEO of Toll Group, shares ways you can reach \nout for support to find your way to the other side. As the \nnew CEO for your organization, the sheer number of tasks \ncan be daunting. Your to-do list likely includes hundreds \nof items. So, here I also help you work out what to do first, \nsecond, third, tenth, and eighty-third.\nThe top regret I hear from new CEOs is that they wish they \nhad moved faster on their teams. Chapters\xa07 and\xa08 explore \nhow to do this as quickly (and safely) as possible. In these \nchapters, you discover how you can get a good read on your \nSenior Leadership Team (SLT)—  or executive committee—  \nthrough both gut instinct, as well as assessment data; why \nyou will need “wide T-shaped leaders”; and why establish-\ning a “first-team” mentality is so important. I also discuss \nthe potential challenges in managing people with different \nthinking styles and the implications of consensus leadership.\nChapter\xa09 covers how to engage the board and investors \nin detail. The headline message is that you’ll need to engage \nboth the board and your investors early and often in your \ntransition. A key learning is that this will take up much more \ntime than you probably expect. In fact, the second-most \ncommon regret from CEOs, behind wishing they had moved \nfaster on their teams, was wishing they had better engaged \ntheir board earlier. How you approach and work with the \nboard is one of the markers of your success in transition and \nin the longer term. I discuss ways to do this, the common \npitfalls to watch out for, and the areas where boards worry \nabout you— their new CEO.\nCultural change is hard and takes a long time. Every cul-\nture has a positive side and a dark side. In your transition, \nyou need to take the time to really understand whether the \n Downloaded from https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/ by ibrahim ragab - Oregon Health & Science Univer , Wiley Online Library on [01/10/2024].
